The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of the science of medicine, and having reached the peak of clarity and explanation in it, saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of practical application.
Because practical application, by hand, is completely absent in our country and time, so much so that its study almost ceased, and its effect vanished. Only a few traces remain of it in the books of the ancients, which have been corrupted by hands and affected by errors and confusion, until its meanings were obscured and its benefit distanced.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this chapter upon the path of explanation, clarification, and summarization, and to provide illustrations of cauterizing irons and other tools of the craft, for this is a means of increased clarity and a compilation of what is necessary.
The reason there is no skilled practitioner with adept hands in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first undergo training in anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he may understand the uses of the organs, their arrangements, temperaments, connections, and separations, and the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their number and exits, arteries, veins, and their exit points.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of the hand. We have mentioned a glimpse of this in the introduction of this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is liable to make a mistake that kills people, as I have witnessed many who presume knowledge and claim it without understanding or study.
For I saw an ignorant physician who incised a swine-like tumor on a woman's neck, and severed some of the arteries of the neck, so the woman bled until she fell dead at his hands.
And I saw another physician, in extracting a stone from an elderly man, the stone being large, acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder's tissue. The man died about three days later.  I was called to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the patient's condition that I could not have done so.
And I saw another physician who made his living by practicing medicine among some of the leaders of our city.
A dark-complexioned boy had a fracture in his leg near the heel, along with a wound. The physician, in his ignorance, quickly tightened the fracture against the wound with bandages and splints, binding it tightly and not leaving the wound any breathing space.
Then he indulged his desires, and left him for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandage until his leg and foot swelled, and he was close to death.
So I was called to him, and I quickly loosened the bandage. He found relief and his pains lessened, but the damage had already taken hold of the limb, and I could not stop it.
The damage continued to spread in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a cancerous tumor. After a few days, it ulcerated, and the patient's suffering worsened. This is because if the cancer is purely of a dark, black humor, it should never be treated with iron.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety and work that is more often associated with injury.
And I have warned you throughout this book of all work that involves risk and fear, and you should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to speak ill and criticize, take for yourselves firmness and caution, and in your treatment, gentleness and certainty.
Use the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure illnesses.
Keep yourselves free from anything that you fear might bring doubt upon your religion and your worldly life, for it is more lasting for your honor and higher in this world and the hereafter for your standing.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad illness, and so call yourselves bad physicians.
This article is divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization with fire and cauterization with a sharp medicine, arranged from the heel to the foot, images of the instruments, cautery irons, and everything needed for manual work.
And the second chapter.
On incision, lancing, phlebotomy, cupping, surgeries, extraction of arrows, and similar matters, arranged and illustrated with images of the instruments.
And the third chapter.
On fractures, dislocations, treatment of sprains, and similar matters, arranged from the heel to the foot, and illustrated with images of the instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
Before mentioning the procedure, it is necessary to describe the benefits, harms, and temperament in which it is used.
I say that discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization is lengthy, a precise science, and a hidden secret.  Many wise men have spoken on this topic.
Their opinions differed, and I have summarized their few words, fearing prolixity.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for every bad temperament, with or without matter, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without matter and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, opinions differ. Some say that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others say the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for an illness arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry illness with a hot, dry remedy.  The one who says the opposite claims that cauterization with fire can benefit from a hot, dry illness that arises in people's bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say in agreement with him, because experience has revealed this to me repeatedly. However, one should not consider this matter except for those who have diligently and intensely studied the art of cauterization. And he who has understood the differences in people's temperaments, and the nature, causes, symptoms, and duration of illnesses.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear of them, and especially cold, moist illnesses. All physicians agree on them, and there is no disagreement on the benefit of cauterization in them.
Know, my children, that the secret of treatment with cauterization by fire is superior to cauterization with a burning medicine.  This is because fire is a singular essence whose action is limited to the affected organ; it does not harm another connected organ, except to a slight degree.
Cauterization with burning medicine can extend its effect to other organs, and may possibly induce a difficult-to-treat illness or even death in the affected organ.  Fire, because of its noble and esteemed nature, does not do this unless it is excessive.
This has become clear to us through experience, due to the length of service, care in the practice, and the understanding of the truth of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse, although, were it not inappropriate for this book, I would have presented to you a profound secret about fire, its action on bodies, and its removal of diseases, using a precise, philosophical, and demonstrative argument that would be too subtle for your comprehension.
Know, my children, that they have differed in opinion concerning the time suitable for cauterization, and have considered spring to be the best time.
And I say that cauterization may be appropriate at any time, for the harm caused by the time is absorbed into the benefit obtained through cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, necessary and severe ailments that cannot tolerate delay, lest a greater calamity should follow than the slight harm caused by the time.
Do not think, my children, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a disease will never return and you treat it as a blemish.
And the matter is not as you think, for cauterization is like a medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humidity which is the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is preferred to medicine because of its swift success, strong effect, and intense power.
The illness may return after a time, depending on the patient's temperament, the severity and strength of the disease, and the confluence of factors in the body, as well as their negligence in acquiring proper foods, and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one, and in a part of the body with little excess moisture and humours. For example, cauterizing a tooth for pain, and similar cases, the pain may not return, which is the least that can be hoped for.
Also, the common saying that cauterization is the final stage of medicine is correct, but not for the reasons they believe.  For they think there is no cure, neither by medicine nor otherwise, after cauterization; but it is different.
The meaning of "cauterization is the final stage of medicine" is that, when various treatments are used for an illness and those medicines prove ineffective, and then cauterization is used as the final recourse and it is successful, this is why cauterization is considered the final stage of medicine. Not in the sense understood by the common people and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, because of the gold's moderation and the nobility of its substance. They said that the cauterized area does not fester, but this is not universally true.
Because I have tested this and found that it works in some bodies but not in others; cauterization with gold is better and superior to that with iron, as they said. However, if you heat a gold cautery in the fire, you will not be able to determine when it has reached the desired heat for the redness of the gold.
Because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and is fused, and the craftsman is occupied with this.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is, in our opinion, faster and closer to correct practice, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections, organized from the head to the foot, so that the student will easily find what they need, God willing.
The first chapter.
Cauterization of the head: one cauterization.
This cauterization is effective against the excess moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and sore throats. In general, for every disease arising from coldness, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and stupor, and similar ailments.
The method of this cauterization is first to order the patient to induce vomiting with a purgative medicine suited for the head for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and habits.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit before you, squatting, with his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-oil cautery, which is of this form.
If the bone is exposed to the extent of a bodkin's head or a carbuncle's size, raise your hand. Otherwise, reheat the same iron or another one if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as described, then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it. Place it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in clarified butter and leave it there until the burning sensation subsides.  Then treat it with the four-component ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it produces, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have said that the skin should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some of the bone's thickness is burned.  Then the burned portion of the bone should be scraped away, and then treatment should be applied.
Others said that the cauterization should be intense, affecting the bone strongly, until it falls off like a small, flat piece of rock or a small disc. They claimed that it releases vapors from the head region through that area and the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals. I do not see either of these types of cauterization at all, except in some individuals and by way of chance.  I prefer to leave it alone, and with best wishes. For the head weakens when its natural connection is disrupted, as we have seen in other limbs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and with it you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
On cauterizing the head as well.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head, and this has persisted in the patient, and if applying compresses, poultices, rubbing, ointments, and bandages have been used, especially if a single cauterization as we described has already been performed, and if none of these remedies have benefited, then consider... If the patient's head is strong constitutionally and not weak, and if they experience severe cold, cauterize again a little above the previous one. Then cauterize on each horn of their head a cauterization until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is exposed to the extent we described. And cauterize a cauterization at the back of his head in the place known as the "axe" (or "battle-axe"), and be gentle in this one, and do not expose the bone; for the patient will experience intense pain, unlike the pain of the other cauterizations of the head; and I will mention this cauterization in its proper place.
The cauterizing instrument used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than that used for the middle of the head; this is its form.
Chapter Three.
In the cauterization of the chronic sister (or, the chronic cheek).
If pain occurs in the opening of the head, accompanied by headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient be purged with purifying medicines for the head, and employ all the other treatment that I have mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If this does not work, then there are two ways of cauterizing in this case. Either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends on both sides. Then, split the painful area of the temple with a wide scalpel until a space is created under the skin large enough to accommodate the clove.
Insert it under the skin until it disappears, then apply firm pressure with bandages. Leave it in place for about fifteen hours, then remove it and take out the garlic. Leave the wound for two or three days, then apply a dressing.
Place a cotton swab dipped in clarified butter on it until the area becomes suppurated. Then, treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  If you wish, you can use some of the burning medications that I have given in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterizing with iron, it is done in this way: Heat the cautery iron, which has this shape:
It is called the nail-shaped cautery because its head resembles a nail, having some grooves and a small protrusion in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while slowly rotating the iron. The amount burned through the skin's thickness should be about half. And lift your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath, causing bleeding, then soak a cotton swab in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then bandage the cotton with oil, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this vein with the sharp, pointed end of the cautery, preserving the vein from cutting the artery in this particular, non-chronic vein.
Chapter Four.
Treating chronic veins.
If you have treated the vein using the previously mentioned treatments and the treatments mentioned in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment proves ineffective, and you see a cause that the aforementioned cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire cannot address, Then you should heat the sharp cautery until it whitens, having previously marked the site of pain with a line of half a finger's length or so, and let your hand rest once while tightening it until the artery is cut and you reach the bone.
However, you must be careful not to touch the jaw that moves during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, causing spasms.
Be cautious and vigilant about the bleeding of the artery you have cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who does not know what to do and has not been a proven practitioner; abandonment of the procedure is preferable.
Treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery will be discussed in its appropriate place in the book, God willing, if you see a condition in the affliction for which this cauterization is insufficient.
If you see that the patient's body can endure it, cauterize a spot in the middle of the head as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, use the cauterization mentioned in the chapter on ligating an artery with a double-bladed cautery; it is a superior and more effective cauterization than this one.
Fifth Chapter.
On cauterization for earaches.
If an earache due to cold occurs and is treated with laxatives and other treatments mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, cauterize the ear with the cautery called the "point," whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply drops of it around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in both. Cauterize slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the location with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten dots in each ear, or thereabouts.  Then treat the affected areas until healing occurs, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing with a cautery.
The obstructions treated by cauterization are those resulting from phlegm, as I mentioned in the divisions of illnesses.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve constriction.
If, having treated this type of obstruction with irrigations, emetics, and gargles, your treatment proves ineffective, then the afflicted person should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, for relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be performed in the image of a cauterization corresponding to the upper part of the ear, a little below the crown of the head, and another in the temple, with a length equal to the length of the thumb.
Apply the cauterization until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cauterizing instrument, which is a type of scalpel, a little gentler than the previous one, as you see. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, and then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization of chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you have treated it as we mentioned but your treatment is not effective, and there is no fever in the patient, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one in the center of the head as we mentioned, and one at the back of the head, as previously stated.
The cauterization method, as previously described, may also involve a cauterization on the mouth of the stomach, which can be more effective, then treated as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On Cauterization for Forgetfulness.
One who has forgetfulness arising from phlegm should first be given large doses of purgatives and brain-cleansing herbs.
Then, shave his entire head and apply a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, several times; it is a form of cauterization.
And do this in the same manner as previously mentioned. If he is cured by this, then do not proceed further. Otherwise, apply three cauterizations to the back of his head, arranged in a row from the top of the head down to the base of the neck.
Place the width of a finger between each cauterization, then treat the cauterizations as previously described. If you wish to increase the treatment and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize the temples, then treat him until he is healed. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and general relaxation of the body.
It is necessary to first purify the head with the methods mentioned previously. Then, shave the patient's head, and cauterize one cauterization in the middle of the head, one cauterization on each temple, and one cauterization at the back of the head.
And three on the cervical vertebrae. If the ailment of relaxation of the body requires more than that, and the patient can tolerate it, and the illness is strong and entrenched, cauterize four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize deeply enough to burn most of the skin, then remove your hand, and treat him as mentioned previously, until he is healed, God willing.  Use an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be cleansed with large syringes and with all the other treatment we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is large and can tolerate taking medications.
However, if he is a child who cannot tolerate medications, then use gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain many days beforehand, while also improving his diet.
Then shave his head and apply one cauterization in the middle of the head, as described previously, and another in the back of his head. And on each temple, a cauterization.  If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize as described for paralysis and muscular relaxation, on the cervical and lumbar vertebrae, using an olive-shaped cautery as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
In cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupted moisture and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauterizations that we have described for paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile, and the patient's body is moist, purge what purifies his brain as described in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of flax like a circle, and place it on the center of his head. The patient sits upright and holds onto it from all sides. Then take one pound of aged sheep's butter.
Then heat it over the fire to a moderate heat, so much that a finger can tolerate it when placed in it. Then pour it into the circle on the center of his head and leave it until it cools. Do this to the patient once a week along with the rest of his good treatment until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize him with many small, scattered points, without holding the cautery in your hand, but with a gentle touch.  This type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton pad soaked in butter or chicken fat.
The Twelfth Chapter.
Cauterizing the watery discharge from the eye.
If you notice watery discharge from the eye, with the signs mentioned in the classification, immediately dry the patient's head and protect it from all moisture.  Have him sweat in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize the center of the head.  Cauterize the temples twice if the watery discharge is from both eyes or from one side only if it is from one eye.
Then, using the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin, ensuring the cauterizations extend lengthwise and across the temples.  Prevent bleeding.
If you see anything remaining, cut it at the site with whatever treatment is possible.  We will discuss in detail the wisdom of cutting the arteries and preventing bleeding.
He may also be cauterized twice intensely at the base of the skull, below the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and continuous, and originate from veins and arteries on the exterior of the head, and you are certain that it is due to a thick, cold, phlegmatic excess, cauterize it with the same cautery I described at the beginning of the descending water. A cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries on the temples, and two cauteries at the back of the head below the bones.  If more is needed, cauterize on each side of the eye's eyelid, at the edge of the eyebrow, using a small cautery.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing nasal foulness.
If treatment with what we mentioned in the division is ineffective, then quickly apply cupping to the patient's head for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle portion with an olive oil cautery. Then cauterize with a nail-shaped cautery two points above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, being careful to avoid the artery, do not cut it. This is an image of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, spaced apart from the temples, and the length of each cauterization should be along the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization with your hand, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the cautery should be of this shape.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inward and injure the eye, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire, or cauterization with a burning medicine.  As for cauterizing it with fire, you instruct the patient beforehand to allow their eyelashes to grow long and even, if they are the sort who pluck them.
If it injured the eye while the eyelashes were growing, then bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so they do not move until they grow.  When they have grown and become even, place the patient's head on your lap, and mark the eyelid with ink, in the shape of a small leaf.
The mark should begin near the eyelashes. Then, place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or the juice of a prickly plant under the eyelid.  Then, heat the cauterizing iron – this is its image.
Then, cauterize in the shape you marked, little by little, in many applications, until the surface of the skin, which should all be visible in the shape of the small leaf, is burned. A sign of successful work is that you see the eyelid has shrunk and the hairs have risen from the surface of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days.  Then apply a cotton swab with oil until the scab comes off. Then treat with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair returns after a while and the eyelid loosens,
Then repeat the cauterization on that spot as you did at first.  If the hair is on the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not injure the eye. As for cauterization with the burning medicine, instruct the patient to allow the eyelashes to grow long and even.
Then make a shape of a small leaf from paper.  Then, take from the familiar soap and quicklime, one dirham or similar in weight of each, and thoroughly pulverize them both. Do this quickly so they do not dry out. Then spread some of this mixture onto the paper, shaped like the small leaf, and place it on the eyelid, either one or both.  Place a cotton swab soaked in egg white under the eye, and hold the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on top of the medicine and gently press it while moving it whenever the patient feels a burning sensation from the medicine, because it feels like a burning sensation. As long as the burning sensation is present, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And look; if you see the eyelid has risen as it does when cauterized with fire or cut, then do not do anything further. If not, reapply the medicine to the areas where it has not taken effect and has not blackened, until your task is complete and the eye is cauterized.  Then apply cotton soaked in oil, so as to relieve the burning sensation.
Then treat it with the date palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You must be extremely careful during treatment, so that no medicine gets into the eye.  If the eyelid relaxes after a few days, and you are worried about something happening to the eye, [take precautions].
Then reapply the medicine to the relaxed part of the eyelid, specifically, as you did before, and treat it until it heals.  This is the shape of the small leaf (or, as is sometimes translated, the yarrow leaf).
Know that people's eyes vary in size and age; therefore, adjust your cauterization accordingly.  The correct approach is not hidden from one who has practiced this art.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the nasal discharge appearing in the eye's conjunctiva.
If you have treated the nasal discharge with the methods described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment has not been successful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Instruct the patient to place his head on your lap, and have an assistant hold the head firmly, preventing any movement or shaking.
Then place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the juice of a prickly pear on his eyes, and then heat the cautery iron—which is illustrated in this figure.
It should be hollowed out, like the shaft of a hawk's feather, on the one end where the cautery is applied. If you wish, the other end can be pierced, or if you prefer, it can be solid like a rod. However, the hollowed-out type is better for your work, God willing.
Then squeeze the nasal discharge if it is open, and remove the matter and dry it; or if it is not open, then open it and remove the pus.  Then, apply the very hot cautery iron to it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand a little further away from the eye towards the nose when applying the cautery, so that your hand doesn't miss the mark or the patient becomes agitated, causing the cautery to fall on the eye's eyelid and damage it.  If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then do nothing more. Otherwise, reheat the cautery a second time if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton swab with ghee and treat it with the dried ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, then apply the strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it as described in its relevant chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the nasal discharge, mentioned by some earlier authorities, is to carefully cut open the area of the nasal discharge and then insert a thin funnel into the same incision.  This is its form.
Pour in lead, melted to the weight of a dirham, and hold the funnel firmly in your hand, ensuring the patient does not move at all, so that the molten lead does not drip into their eye.  It is necessary to place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the nasal discharge and heal it remarkably. If the nasal discharge heals with the cauterization and treatment described, then no further action is necessary.  Otherwise, a nasal perforation must be performed and the discharge returned to its nasal passage, as will be detailed in its specific section.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, known as a fissure, often occurs, especially in the lips of young boys.  If the cleft, treated as described in the preceding section, does not heal, then apply a small, knife-like cautery iron in this manner.
Its [the cautery iron's] interior will be as thin as a knife blade.  Then, holding it hot, quickly place it into the cleft until the cautery reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with qirūṭī until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a nasal fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, then ulcerates and bursts, forming a fistula from the flow of pus, and treatment is unsuccessful, then a cautery iron should be heated to a size that fits the fistula.
Then, introduce the hot iron into the opening of the fistula, and hold it until the heated iron reaches its deepest part, its end.  Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals, God willing.
If the matter is stopped and healed, well and good; otherwise, the area must be examined, and the corrupted bone removed, as will be described in the relevant chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Relaxed teeth and gums.
If the gums have relaxed due to moisture, and the teeth have shifted, and medication has been ineffective, then place the patient's head on your lap, and heat the cautery iron, whose image will appear later, after placing the tube on the tooth.
Quickly insert the hot cautery iron and hold it for a short while, until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth; then, remove your hand.  Then, repeat the process with the cautery iron as many times as desired.  Afterwards, have the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out.  The loosened teeth will become firm, the relaxed gums will tighten, and the putrid moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If toothache is caused by cold or worms, and treatment with medicine is ineffective, cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
As for cauterization with oil, take cow's oil and boil it in an iron ladle or a shell. Then, take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of the rod, dip it in the boiled oil, and place it on the aching tooth quickly. Hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  Or, if desired, dip wool or cotton in cold oil, place it on the aching tooth, and place the heated iron on top until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, use a brass or iron tube, with some thickness to prevent the heat of the fire from reaching the patient's mouth. Then, heat the cauterizing instrument, place it on the tooth, and hold it until it cools. Repeat this several times.
The pain will subside either that same day or the next.  After the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good oil, hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the shape of the cauterizing instrument.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer, and in the way that is possible.  This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing pigs.
If pigs are suffering from phlegm and cold moisture, and do not respond to ripening with medicines, and you desire rapid ripening, heat the hollow cauterizing instrument, whose image is shown with both ends pierced.
To allow smoke to escape from the opposite end during cauterization, place it on the same tumor, once, and again if necessary, until you reach the tumor's depth.  If the tumor is small, make the cauterizing instrument the same size as the tumor.
Then leave it for three days, and apply a cotton ball dipped in clarified butter to remove what was burnt by the fire.  Then treat it with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-three.
In cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If phlegm predominates in the trachea, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxatives.  Then, a cauterization should be performed on the nape of the neck, at the base of the throat, in the lowest part of the area.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cauterization, and burn only half of the skin. Then cauterize again at the joint of the neck, at the last bead, strongly.  Use a nail-shaped cauterization iron, in the manner previously described.
Then treat him as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and coughing.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moisture, and the patient does not have a fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, empty, low areas, and cauterize once in the center of the chest between the breasts.
The cauterizing instrument should be a pointed one, as described previously.  If you prefer, the cauterization may be by dripping using a cautery known as the "point," the shape of which was previously described in the treatment of ear pain.  The number of drops should be from thirty to approximately that number.
Then treat him with what was previously mentioned until he recovers.  A cautery with three prongs may also be used, in this manner, and hastens the cauterization, since with it you cauterize three areas simultaneously.  This is the design of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moisture or if it does not remain fixed when the dislocation is reduced so that it then dislocates again at the slightest movement, as we have observed, then the joint must first be reduced. Then the patient lies on his back or on his right side, and the skin inside the armpit is lifted upwards with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint dislocated inwards.
Then the double-pronged cautery, which is depicted in this image, is heated.
Then it is used to cauterize the skin until it penetrates to the other side.  The resulting cauterization pattern is four cauterizations; it may also be cauterized with a three-pronged cautery, in which case the pattern will be six cauterizations.  The prongs are placed along the direction of the affected area.
This is an image of the three-pronged cautery.
And one more may be added to this number, making eight cauterizations. Then, pounded balls mixed with salt are applied to the cauterization. The patient must be kept at rest, and not move the affected limb for a time until it has strengthened.  If the dislocation is upward, it is rare.
Cauterize one good, nail-like cauterization or many small cauterizations above the shoulder blade. Then the joint will strengthen, the moisture will go away, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If cold and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, disturbing its temperament, and frequent afflictions befall it, and if various treatments have failed, then the patient should lie on their back, with legs and arms outstretched, and three cauterizations should be applied. Apply one cauterization beneath the breastbone, the size of a fingertip, using a nail-shaped cautery iron, and two further cauterizations below and slightly off to either side of the first cauterization, so that the cauterizations form a triangle, maintaining some space between them to prevent them from joining if they become suppurated.  The depth of the cauterizations should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and the shape of the cauterizations should be as described.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the center of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery iron used for this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
The stomach can also be cauterized by puncturing.  For those who fear this cauterization, mark dots on the stomach to the desired extent with ink, then cauterize them with a pointed cautery iron, and then treat it with the previously mentioned remedies until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.
If the liver exhibits pain due to cold and dampness, or due to excessive and unnatural heat and the patient was treated as described in the classification, but this did not help. The patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries, of this exact shape and size, should be marked with ink on the liver, below the collarbones, where the human forearm ends.
Each cautery should be the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be performed straight, the length of the body. Do not press too hard with the cautery iron.
The burn should be no more than half the thickness of the skin, and no more. The patient should stand if possible, or lie down with legs extended and arms raised. This is the shape of the cautery iron.
You may use a knife-shaped cautery iron for these cauterizations if you are skilled and careful, taking care not to over-cauterize and burn all the skin, burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines, as the skin there is thin. Be aware of this.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
For cauterization of a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know whether the tumor is in the liver tissue or its covering, if it is in the liver tissue, the patient will feel a heaviness and pain, but not intense.
But if it is in the liver's covering, the pain will be accompanied by intense sharpness.  If the physicians have exhausted their efforts in treating it, the patient should lie on their back, and then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire, the cautery that resembles a spatula, and this is its form. Cauterize it with a single cauterization, until the entire skin is burned, and the cauterization extends to the covering, until all the matter comes out.  Then treat it as an abscess until it heals.
This type of cauterization should not be used except by those whose experience in medicine is extensive and who have treated these diseases repeatedly through experimentation.  Only then should they undertake such a procedure.  To leave it to me is better.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing cold fistulas with the roots of the zera'und in this manner: take one of the longest, driest roots of the zera'und, as thick as a finger.  Then dip it in oil and hold it over the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly inclined towards the area under the chin, avoiding the veins.  Two large cauterizations should be placed above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly tilted backwards. Another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach. Three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders, and two on the sides of the loin, below the cauterization that is between the collarbones.
One should not penetrate deeply with the cautery, but rather apply it superficially to the skin, causing a slight burning.  Some of the ancients have written that some people used a heated iron instrument shaped like a spearhead, protecting it and inserting it between the ribs until it reaches the tumor itself.
The pus will drain as we described for liver tumors, and in this case, cauterizing the abscess carries a risk.  Either the patient will die immediately, or a non-healing fistula will develop at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
In cauterizing the spleen.
If you have treated spleen disease as we have described in the classification and your treatment proves ineffective, cauterization can be done in three ways, all correct: one is to cauterize three or four times, arranged along the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations previously described, with the space between each cauterization equal to the thickness of a finger or slightly more.  Use the same cautery as described for liver cauterization. Do not insert your hand deeply into the cauterization, and the patient should be lying on their back.
The other method is at the elbow; raise the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends. Raise the skin across the width of the body so the cauterizations are lengthwise along the body.
Then, insert the cauterizing instruments, very carefully, until they pierce the skin on the other side. Then withdraw the cautery, resulting in four cauterizations; if you wish, use the three-pointed cautery instead.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing pus to collect for several days; this is more effective than any other previous treatment.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is helpful in hydrops of the abdomen, particularly. If you have treated the patient with the kinds of remedies mentioned in the section, and your treatment has not been successful, then you should cauterize four times around the navel, and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver and one on the spleen, and two behind his back, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach.  The depth of the cauterization should be roughly the thickness of the skin.  Then leave the cauterization open, allowing pus to collect for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient with further treatment after the cauterization, as is proper, so that the affected parts may quickly heal.  The cautery iron for the back is nail-shaped, as previously described, and that for the front is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they (the feet) swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize the back of the foot at the point between the little finger and the ring finger.
Hold your hand with the cautery iron straight, do not bend it.  Then raise your hand and do not lower it again, for it will drain the yellow water.  The cautery iron should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg.  Use the sharp end of the cautery iron.  Cauterize along the length of the leg, one just below the knee, the other lower down toward the middle of the leg.  Cauterize each thigh, two cauterizations on each.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, allowing the water to drain.  Then treat it with the rest of the methods we have described.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On cauterizing dysentery.
If the dysentery is due to cold and dampness, weakening the digestive and binding forces in the stomach and intestines, and if treatment has been unsuccessful, and you see the patient is strong enough to tolerate a cautery, cauterize his stomach with a large cautery. As previously mentioned, cauterize the stomach with a circular cautery iron.  Apply four cauterizations around the navel using a fine pointed cautery iron, and cauterize the lower back area above the coccyx with one or two large cauteries.
If you observe excessive moisture and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the pubic area, each flank, and perhaps add two small cauterizations near the larger one on the stomach. This is a sure and effective treatment, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the rectum.
If there are many chronic hemorrhoids in the rectum, or a single one, composed of thick cold humors or putrid moisture, and treatment as described in the previous sections has been ineffective, then cauterize the patient with three triangular cauteries on the lower part of the spine, slightly below the table.
And cauterize below the navel with a cauterization the size of two fingers.  If you judge that the patient's stomach is cold, and their food is not digesting, and you see their face swollen, cauterize the stomach with a large cautery as previously described, and cauterize the liver. And another on the spleen with a nail-like cautery. Leave the cauterization open for a time, then treat until healed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery, which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart, and press firmly until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with suitable ointments until healed.  If a large cauterization is made on the cotton, it is more effective.
Chapter thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula that occurs in the buttocks and surrounding areas.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure described in its place, and recoils from it, perhaps he will be cured by cauterization.  If someone has a fistula that has persisted and pus and corrupt humors are discharged from it, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then determine the depth of its penetration with the probe, then heat the cautery iron, resembling a file, and insert it hot into the same fistula in line with the depth of the fistula and the extent to which the probe entered. Cauterize it repeatedly, until all the corrupt matter is burned, once, twice, or three times, as needed.
And beware of burning a nerve, if there is one, or a large vein, if the fistula leads to the bladder or to the intestines.
And be cautious of all these locations.  Only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you judge that it is not penetrative.  Then treat the area until it heals.  If the area seals over, the materials cease, and it remains so for a time, know that it has been completely cured.
And if the materials do not cease, know that it is penetrative, or there is a corrupt bone or something similar within its depth, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys due to cold or thick wind, and the patient's sexual function is diminished as a result, it is appropriate to cauterize the kidneys on the flanks, with a cauterization on each kidney using the nail-shaped cautery mentioned previously.  Sometimes we cauterize a third time on the same spot, achieving three consecutive cauterizations, which is more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and dampness, such that the patient cannot control urination, cauterize the bladder with one cauterization below the navel, where the pubic hair begins, and another cauterization to the right and left of the navel.
After each cauterization, the cauterization should be the size of a thumb's width.  Cauterize the lower back, or two cauterizations if needed.  The cautery should be nail-shaped as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-nine.

Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous moisture may collect in the hip joint, causing it to dislocate.  A sign of this is that one leg is longer than the other when the legs are measured against each other.  A hollow is found at the site of the dislocation.  A semicircular cauterization should be performed on the affected hip joint.
After marking the joint with ink in a circular fashion so that the joint is centered in the circle, use the cautery pictured in the stomach cauterization.  If this cautery is unavailable, use three cauterizations with the large olive cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach all the skin's thickness, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-one.
In cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and the treatment described in the classification was ineffective and the condition prolonged, it is necessary to thin the thick humors with rotten seeds, prickly pear seeds, or similar remedies.
Then cauterize.  Cauterization has two types: either with burning medicines or with fire.  Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations, like this image.
This creates a beneficial depth.  The distance between each cauterization should be roughly the thickness of a finger.  The cautery should be olive-colored.  One could also cauterize in the center of the hip joint itself, resulting in four cauterizations.  If desired, one cauterization can be applied in a circular manner, as previously described in cauterizing a hip dislocation.
The circle should encircle the entire hip and burn the entire thickness of the skin.  This is the method of cauterization for sciatica, if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh or leg.
If the pain extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize twice on the thigh, at the location of the pain indicated by the patient.  Cauterize once above the heel, approximately four fingers towards the outer side.  The cautery should be a pointed/slitting type, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize at the indicated location with a pinpoint cautery three or four times, or more if necessary. If the pain is below the knee towards the leg, cauterize with a single pointed cautery there.
In all your cauterizations, be careful not to cauterize so deeply that you burn a major nerve or artery, as this would inflict a serious injury or ailment upon the patient.
I have observed one and another who cauterized above the heel and overused the cautery, causing the leg to become swollen until it reached the foot, and the entire foot to ulcerate and rot, then diarrhoea and death followed. If the pain is in both locations, cauterize them using the same method.
Some wise scholars have described a hip cauterization using this method: a cup-like instrument is made of iron, with a diameter of half a span.  Its rim is as thick as a date stone, or slightly less.  Inside this cup, there is another cup, and then a third.
There is a space equivalent to the width of a thumb's breadth between each cup.  The cups are open on both sides and their height is about one or two thumb's breadth.  A handle made of iron is securely attached to the cups.  This is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until red-hot and emitting sparks.  Then it is placed on the affected hip area, with the patient leaning on their healthy side.  Three circular cauterizations are made at once.  Then, the patient is left for three days, the wound is dressed with ghee, and the wound is left open for several days.
Then treat it with ointment until healed.  The author of this book said, "This type of cauterization is rarely used due to its harshness and frightening appearance, and few can endure it.  However, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it, and for those who have it in the correct place.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it involves making two cups resembling the rings through which the hinges of a door pass, either of copper or iron.
Their height will be about two thumbbreadths or similar, and the distance between them will be about the thickness of a finger.  The lower and upper parts will be open, and they will be fastened together in this way.
Then place them on the affected hip area, the patient lying on their healthy side.  Hold your hand gently.  Then pour a little strong, heated water between the two circles. Hold it for about the time of an hour-glass. The patient must endure the sting and burning, for it feels like fire.
Until the stinging subsides. Then lift the instrument, and wipe the affected hip area with fresh water, removing the strong water.  Leave it for three days, and apply ghee until the black marks of the burn disappear. Leave it for several days to allow the pus to drain; this is more beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.  If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for containing the water, similar to the one made for the hip. This is the description of the strong water. It is confirmed in the treatise on correcting medicines.
Take one part of lye and one part of quicklime, grind them, and place them in a new pot with a single small hole in the bottom, sized to fit the pestle. Place a separate glazed pot underneath the bottom of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the lye and lime until it covers them by a finger's breadth, after grinding them well with your hand. Leave the pot until the strong water drips into the glazed pot below. Then collect all that water, and pour it over fresh lime and lye, also freshly prepared.
It will then be extremely strong in its sharpness, useful in many medical procedures and for cauterizing other parts of the body, for it acts like fire itself.  Some medications, such as safflower, balaذر honey, and lime mixed with soap, are ground and can also cauterize the hip.
Galen, recounting an ancient remedy for hip and sciatica pain, greatly praised it, claiming it requires no other treatment and heals in a single application.
Even, perhaps, a patient was carried into a bath and emerged healed.  This involves taking fresh green chiṭṭarīj; if no fresh green is available, use fresh dry chiṭṭarīj, grind it with some fat, and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
Then bind it tightly and leave it on for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Then take the patient to the bath.  When their body is damp, place them in a tub.  The pain will subside, and the patient will heal, God willing. If not healed, repeat the dressing after ten days.
He will heal, God willing.  Know that no one should use these cauterizing medications until the body is emptied. Dioscorides mentioned that goat's hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be done thus: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep spot between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow.  Then, take dry goat's hair.
Then ignite it with fire until it becomes a glowing ember, place it on the wool, and leave it until it dies down.  Then take another one and repeat this until sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing.  This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat's hair.
The Forty-second Chapter.
Concerning cauterization of back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from numerous causes, such as a fall, blow, excessive vomiting, and similar occurrences.  It may result from the accumulation of a cold, moist substance.  Cauterization is only effective in this type of case.
After the patient has vomited, using substances like rotten seeds, one should cauterize the back where the pain is, making three rows across the table. Each row should have five cauterizations or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and strength, marking the area with ink first.
Cauterization should be done with a pinpoint cautery.  If desired, you may also use a medium-sized nail cautery, applying three or four cauterizations in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
In cauterizing the initial bump.
This ailment often affects young children.  A sign of its onset in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and a bead-like protrusion appears at the end of the spinal vertebrae, standing out from the other beads.
If you see this and want to stop its progression, cauterize it with a circular cautery of this type. Apply the cautery to each side of the vertebra evenly. If you prefer, cauterize around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery, in two or three rows.  Keep the pinpoints close together. Then treat the affected area until healed, using the methods described.
Avoid using cautery on a bump that results from nerve tension.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are caused by cold dampness that flows to any part of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, physicians usually call it gout, specifically.
If the cold gout, the cause, is treated with the methods we have described in the classification, and the pain does not subside, cauterization will relieve it.  Cauterize the area around the joints of the legs many times after inducing vomiting.  Use a medium-sized olive-oil cautery in this manner.
And if it is necessary to apply small cauteries to the leg, do so with a pointed cautery.  If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, as often happens, cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with the same olive-oil cautery.
If more cauterization is needed, cauterize, but do not make the cautery too deep; it should only penetrate the skin's thickness.  If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use the cauterization methods described in their respective sections.
If the pain is only in the hands, apply small cauteries around the wrists, in two rows.  If the pain remains in the fingers, apply a small cautery to each joint and on the palm of the hand.  If the pain ascends to the elbows or shoulders after several days,
Cauterize them on both sides.  The patient must also receive good treatment and take medications.  If he improves his diet and expels phlegm, he will recover with this cauterization, God willing.
The forty-fifth chapter.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some of the intestines and bowels have descended to the testicle, and this is a recent onset, the patient should be instructed to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives to encourage bowel movements.
Then, have the patient lie on his back before you, and instruct him to restrain himself until the bowels or intestines are expelled. Then, push them back with your finger.  Afterwards, mark the area beneath the hernia on the pubic bone with a marking resembling a semicircle, its ends extending towards the upper body.
Then, heat a cautery iron – this is its form.
Until it becomes white and emits sparks. Then return the intestines or bowels to their place.  Afterwards, have a servant place their hand over the area to prevent the intestines from protruding.  Spread the patient's legs, and place a pillow beneath them. Another servant should hold the patient's legs together.
And another servant should hold the patient's hands on his chest. Then, apply the cautery iron to the same mark. Your hand holding the cautery iron should be held upright, and maintain the application until it reaches the bone, or repeat the process if it did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
Take care to prevent the intestines from protruding while cauterizing, so as not to burn the patient, which could result in death or a great injury. Understand that if the cauterization does not reach the bone, your effort will not succeed. The cautery iron should be appropriate in size for children and for adults.
Then, treat the cauterized area with ghee after three days, to alleviate the burning, then treat it with other ointments until it heals.  Let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the wound is closed.
His diet during his treatment should be such as to soften his bowels, so that the intestines do not protrude when he evacuates.  Then, if he wishes to stand after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  Reduce his exertion, and limit his intake of food, drink, and loud shouting.
If he follows this treatment, he will recover completely, God willing.  I will describe the treatment of hernias by incision in its own section, God willing.  As for a hernia that develops throughout the abdomen and is in its early stages, if you wish to prevent it from worsening, cauterize the hernia with a circular cautery iron of the appropriate size. Let the amount of skin burned be about a third of the hernia's size. Then treat it as described above; it will not worsen, God willing. Some earlier scholars described cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery iron, in this manner.
After drawing a line on the hernia with ink, making the upper horizontal line of the hernia and the other line below it, place your hand in the middle to cauterize with a single, nail-like cautery. The initial cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in some limb, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time and does not respond to treatment with medications, then the affected area should be cauterized with a pointed cautery iron.  The cauterization of each limb should be performed according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
If the pain and fistula are relieved by the first cauterization, then the patient is cured. Otherwise, the cauterization should be repeated, as it is common for these ailments to spread from the affected limb to neighboring areas.  Therefore, cauterization should be continued until the patient recovers.
Chapter Forty-Seven.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers may greatly benefit from cauterization, especially those with leprosy arising from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, examine the leper.  If the leprosy is in its early stages, and if treatment as described in the section on classification has been applied, and it has not worsened, or stagnated, and you fear the corruption may spread throughout the patient's entire temperament, cauterize the head five times. The first on the usual middle of the head, the second below it toward the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind at the nape of the neck.  Cauterize thoroughly until a slight effect is produced on the bone.
Scales will peel off, facilitating the discharge of thick vapors, and cauterize the spleen as previously described.  If, however, the leprosy has persisted and become evident, then you should cauterize with the methods on the head that we have described. Cauterize the tip of the nose, two cauterizations on the cheeks, two on the cervical vertebrae, six on the dorsal vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the tailbone, another above it in the same area, and two on each thigh. Two on the knees, one on each knee, two on the collarbones, two on the elbows, two on the sides of the chest, and cauterize each joint of the fingers and toes, and each heel of the feet and the forearm of the hands.
And protect the nerve behind the heels from burning, and a cauterization can be performed on the pubic bone, another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations, the more beneficial and effective.
Know that the patient will not feel pain from the cauterizations as much as a healthy person, because the body has become numb.  The cauterizing tools should vary in size and shape, according to the organs and joints, as described in the characteristics of the instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with fine crushed frankincense mixed with honey, and the rest of the treatment until he/she is healed, God willing.
The forty-eighth chapter.
In cauterizing numbness. If a limb has become numb and has been treated with medicines, ointments, and dressings, but has not healed, then cauterize the numb limb with cauterizations appropriate to the size of the affected bone/limb.  Let your cauterizations be slightly deeper in the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Cauterization may also be used for some numbness that affects the hand or foot in the spinal vertebrae, at the exit point of the nerve that moves that limb, to remove the numbness.
One should not undertake this unless they are knowledgeable in the anatomy of limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
The forty-ninth chapter.
In cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it with a small depth, proportional to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  horsetail leaves, and dove's blood or hookworm blood, one part of each. Mix all together, apply to a cloth, and bind to the affected area until it heals.
The fiftieth chapter.
In cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop its progression, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it moves.  Some scholars suggest a severe cauterization in the center; however, I do not recommend this, as I anticipate ulceration, and I have observed this many times.
Therefore, the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we have said, or with multiple cauterizations.
The fifty-first chapter.
In cauterizing the fistula.
If a fistula occurs and is slow to mature, either due to the nature of its formation, or due to the patient's age (if the patient is elderly with low blood volume), or due to time, and you want to hasten the fistula's maturation, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations in a dotting pattern.
Then leave it; this will hasten maturation. If you wish to close it with cauterization, heat the cautery iron with the form shown here.
Place it in the center of the fistula until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be below, so that the pus can drain more easily. Then treat it with what is appropriate until it heals.
The fifty-second chapter.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are nothing but decay that ravages the organ, consuming it as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, heat a cautery iron, consisting of many small and large nails, according to what is suitable for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the decay is eradicated and nothing remains. Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scab and all the decay are removed.
Then treat it with ointments that promote the growth of flesh. If after three days you see that the flesh grows soundly, without any decay, then do not do anything further. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on any remaining decayed areas.  The ulcer can also be treated with a strong medicine.
For it serves the same purpose as fire, with quicker benefit.  I have already mentioned treating it with strong medicine in the classification; consult it when necessary.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a harsh, painful condition, often occurs on the soles of the feet.  Cauterization can be done in two ways: either with fire or with strong, sharp water.
As for cauterization with fire, heat a hollowed-out cautery iron, similar to the wing of a griffin, fashioned from iron, that encompasses the nail on all sides and has a thin border. Then, bring the hot cautery down onto the nail and rotate your hand with the cautery around the nail until it reaches the nail's depth.
Leave it for three days, allowing it to suppurate. Then, bandage it with pounded wild thyme mixed with salt. Leave the bandage on for a night, and the nail will detach from its roots. Then, treat the wound with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals—if the nails are not inverted.
It frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  One should take a tube of copper, iron, or griffin's wing, and apply it to the nail or wart.  Then, pour a small amount of strong, sharp water into the tube.
Hold the tube with your hand, rotating it and slightly pressing it, so that the tube's edge impacts the nail's root.  The sharp water will then penetrate to the nail's root.  The patient should endure the sharp water's sting for a while.
Then leave it; the nail will detach from its roots.  Do this one by one, until you have dealt with all of them on the body.  Afterwards, treat the affected areas with flesh-growing ointments.
Chapter Forty-five.
Concerning the cauterization of the scabies.
If someone suffers from scabies caused by cold in the nerves, or from quartan fever, or something similar, you should cauterize four or five times on the spine, between each cauterization, placing one on the chest. And cauterize his stomach with the olive oil cautery, for the scabies will subside and quickly mature the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-five.
Concerning the cauterization of the sores that arise on the body.
Ugly boils may arise on the body, caused by thick, corrupted cold materials.  When they first appear, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a burning stick, or the end of a long frankincense stick, or with a lens cautery.
And boils in their initial eruption may be cauterized in this manner.  Thus, the causative agent will not increase, it will dissipate, and the afflicted person will recover.  However, this must occur after the afflicted person has been bled.
Chapter Fifty-six.
Concerning the cauterization of bleeding occurring after an artery is cut.
Bleeding frequently occurs from a severed artery in a wound from the outside, or when a tumor or organ is incised or cauterized, and the like.  Cutting it off is often difficult.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger over the opening of the artery and press firmly until the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing escapes.  Then, take several small and large olive-shaped cauteries and heat them in the fire until they are very hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, according to the wound and the location where the artery has opened, and apply the cautery to the same vein after quickly removing your finger and holding the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding returns when you remove your finger from the artery's opening and the cautery has cooled, quickly take another cautery from the heated ones and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn the nerve, for this would cause another affliction to the patient.  Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be severed, especially a large one, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop; or it may be tied tightly with threads; or the drugs that stop bleeding and constrict with bandages may be applied firmly.
As for those who attempt to stop it by tying, or by constricting with cloths, or by applying burning things and similar methods, it will not stop that way, except rarely. If someone experiences this and no physician or medicine is present, then let them quickly place their index finger over the opening of the wound, as we described.
Press it firmly until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound and the artery.  The finger should not be removed from over it, keeping it continuously in contact with very cold, icy water, until the blood thickens, clots, and stops flowing.  During this time, observe what cauterization or medicine is needed.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, lancing, venesection, and wounds, and similar procedures.
Khalaf said:  We have already discussed in the first chapter all diseases in which cauterization with fire and burning remedies are appropriate, their causes, reasons, and instruments, and the forms of cauterization. We have made that into chapters, from the crown to the foot.
I will follow the same path in this chapter so that the student may easily find what he seeks.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my children, that this chapter contains more potential for error than the first chapter on cauterization.
For this reason, the caution must be greater, for in this chapter, the work often involves the expenditure of blood, which is essential for life, when opening a vein, or making an incision on a tumor, lancing an abscess, treating a wound, removing an arrow, or incising a stone, and similar procedures.  All of these are accompanied by risk and fear, and death often results.
I advise you to avoid anything doubtful in this practice, for in this craft, you will encounter various types of people with various illnesses.  Some of them are weary of their illness and have little fear of death.
Because of the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, some show signs of impending death.  Others offer you their wealth, hoping for their health; yet their illness is deadly.  You should in no way aid those who exhibit this kind of desperation.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness. Do not undertake anything of this sort unless you have certain knowledge of a favorable outcome. In all your treatment of patients, use profound knowledge and warn them of the possible consequences for their well-being.
For in this, you will find help in gaining praise, renown, remembrance, and thanks. May God, my children, inspire you with wisdom, and may He not deprive you of correctness and success; for that is in His hand, and there is no god but Him.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, following the previous chapter on cauterization, from the head to the foot.  This will, God willing, make it easier for you to find what you need.
Chapter One.
On treating the water that collects in the heads of young boys.
This ailment frequently affects young boys at birth if the midwife presses the boy's head roughly.  It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have seen this ailment only in boys, and all those I observed died quickly. Therefore, I have avoided treating it. I once saw a boy whose head was filled with water, and the head enlarged every day. Until the boy could no longer sit up due to the enlargement of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone or underneath the bone on the peritoneum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, a single horizontal incision should be made in the middle of the head, about two fingers in length, so that the moisture drains. This is the method of incision.
If the moisture is more extensive and the swelling greater, make two intersecting incisions, like this:
If the moisture is under the bone, and the sign is that the sutures of the head are open on all sides, and water descends when pressed with your hand to the inside, this should not be hidden from you.  Then three incisions should be made in the middle of the head, like this.
After the incision, all the moisture will come out. Then, the incisions are closed with strips and bandages.  Then, it is bandaged from above with poultices and oil until the fifth day.  Then, the bandages are removed, and the wound is treated with ointments.  Do not allow the head to be constricted.  The patient should be fed with any dry food low in moisture.
Until the organ strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to observe where the swelling bone and the collection of water appear, as it may be more prevalent in the back of the head, or the front, or on the right or left.
Therefore, aim the incision where the swelling and the accumulation of water are apparent, incising as best you can, being careful not to cut a vein, causing bleeding that will lead to the patient's death from such bleeding, along with the expulsion of the moisture.
The second chapter.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the *ḥassīsīn*.
If someone suffers from a severe affliction of the eyes or chest, and medication proves ineffective, then the best treatment is the severing of these two arteries.
Therefore, if you wish to sever them, you must shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to expose the artery.  Then, tighten the patient's neck with the help of their garment.
