Then, look for where the veins pulse, and their locations, the two hollows behind the ears; rarely are they hidden, except in some people.
Then, mark them with ink, and sever them with a dissecting scalpel, completely to the bone. This should be done with the head in a presentation/exposed position.  If desired, insert the scalpel beneath the artery and cut upwards; the length of the cut should be about two clenched fingers.
If the artery is severed, blood will flow in a pulsating, forward, and continuous stream.  If the artery is not visible to the eye, one should measure from the ear about three finger-widths, then mark it with ink and cut to the bone; and what is necessary to...
Approximately six ounces of blood will immediately flow, sometimes more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries.  Then, examine the wound; if any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it away.
So that a hot swelling does not appear, then bind the wound with a linen thread.  Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic headache or acute ailments from sharp moisture and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headaches, and similar conditions, and treatments from the medical arts have not been effective, then we have tried, in these illnesses, to lance or cauterize the arteries of the temples, as described.
The procedure for lancing them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the apparent artery in the temple should be located.  Its pulsation will make it clear; it is rare for it to be hidden, except perhaps in some individuals.
Or, if the cold is severe and it is not obvious, then the patient should tighten his neck with his clothes, and you should rub the area with a cloth, or apply hot water to the area until the artery clearly and distinctly appears. Then take the lancet which has this image. Then gently slice the skin with it until you reach the artery, and insert a probe into it. Pull it upward until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides.  If the artery is thin, twist it with the end of the probe. Then cut off a portion of it, sufficient to separate its ends and cause constriction, without causing bleeding. If it is not severed and not cut, the blood will not recede.  Then drain the blood from six to three ounces.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread.  The thread should be either silk or bowstring material, so that the wound does not become infected before healing, and bleeding does not occur.
Then cut off the excess between the ligatures. Do this at that time or after some time.  If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a scalpel-like cautery, until its ends are severed, this will accomplish the same or better results as described.
Unless the patient has a fever or an inflamed temperament, because cauterization aids in the destruction of moisture, and is more beneficial.  After dissecting the arteries, the area should be packed with old linen and covered with firm dressings.  After healing, treat with dry medications that promote flesh growth and with compresses until healed, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, quickly stop it either by cauterization or by filling the area with alum and pressing firmly until the blood stops.  If none of these are available, use your finger to stop the blood.
Apply very cold water to the area until the pain subsides and it is properly firm.  Easier and less demanding than dissecting the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the areas with ink.
Apply intense heat until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places, so the ends are far apart.  This cauterization will not cause them to join.  This is an image of the cautery.
The blades are sharp, resembling small blades, but much less sharp than a knife.  If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them more quickly and the flesh would not be severed quickly. If there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire will remain there.
This quickly cuts the flesh.  This method is superior, easier, and less demanding than any other. The distance between the blades should be approximately the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
For the treatment of persistent, hot tearing from the eyes.
If the tearing is constant, and the discharge is from the veins on the skull from outside, and if nothing from medical treatment with drugs is helping, and you see that the patient's face has become red. He feels a crawling sensation in his forehead, like that of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their edges eroded and their eyelids glued shut by the intensity of the tears.  Early practitioners of this treatment, therefore, were driven to this procedure.  It is called treatment with the sword, and it involves ordering the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, along its length.  Each incision is about two fingers long, parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second one slightly spaced from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the opposite side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the contact of the jaws.  After each incision, there should be a distance of three fingers held together.  Have prepared sponge or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then insert the sharp double-ended scalpel, which is of this form. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, with it, all the skin between the incisions, along with the periosteum on the bone, is scraped away.  Then, do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, and then remove the scalpel. And insert into the first incision another instrument, called a single-edged, sharp knife, with a smooth, non-sharp side.  This is its image.
Place its sharp side towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone.  Push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes without reaching the skin's surface.
Then do this from the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas of coagulated blood.  Then place a cotton wick in each incision, and put a dressing moistened with a mixture of oil and vinegar, or oil and vinegar, upon it.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, the ligaments will heal. Use copious lukewarm water compresses. Then treat it with Basil ointment, rose oil, and other remedies used for wounds until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On treating tears and discharges from the interior of the head to the eyes.
When someone suffers from frequent, severe, persistent discharges, and the eyes appear weakened and small, and their sight is impaired, and the eyelids are ulcerated and the eyelashes fall out. And there will be a sharp, painful ache deep within the head, and repeated sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these fluids and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then to make one incision in the center of the forehead.
And there will be a sharp, painful ache deep within the head, and repeated sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these fluids and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then to make one incision in the center of the forehead. Or to raise it slightly transversely, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be towards the bone, keeping your hand away from the movable temporal muscles during chewing.  Once the bone is exposed and all blood has been wiped away with a sponge...
Then separate the edges of the incision with absorbent cotton or linen thread.  Then press from above with compresses soaked in medicine and oil to prevent inflammation.  When you have resolved it and see that the inflammation is gone, you should rub the bone so that flesh begins to grow.
Then treat it with a desiccated preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts of a certain substance (likely a medicinal preparation) and preparing them into an ointment.  Use this ointment to encourage the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of things that fall into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic or semi-metallic stone, such as iron or glass; or a vegetable seed, such as chickpeas, nuts, and similar; or a liquid, such as water or vinegar, and similar.
Or if an animal, such as a stone or similar object that does not proliferate within the ear falls into the ear, face the ear towards the sun. If you see the stone, apply a drop of violet or siruj oil to it. Then try to remove it by moving your head or causing a sneeze with a catheter. Close the nostrils when the sneeze comes after placing a bandage of linen or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards.  Often this treatment will remove it. If it does not, then try to remove it with the gentle forceps which has this shape.
If it comes out with the forceps, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly bent, blunt hook. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil, so that air has no way to pass except through the tube. Then pull it out with a strong pulling motion.  Often it will come out with this. If it does not come out with the methods described, then take a small piece of Nabataean gum or prepared gum, which birds are caught with, and place it at the end of the applicator.
After wrapping it tightly with linen, gently insert it into the ear canal after the ear has dried from moisture. If it does not come out with all the described methods, proceed to incision before a hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient in the temples, taking blood according to their strength. Then seat the patient before you, tilt their ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the cartilage, at the lower part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone. Then remove it with the best instruments you have. Then stitch the incision immediately and treat it until it heals.
If the object that has fallen into the ear is one of the grains that proliferate and swell, try to remove it with the methods mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a thin, delicate scalpel, like this.
Try to cut that type of fallen grain in the ear with it, but only do this if you are certain that the grain has become moistened with ear wax until it is divided into many small pieces. Then remove it with a blind probe, a soft swab, or with suction.
As mentioned, it is easier to remove it.  As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use a bellows to induce sneezing.  They should first fill their ear with some old cotton while lying on the affected ear.  If it comes out that way.
Otherwise, take many small, smooth pebbles, warm them slightly over a fire.  The patient should insert one into the ear canal.  The patient should then stand on one leg, on the same side as the affected ear, and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with pebble after pebble until all the water is removed.  Water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or feather, inserting one end into the ear, and holding the other end over a fire until most of it is burned, and then using another feather.
Repeat this until all the water is expelled or use the tube as described for the pebble.  As for removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, like a flea or similar, treat it as described in the classification.  As for...
If it is large enough to be felt, try to remove it with absorbent cotton and forceps.  This is easier than all other ear problems.  As for removing worms generated in the ear, if treatment with what was mentioned in the classification in the chapter on earworms has not been effective.
You should examine the ear in the sun. If you see any worms, remove them with absorbent cotton or fine forceps. If you do not see anything, take a tube—this is its form.
It is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top.  Insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck vigorously.  Do this repeatedly until all the worms are removed. If this does not work, seal around the tube with wax, as I mentioned for the pebble.
If it is not removed by the methods described, then use the ear drops that I have mentioned.  The ancients have used them to kill worms, and you will find this in the chapter on ear drops.  You can use this instrument to apply ointments and medicines to the ear. This is its image:.
As you see, it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, the tube inside the instrument can be made of strong copper, or you can take a thread and wrap it tightly around the end with cotton.
Then place the ointment, juice, or whatever medicine you want in the tube, while it is in the ear. Then, using the thread with cotton, gently squeeze with your hand until the medicine is driven into the ear and the patient feels it.
Have the patient lie inside, and whatever you pour into the ear should be warmed slightly in the fire. Beware that the substance being instilled should not be extremely cold or extremely hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of ear obstruction.
Some children may be born from their mothers with their ear canals unperforated.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or growing flesh within it.  This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, and not visible to the eye.
Sometimes it may appear slightly outside the canal and be visible.  Obstructions deep within the ear, unseen, are often difficult to cure.  If the obstruction is visible, the patient's ear should be placed in the sun. And examine it. If you see the obstruction outwardly, open it with a gentle scalpel, in this manner:
Its tip should have a slightly limited projection, while the rest of the scalpel should have smooth sides so as not to injure the ear.  If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate forceps and carefully sever it to remove all the flesh.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient experiencing a dullness of hearing, gently rotate it.
Be careful not to injure the ear drum. Then place a wick, sized to fit the canal, coated with Egyptian ointment, in the ear.  Do this for several days until the wound is healed. Alternatively, soak a wick in water, and sprinkle powdered zeag on it.
Use it.  If you see the ear swollen with a hot swelling, you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick coated in a preparation made from rose oil until the hot swelling subsides. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding described in the section on cauterization.
Chapter Eight.
On treating warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  One should grasp them with a scoop or forceps, cut them with a scalpel from their roots, then apply powdered alum to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterization is better after the cutting, and it often returns if anything of the roots remains.  If cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On treating colds affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling a cold, both in intensity and hardness, often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called a cold, being a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelid.  If the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid, and moves in every direction, then it is easily treated by making a transverse incision on it.
Then gently peel it away from all sides until it is removed, then suspend it with a hook and cut it. If cutting it is not possible without piercing the eyelid, it will not harm the patient. If the opening is large, stitch it together and treat it until healed.
If it is small, there is no need to worry; the ointment will heal and close it. If the cold is inclined toward the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, suspend the cold with a hook without needing an incision, and trim it from every side.
If the eyelid is pierced by the cut, this will not harm it. Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting the cold, and treat the area with something that will heal it until the patient is cured.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the pterygium that appears on the eyelid.
The pterygium is a membrane that is found in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It most often affects boys, weighing down their eyes and causing them to have colds in the mornings, and they cannot look at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces, and they sleep on them. Their eyelids are moist beneath the eyebrows, and they may have swelling. The pterygium is obvious. If you press the area with your fingers, you feel the pterygium between them.  The procedure is for the patient to place their head on your lap.
Then, take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, and form a circle from that wick, encompassing the pterygium from all sides. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the pterygium into the center of the circle.
Then, cut through the middle of that moisture with a lancet, a cut across. The cut should not be wider than a cut made in bloodletting.  As for depth, you must cut through the entire skin until you reach the pterygium.
In most cases, the pterygium protrudes from the cut like a white piece of fat.  You pull it outward with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, moving your hand right and left, until it heals.  Beware of increasing the cut, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the pterygium does not appear at the initial cut, you should increase the cut slightly and gently until the pterygium appears.  Then, pull it out as we said, then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and apply it to the area, securing it with a bandage.  Some people crush salt.
They place it in the opening of the cut to dissolve any remaining moisture. Then treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the varieties of eye dressings.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course, beneath the natural lashes, and become thick, they harm the eye and cause various illnesses such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and white discoloration and thickening, even causing the eye to become useless.
And the treatment of the eye has four aspects: either by cauterization with fire, or by a sharp medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization; or the treatment is by cutting and stitching, or by using a reed, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, then do nothing; otherwise, insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid, and pierce the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself.  Pull the thread upwards through the eyelid.
Turn it with a rod, then cut into the inside of the eyelid, away from the excess hair, using a sharp lancet from the larger to the smaller incision. Then, remove the thread and place a small pad of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then bind the eyelid with a bandage.
Like the shape of an artemisia leaf, but it should be shaped according to the amount of eyelid elevation you need, because it can vary from person to person. Some may need to cut a suitable amount of the eyelid, commensurate with the relaxation of the eyelid.
Some need a smaller cut, all depending on the relaxation of the eyelid. Then, cut with the lancet along the two lines you marked, starting from the larger incision to the smaller.  Each cut should be close to the natural hair, about the thickness of a rod.
Then, insert a hook into one of the corners of the skin and peel it all away. Then, bring the two edges together with sutures using a needle and thin wool thread, clean away the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
You may or may not do this; it does not matter. The sutures and threads remain for about three or four days. Then, treat it.  If you prefer, leave the wound unsutured and treat it with something that dries and constricts. The eyelid will rise when the wound is closed and brought together. Sutures are better.
This method of incision, mentioned by the ancients, is somewhat burdensome for the patient, but it is a good procedure and carries no risk. Another method of incision exists: to shape the eyelid like an artemisia leaf, as we described. Then, lift the eyelid with three hooks, either spaced apart or grouped together in this manner.
Then, trim away any excess eyelid with a small scissors in this manner, cutting evenly.
If you are unable to hold the hooks in place, or if the procedure is not successful, take a needle with thread. Insert it into the center of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger opening, and a third near the smaller opening. Hold the threads evenly between your fingers. Then, lift your hand with a steady movement.
Lift the marked skin completely, as described. Then, close the wound edges with stitches and treat it until healed. If a hot swelling appears after cutting or incision, reduce the swelling with *qirrot* and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its edges turn inward.  In this case, the methods described for cutting, stitching, and cauterization with a strong medicine or cauterization with fire should be used.  Cauterization with a strong medicine or fire is milder and easier on the patient than cutting and stitching.  Cutting and stitching are more difficult.
Cauterization with reeds is performed as follows: Turn the eyelid inside out and cut a slit as described previously.  Then, make two thin reeds or pieces of wood the length of the eyelid and narrower than a scalpel's width.
Having notched their edges on both sides where the threads grip, gently gather them to the remaining part of the eyelid.  Tightly and firmly pull the reeds from both sides. Leave for a few days; the tightened skin will die, turn black, and putrefy.
Until it falls off naturally. If it takes too long, trim it with a pair of shears, then treat it until it heals. Once healed, the eyelid will lift, and the eyelashes will not prick the eye. This is the shape and size of the two reeds: [image/drawing omitted].
Chapter Twelve.
For removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If a hair or two are bothering, take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, thin silk thread, tie the ends in a very gentle knot. Make the thread about a span long.
Then, tie another thin thread, shorter than the first, to the needle's eye.  If desired, tie its ends. The patient should place their head near your hand, close to the light, so the work is clear, as it's a delicate procedure.  Then, using the needle and thread, insert the thread into the root of the excess hair.
And carefully pull it through the eyelid until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread upward, and remove the hair from the needle.  If there are one, two, or three hairs, remove them all from the needle.
Then, pull your hand to extract the hair along with the needle through the eyelid, alongside the natural hair. If pulling the needle doesn't remove the hair, pull the thread attached to the needle downwards to extract it from the top opening.
Then, reposition the hair in the needle and pull both. Repeat until the hair is high enough in the opening that you can see it clearly. Then remove the needle and thread. Compress the eye and leave it compressed for two or three days, until the hair is securely in place and tissue has grown around it.
If the hair is short, either attach a longer strand of natural hair to it, so they rise together, or let it grow longer and then remove it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the treatment of the stye that occurs on the upper eyelid of the eye.
The stye, which this stye is subject to, is called an arnbia.  This stye is either natural or accidental.  The accidental one results from the inflammation of a wound, cut, burn, or similar condition.
The procedure is to cut the inflammation, separate its edges, and place a linen thread between them, tying it until it heals.  One should not use things that dry and constrict in treating it.
For if you do that, the stye will return worse than before. Instead, use things that loosen it, such as fenugreek, soaked in boiled water and rosewater, flaxseed, and diachylon ointment mixed with some oils.  Apply this mixture to the thread and use it for treatment.
The aim of treatment is to prevent it from adhering in its original form by all means possible.  This method we have described is only a part of correcting the stye, not a complete return to the original condition.
Fourteenth Chapter.
On treating styes in the lower eyelid.
This stye that occurs from below is properly called a stye, and it is natural;  an accidental stye arises from a wound, cut, burn, or the like.
The method is to take a needle with a doubled thread, insert it into the flesh, piercing from the inner to the outer corner until the thread is on both ends of the flesh. Then, pull the flesh upward with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper form, well and good. Otherwise, take a spatula and place it on the site of the cut. Use it to turn the eyelid over and make two cuts on the inside of the eyelid, with the ends of the two cuts starting from the angles of the cut and meeting to form an angle. So that when they meet, the shape will resemble this shape.
Then, remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp side is lower than the eyelid, then rejoin the separated parts with two stitches of wool thread.  Then treat it with the relaxing and twisting remedies we previously mentioned until it heals.
If the fissure occurred from a cut, stitch, or cauterization, then a small incision should be made, also without touching the eyelashes, as previously described.  Then separate the lips with a twisting motion, as we have stated.
In short, for treating a fissure whether it is above or below, perform the procedure according to the appearance of the fissure.  For the fissure can vary greatly in appearance.  A skilled artisan, by whatever means possible, devises a solution.
Until the shape is restored to, or nearly restored to, its natural state.  The disease itself indicates the suitable treatment and tools to use in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, hindering its ease of movement and impeding its normal functions.  Therefore, a slender instrument should be inserted under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, one not as sharp as cutting scalpels, but rather somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slips and injures the eye with its sharpness. Your cutting should be as if you were peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion is healed. Then, instill into the eye salty water to wash it, or instill a solution of zinjari (a type of antimony) in water from a dropper. Then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen thread.
And place over the eye a piece of cotton soaked in egg white. After three days, use medicated drops until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the toenail and deep flesh growths.
The toenail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a hard, white moisture that, if touched by iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook, breaks.
And a hook does not hold it. Both types begin from the largest part and gradually spread until they cover the eye and block light and eye movement.  The procedure for cutting them is that the patient places their head on a support.
Then, he opens his eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand.  Then, you grasp the toenail with a slightly curved hook, pulling it upward.  Then, take a needle, thread it with horse or ox hair and strong thread, bend the needle tip slightly, and insert it into the middle of the toenail.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the toenail with the thread, pull it upward, and use the hair to peel the toenail's side towards the cornea, as if spreading it out with the hair to its end.  Then, cut the remainder at the base of the nail with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural nail flesh.
So that a constant flow of tears is not produced from its cutting.  The toenail may be pulled out with the hook alone, or with the thread, as we said, then the smooth blade peels the portion which is this image:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, causing a rupture in the cornea and injuring the pupil.  Then, after cutting, place some powdered salt or zinjārī shiyāf in the eye, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the toenail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure a hook, this type of toenail is often treated with medicines and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this image. Carefully scrape the toenail from above with it.  If you see that it is crumbling and only the scraping has taken effect, drop some zinjārī shiyāf or red shiyāf, or powdered salt, from your watch into the eye.  Hold the eye closed until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot swelling appears in the eye. Leave it, and treat the hot swelling until it heals, then resume scraping until it heals.  And concerning the protruding flesh (of the toenail).
If the protruding flesh causes excessive pain to the eye, attach it with a hook and cut off some of it. Do not cut too much, lest weeping occur. Then, instill red or zinjārī shiyāf into the depths until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the warding and excessive flesh that grows in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, layered flesh may grow until it covers the pupil or nearly covers it, or even extends onto the eyelids.  Sometimes the eyelids turn outward, resembling a rose.
The procedure is to have the patient rest their head on your lap, then open their eyes, and use appropriate hooks to remove all the red flesh.
Or hold it with a lancet or forceps, then cut the flesh piece by piece until it is all gone, protecting the eye from harm during the procedure.  Your cuts can be made with the scalpel we described for cutting the toenail, or with small scissors. Like those used to gather splinters, as their image will appear in the next chapter.
Once you have finished cutting and all the flesh is gone, fill the eye with pounded salt or drop in red or similar corrosive medicines.  Place a white egg white compress on the outside of the eye to alleviate the inflamed swelling.
If any flesh remains and bleeding is excessive, causing you to fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it to reduce the swelling, then return to the procedure until healed.  Likewise, deal with any excess flesh of this type that appears in the eye.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye by either natural means or by applying iron, and do not insert your hand into any of these procedures until you see that the organ is suitable for the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the extraction of foreign bodies from the eye.
Foreign bodies are red veins that weave over the eye, hindering its function and weakening it over time.  Therefore, you must first examine it.  If the eye with the foreign bodies is strong and has no other ailment besides the foreign bodies.
Then, extraction is performed by instructing the patient to place their head on your lap.  Then, grasp the veins with one or two fine hooks, as your skill dictates.  The hooks should be flexible in this manner:
Or the hooks may be double-ended on a single piece, in this manner:
Then, delicately extract the veins with a fine pair of scissors.  Occasionally wipe away the blood until you see that the eye is clear of those veins and the blood has drained.  Take care not to injure the eye with the scissors' blades.  Work in the mid-day sun.
And be very diligent in your work so as not to cut anything other than the veins. Once finished, instill green or red cauterizing powder into the eye to consume any remaining foreign bodies.  If you were unable to remove all of them at that time, bandage the eye to soothe any pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and any inflammation has gone down. Then, repeat the procedure exactly as described until the patient is healed. This is the manner of using the scissors to remove the problem.
Leave it [and the patient's head should rest on the student's lap, and another student should wipe away tears and examine the front and back of the eye].  The essential method is to open the eye using a fixed or triangular instrument, held by a student.
Focused, and the patient's head rests on the student's lap, while another wipes away tears and examines the front and back of the eye.  You have the choice to begin extracting from the top or bottom of the eye.  It is better to remove two forceps from the upper eyelid, one at the back and the other at the front edge.
Hold it [the forceps] between the middle and index finger, then suspend forceps from beneath it. Then, hold the forceps between the ring and little finger, and slowly remove your hand so that you see all the foreign bodies raised.  If you desire, cut them out with small scissors; if not, with a gentle scalpel.
Insert the needle into the affected area and probe all the foreign bodies until they rise to the surface.  Do not cut or break them apart, as tears and blood will make it difficult.
Then cut with the scissors any part exceeding the edge, both top and bottom, so that only what remains on the cornea is exposed.  If possible, lift any that remain on the cornea with the needle; it is better, although in some cases this may not be possible.
If you cut it, a scab will form, relieving the pain and inflammation, then resume the procedure.
Chapter Nineteen.
Regarding the repositioning of the nasal septum.
If physicians treat a deviated nasal septum (nasus) with cautery or corrosive medications, as previously described, and it does not heal, the only recourse is to incise the tumor when it is ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see damage or discoloration, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
The scraper, made of Indian iron, has a round head like a button, engraved with fine grooves like a rasp or a file. Place it on the affected area of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly clenching your hand, until you are certain that the damage has been removed.
Repeat this several times, then apply binding, drying medications to the area. If the area heals, flesh grows, the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, pus, or any new problems.
Therefore, know that it is healed. If not, there is no other recourse but to return the deviated septum to the nasal opening in this manner: expose the bone again with a knife or a corrosive medicine. Once the bone is exposed, take a bone spreader of this shape:
The end of the iron instrument is triangular, and its shaft is a tapered, pointed cone as you see.  Then place it on the bone itself, near the nostrils, keeping your hand a little further from the eye.  Rotate your hand with the spreader until the bone is pierced and the patient feels it.
If he finds air escaping from it when he holds his hand over his nose, then bind the area with drying astringent medications, as I have taught you.  If flesh grows and the area hardens, the material that had been protruding outward will return to the nose, which will be less of a burden on the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On repositioning the protruding eye.
If the entire eye has protruded, and there is no impairment or loss of sight, then the patient should be eased, then fastened in a sling.  Then, apply a cupping glass to the back of the neck, without any specific rules, and perform a gentle cupping.
Then, apply a compress made of acacia, aloes, frankincense, and henna to the eye.  Then, bind the eye from above on the bandage with many thin strips.  The method for making these thin strips is to take a soft linen cloth, fold each piece into four folds, and make many of these.
Then, place these strips one on top of the other, as much as is needed for the eye. Then, bind them tightly with a strong cloth.  Reapply the cupping glass, cupping gently, without specific rules.  Then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bound for one day and one night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened again.  Then, remove the bandage after one day and one night, as we said. If you see the eye has returned to its place, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the bandage, the strips, the tightening, and the cupping until the eye returns to its proper position.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cutting the conjunctiva.
If a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva, protruding and appearing outside the eyelids like a membrane, spoiling the appearance of the person, and you wish to cut it, proceed as I describe.
Insert a needle into the base of the conjunctiva from below to above, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the tear, penetrating it, and leaving the first needle as it is.
Then cut the point where the doubled thread bends, tie part of the conjunctiva around the needle and tighten it well.  Then remove the needle and apply a cloth soaked in egg white to the eye, leaving it until the threads and the conjunctiva fall out.  Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  The conjunctiva can also be tightened from all sides with a circular piece of cloth to increase the eye's protrusion well, then use a thin scalpel to reach the depth of the eye, allowing the white moisture to drain, and the eye to subside and wilt in its position.  Then tighten it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of fistula.
This ailment, called fistula, is a fluid accumulation in the eye, resembling water that has descended but without the actual water.  The method of treatment involves having the patient sit upright on a chair, then holding their head with both hands at the sides.
And move it until you see the fluid descend to the bottom with your eyes, then hold it firm, so that it does not move, and the light emerges. The patient will then see objects as they used to. If the fluid does not descend to the bottom, we know it is water. If the fluid does not descend as described, then...
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision just above the cornea, where the adhering substance meets the cornea at the limbus, so that the matter may be expelled.
Then, after the expulsion, instill warm water, mixed with honey or water boiled with licorice and honey, into the eye, and then continue the treatment until healing occurs.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already described the types of water for instillation in detail.  Follow those descriptions for the proper type.  Then, the patient should sit before you, squatting facing the light, near the sun.
Bind his/her healthy eye tightly, and then raise the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if the affected eye is the left eye, or with your right hand if the affected eye is the right eye.
Then, place the tip of the cupping vessel close to the limbus, at the thickness of a date pit, on the white of the eye near the smaller corner.  Then, push the cupping vessel firmly, while rotating it in your hand, until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the cupping vessel has reached an empty space.
The depth of the cupping vessel should be equal to the distance from the child's eyelid to the edge of the iris. The copper vessel will be seen by the observer, due to the clarity of the cornea, as though in the same place as the eye.  Then, move the cupping vessel upward to the location of the water.
Then press it downwards repeatedly.  If the water descends at once, the patient will see what has been revealed to his/her sight at once, and the cupping vessel is in the eye. Then, the patient rests for a little while. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cupping vessel.
If it settles and does not rise, remove the cupping vessel gently, twisting your hand slightly. Then, dissolve some clean, refined salt in water and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then, apply moistened lint or wool to the entire outside of the eye.
With rose ointment and egg white, and bandage the healthy eye.  As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it. If the cupping vessel does not enter the eye due to its hardness (because some people have very hard eyes). One should take the lancet called the *al-bayrid*, which has this form:
Pierce only the adhered tissue with it, and do not overdo the piercing. Instead, lightly puncture the cupping vessel in a delicate place. Then introduce the cupping vessel as we have described. Once your work is complete, prepare a secure, darkened room for the patient to lie in on their back.
He should refrain from all movements and coughing.  His food should be easily digestible and he must not move his head at all, left or right. The bandage should remain in place for three days. Then, in that dark room, the bandage is removed, and his eyesight is tested.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage for seven days.  This should not be done during the treatment or after the cupping procedure.  Instead, avoid doing this until the moisture has receded quickly from intensive sight.
If a hot swelling appears, the bandage must be removed before the seventh day and treated to relieve the swelling.  Once the swelling subsides, the eyesight can be released, and a cloth is placed over the face, with something placed beneath to train the eyesight, for several days in the dark room. Then...
He gradually leaves the room and goes about his affairs.  Know that the learner cannot dispense with observing the procedure multiple times before undertaking the work. I have heard from some Iraqis that they make a cupping vessel in Iraq that draws out water.
I have not seen anyone in our country do this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients.  It may be a recent innovation.  Here are the images of various types of cupping vessels for their understanding.
They are made of copper, specifically, and their tip is precisely this thin, triangular, sharp-pointed shape.
Chapter Twenty-four.
In treating flesh growing in the nose.
Various excess fleshy growths may sprout in the nose; some resemble scorpions with many legs, others are a cancerous, hardened, dark-colored flesh, and some are soft and not dark-colored flesh.
For those fleshy growths that are soft and not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, open their nostril, and insert a hook into the flesh. Then pull it out, and with a gentle, sharp scalpel, cut off what the hook has grasped from one side.
So that you know all the flesh has gone; if any remains that you cannot cut off, then carefully remove the remaining portion with one of the gentler tools, so that nothing of it remains. If bleeding is excessive or a hot tumor develops.
If it is a malignant tumor, act quickly and cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all flesh is gone. Then, after cutting, place vinegar and water or some other drink in the nose. If the nose opens and moisture flows into the throat.
Know then that he is cured. If the moisture does not drain properly, know that there is a fleshy growth inside, on the top of the porous bones, which the cutting instrument did not reach.  In that case, a thick linen thread should be taken.
Make many knots on it, placing each knot a finger's width or less apart. The patient should maneuver the end of the thread into their nose with a rounded object, or whatever means available, shaping it like a button. Pull on the thread to reach the nostril.
It will come out through the throat.  Children often do something similar in schools.  It is easy for whoever wants to do it. Then, bring together the two ends of the thread – the one that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then, use the knots in the thread to separate the flesh.  Do this until you are certain the flesh has been cut by the thread knots.  Then, remove the thread, and place the thread in the nose after cleaning the blood.  Soak it in Egyptian ointment for three days or longer.
Until the ointment consumes all remaining flesh. Then, finally, a lead tube will be placed in the nose for some days until healed. If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is an image of the syringe used to drip ointments and medications into the nose.
A silver or brass vessel, similar to a small lamp, with an open container for ointments and a channel for its passage.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the syringe head flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when heating ointments or any other flowing substances.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing and enlarging over time, until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and remove the entire wart. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medication which serves as a cautery. If it is delayed and has grown large, consider if it is hard, stony, dark-coloured, and insensitive.
Do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen that cutting this tumor has brought great harm to the patient. If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-coloured, and you see that it can be completely cut, then do so. Empty the ulcer and cut it off without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that if a separation occurs in any of these cartilages, treatment is rarely successful, except in some individuals.
Therefore, if one of these things occurs, examine if the wound is fresh and bloody.  If so, bring together the edges of the wound with stitches and treat it until healed.  If the separation has caused the edges to detach and each part is sound, then it is necessary to remove the skin from each part, to the depth of its visible thickness, until it bleeds.
Then bring the edges together with stitches, tighten them, and sprinkle powdered asafoetida and frankincense on them. Place a poultice of palm ointment, or other healing ointment, on top. Leave it tied for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medication and leave it until the stitches come out by themselves. Afterward, treat it with ointment until healed. The method of stitching is to bring together the separated parts either with needles, as described in the suturing of the abdomen, or with thread, as previously explained.
The Twenty-seventh Chapter.
On removing the knots that appear on the lips.
Small, hard tumors, resembling some carbuncles and others smaller or larger, may appear inside the lips of many people.  It is necessary to turn back the lip, and to cut open each knot and remove it with a forceps.
Cut it from every side, then fill the wound with powdered alum until bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt.  Treat the affected areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
The Twenty-eighth Chapter.
On cutting excess flesh from the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *apōls*.  It should be grasped with forceps or a curette, cut at its root, and allowed to bleed.  Then, powdered alum should be applied to the area.
Or one of the astringent powders, dried. If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does. Cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
The Twenty-ninth Chapter.
On scraping teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  They may darken, yellow, or turn green, and such decay can extend to the gums, thus disfiguring the teeth.
The patient should sit before you, head resting on your lap, and scrape the tooth, or teeth, that show scales and a sand-like substance, until nothing of it remains.  Likewise, treat the dark, green, and yellow discoloration, and anything similar, until it is cleaned away.
If the first scraping removes everything, then do not repeat the scraping. Otherwise, scrape the tooth again, a second, and third time, until the desired result is achieved.  Know that the tooth requires various scrapers, diverse in shape, according to what your task demands.
Because the scraper used for scraping inside the tooth is different from the scraper used for scraping outside, and the one used for scraping between the teeth is different again.  These are various types of scrapers, all of which should be available to you.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should treat the toothache with all possible means, and delay tooth extraction. For it is a noble substance and will not be replaced if extracted, so only extract it as a last resort.
Therefore, if the patient is determined to have the tooth extracted, you must first ascertain that the tooth is indeed painful. Often, the patient is deceived by the pain and believes it is in a healthy tooth, extracting it, only for the pain to persist until the diseased tooth is extracted. We have seen this repeated by the actions of barbers.
If you are certain the pain is in the specific tooth, then you should cut around the tooth with a strong scalpel, so as to detach the gum on all sides. Then you must move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps, little by little, to loosen it.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees, ensuring they are immobile.  Then pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments and insert it underneath from all sides, gently and repeatedly moving it as before.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, you should fill the cavity with a cloth and seal it tightly with a thin, smooth instrument, to prevent it from breaking when you pull on it with forceps.  You should meticulously cut around the gum from all sides, thoroughly.
Use caution so as not to break it, lest a piece remain, causing the patient greater harm than the initial pain.  Avoid the methods of unskilled barbers who pull teeth without using the procedures we have described.
They often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots, all or some of them. Or, the tooth might be pulled out with pieces of the jawbone, as we have witnessed repeatedly.  Then, after extraction, the patient should rinse the area with a drink, vinegar, or salt.  If bleeding occurs, which often happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if the alum is ineffective, use the delicate forceps, which first move the tooth.  These forceps have long handles, short grips, and a thick build so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth. This is the image of large forceps:.
The handles should be thick, so that when grasped, they do not give way or bend.  The tips should be short.  Made of Indian iron or hardened steel, the tips should be firmly joined, with teeth that interlock tightly.
The tips can also be shaped like a file, and thus provide strong control.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On extracting the roots of teeth and removing fractured jawbones.
If, after extracting a tooth, a root remains broken off, you should apply a cotton ball soaked in oil for a day or two to allow the area to relax, then use a probe or forceps. Those whose tips resemble the mouth of a bird called a bularge; this is an illustration of the forceps.
If it proves difficult to extract with these forceps, a burr-like or chisel-like instrument should be used to create an opening and expose the entire flesh with a probe, then introduce a small lever-like instrument of this type:
Short, slightly thick, and not tapered so as not to break; if the root is extracted this way, otherwise, use the following instruments, the first of which is triangular and somewhat thick.
Another delicate triangular image.
We may also use this two-pronged instrument, also pictured here.
And with other instruments and tools, as previously mentioned in the inventory of teeth, we may also use this instrument resembling a large fishhook, whose image is this.
Triangular, bent, with a slight thickness to prevent breakage, and not tapered. Know that dental instruments are numerous, as are other instruments, almost countless.  A skillful craftsman may invent instruments for himself according to the indications of the work and the diseases themselves.
Because some diseases, due to their varied types, were not given instruments by those who came before, if a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth breaks or decays, probe it in its location with a suitable instrument from among these instruments and forceps mentioned for extracting roots.
We use a file, whose image is this.
It should have some slight thickness to grip the bone firmly so that it does not slip out until the bone is extracted.  We treat the area with suitable medications. If the bone has a slight decay, remove the decay and discoloration until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-two.
In the eruption of teeth that grow over others.
Teeth that erupt out of their natural order disfigure the appearance, especially in women and slaves.  If a tooth has erupted behind another tooth, one should examine it.
If it cannot be moved forward or backward, extract it.  If it is attached to another tooth, cut it off with this instrument, which is pictured as follows:
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp Indian steel.  Cut it out over several days, due to the tooth's hardness and to prevent disturbing other teeth.  If it is protruding and firmly rooted, cool it with an Indian file, which has this image.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with a very finely engraved surface, resembling the file used to make needles.  Use it to cool the tooth gradually over many days, gently, to avoid dislodging it and causing it to fall out.  Then, finally smooth and scrape it with other scrapers.
If a tooth has broken, and it hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the fracture disappears, it becomes smooth, and no longer hurts the tongue or impairs speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In fastening loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If old teeth become loose and displaced due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat anything because they fear the teeth will fall out, and treatment with astringent remedies has been ineffective.
The remedy is to tie them with a gold or silver thread; gold is preferable because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, whereas gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of intermediate thickness and thinness, suitable for the space between the teeth.
The method of fastening is to take the thread, insert its bend between the sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound tooth on the other side.
Then, reweave it back to the starting side, applying gentle and judicious tension so that the teeth remain completely stable. Tighten the thread at the tooth roots to prevent it from slipping, then trim the excess thread ends with scissors, gathering and twisting them together with a thread, concealing them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not harmed. Then, leave it like that, tightened, as long as it lasts. If it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread; and thus he can enjoy it for a lifetime. This is the appearance of the teeth and the method of fastening, in two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single or two teeth can sometimes be repositioned in their place after falling out, fixed as we described, and remain.  However, only a skilled artisan can do this.  They may carve bone from an ox, shaping it like a tooth, and place it where the tooth was lost. And tightens it, as we said, so that it remains and is enjoyed.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that is located beneath the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament beneath the tongue may be natural, present at birth, or accidental, from a wound that has healed.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, hold their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, cut this nerve ligament transversely so that the tongue is freed from its constraint. If it has some firmness and complexity, and this is from a healed wound, use a needle and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Beware of cutting too deeply into the flesh, severing an artery and causing bleeding.  Afterwards, the patient should rinse their mouth with rosewater or vinegar and cold water. Then, place a linen wick under the tongue for the patient to hold in place each night.
To prevent re-adhesion. If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area. If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area with a suitable lens cautery. Then treat it with other remedies until healed.
Chapter Thirty-five.
Removing the toad generated under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small toad may form under the tongue, hindering its normal function, and possibly growing to fill the mouth.  To treat it, open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the tumor.
If it appears dark-colored or black and hard, with no sensation for the patient, do not attempt to treat it, as it is cancer.  If it leans toward whiteness and contains moisture, use a forceps to pierce and split it with a gentle scalpel, and remove it from all sides.
If bleeding occurs during your procedure, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until complete removal, after which the patient should gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with further appropriate therapy until recovery.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any tumors that grow in the throat may present glands inside the throat resembling those that appear externally, called tonsils.  If treated as described in the classification, but do not heal.
If the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not use the iron.  If it is red in color and thick in origin, do not use the iron either, for fear of blood loss.  Instead, allow it to mature, either by applying a poultice or allowing it to burst of its own accord.
If it is white in color, round, and has a thin origin, then it is the one that should be cut.  Before operating, observe whether the hot inflammation has subsided completely or if some reduction has occurred.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head resting on your lap, mouth open, and take a servant before you, pressing the patient's tongue downward with a tool, its shape is as follows:
Made of silver or bronze, thin like a knife.  If you press the tongue with this and the tumor is clear to your sight, then take a hook and pierce it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible without pulling out any of the membranes.
Then, cut it with an instrument, its shape resembling scissors, but with both ends curved, each having a sharp edge facing the other.  Made of Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is unavailable, then cut it with a scalpel, its shape is as follows:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after the cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, he should gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or rue leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled until the bleeding stops.  Then treat it until it heals.  Other growths may appear in the throat besides the tonsils, and these are also cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a growth that had developed inside her throat, resembling a small tumor, almost blocking her throat.  She was breathing through a narrow passageway, and it had prevented her from eating or drinking.
She was close to death if she had lived another day or two.  Two branches had grown from the tumor, emerging from the nostrils.  I acted quickly, piercing one with a hook, and pulling it out.  A usable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I located it in the nostril.  I then did the same to what protruded from the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth, pressed her tongue down, and inserted the hook into the tumor itself, cutting off some of it.  Only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened up, and she immediately drank water and ate.  We continued to cut away at that tumor repeatedly over a long period.  The tumor would regenerate after each cutting until the process lasted a long time for both of us.
I cauterized the tumor inside her throat, which stopped its growth.  Then I left the area and did not know what God did for her after I departed.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
In the cutting of a tumor of the larynx.
If a tumor, called a 'ʿunba', descends into the larynx and swells, being white and oblong, it is called a 'ʿamud'; if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an 'ʿunba'. If treated with the methods described in the classification, and the treatment is unsuccessful.
If the inflamed tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be cut.  If it is a solid, round mass with no length, and is dark red, dusky, black, or insensitive, then cutting should be avoided.
There is danger for the patient, so if you see it with the characteristics mentioned—its whiteness and length—the patient should sit facing the sun, the tongue pressed down with the instrument previously described, and then the snare should be inserted into the ʿunba.
Pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the section on cutting the tonsils.  Only the part exceeding the natural limit should be cut, no more; for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
After the cutting, use the treatment described for the tonsils and treat it until it is healed. If the patient is reluctant to have it cut, use the cauterization method without fear or caution. The cauterization is done using a sharp medicine; place the patient's head on your lap.
Then, press the patient's tongue down with the instrument mentioned, then take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with quicklime (unquenched), making a paste neither thick nor thin. Fill the cavity of this instrument; this is its shape:
Its tip, where you place the medicine, should have a depression like that of a date-pitting spoon. Place the instrument with the medicine on the throat itself, with the patient lying on his side so that saliva flows out of his mouth from inside his throat, to prevent anything from flowing back down into the throat and harming him.
Then hold the medicine in your hand, pressing it against the throat for about half an hour until you see it has blackened and the burning sensation of the medicine subsides.  Alternatively, you can take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the tip of a date-pitting spoon, and soak it in the medicine.
Insert the spoon with the cotton into the tube from above so that the cotton adheres to the throat.  Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired level of cauterization of the ulcer.  Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After the cauterization, wipe the area around the ulcer with cotton soaked in ghee, removing any remaining medicine. Then, the patient should rinse with cold water, and be treated externally with lotions and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and less frightening.
The throat can also be treated with gentler methods than cauterization or cutting, using incense in this manner:  Take frankincense, saffron, savory, bistort, wormwood, chamomile, and rue, or similar herbs.  Gather them all or some of them in a pot and soak them in vinegar.
Bring it to a boil, with the pot covered with clay of wisdom.  A hollowed-out instrument, made of silver or copper, should have a hole in the center of the lid.
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the throat through the tube, causing the throat to contract.  Repeat this several times until it withers.  Take care not to use this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Instead, it should be done when the inflamed swelling has subsided.  If this instrument is not available, take a hollow reed and place an egg shell at its end to prevent the patient's mouth from burning.
Because the egg shell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth.  This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
For removing thorns and other foreign objects lodged in the throat.
Often a bone or fishbone, or something similar, becomes lodged in the throat.  It is necessary to remove any visible object that can be seen by the eye, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in the light of the sun.
To make clear what is in the throat.  If something is hidden and not visible in the throat, it is necessary to induce vomiting in the patient before their food is digested in the stomach.  Perhaps the lodged object will be expelled by vomiting, or the patient will swallow a piece of lint or a root of a thistle.
Or swallow a piece of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a thread, then have them swallow it.  If it reaches the location of the thorn, pull the thread quickly.  Do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone adheres to it and is removed. If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this form:
It should be slightly thicker than a lead rod.  At its tip, there is a bend, and the patient will gently insert it into their throat, with their head raised upwards, taking care not to touch their throat to avoid coughing and thereby dislodging the bone or thorn.  Or the physician can insert it with their hand.
The patient inserting it is better, as they know the location of the lodged object.  They push it downwards or pull their hand with the instrument upwards,  all according to what is possible for them until it is removed.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
Removing a leech lodged in the throat.
If treatment with the remedies described in the section on medication is unsuccessful, then examine the patient's throat in the sunlight after pressing down their tongue with the instrument described for you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If this doesn't work, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then introduce a heated iron into the tube.
Repeat this several times.  The patient should abstain from drinking all day.  Then, the patient should take a vessel filled with cool water, open their mouth over it, rinse their mouth without swallowing a drop, and move the water around with their hand from time to time.
The leech will fall off if it feels the water. If it does not come out with the methods described, fumigate the throat with urine and with halit (a substance) using the instrument described in the fumigation of the throat. Repeat this several times, and the leech will fall off.
The procedure for fumigation is to take a vessel containing embers heated by fire, the vessel covered with a lid having a hole in the center.  Place the end of the instrument in this hole. Then, place the fumigant in the vessel and have the patient place their mouth at the end of the tube, closing their mouth to keep the smoke from escaping, until it is known that the smoke has reached the throat.
The leech will fall off if it is affected in this way. If it does not fall off, the fumigation is repeated several times. The patient should endure thirst and eat salty foods and garlic, but not drink water.  The leech must come out with this procedure. This is the image of the instrument for pulling the leech from the throat if it is observed.
It resembles tongs, as you see, except that it has this curve that enters the throat, and its tips resemble a bird's mouth.  They have the roughness of a file, so if they grasp something, they will not let go.
Another instrument is used to compress the tongue. It is a wide ring of iron, sized to fit the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth, standing on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will. The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
It will discuss the lancing and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many diverse types, as will be described individually in this book.  They differ in their lancing and incision in two ways: one is based on the type of tumor itself and the humors it contains.
The second type is determined by the location of the tumor within the body, for a tumor occurring in the head is different from a tumor in the buttocks, and a tumor in a fleshy area differs from one in a joint.  Each has its own surgical procedure.
Some tumors should not be lanced until the pus is fully ripe and mature. Others, however, should be lanced while still unripe, such as tumors close to the joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition worsens until it causes decay around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing paralysis of the limb, or the tumor might be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay lancing it until it matures, you will harm the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, you should lance it while still unripe.  A tumor unripe and near the buttocks should be lanced, lest the suppuration spread and penetrate into the rectum, becoming a fistula.
It will become incurable. You must know when to lance tumors that are fully ripe: when the tumor's pain subsides, the fever is gone, the redness and throbbing diminish, the tumor's head softens, and other symptoms abate.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, for easier drainage downwards, or at the thinnest, most prominent part.  If the tumors are on the arms or legs, the incision should follow the length of the limb.
The incision should be made over muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, in short, over all straight, unyielding areas.  For areas that bend, the incision should follow the contour of the area. If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it's best to avoid lancing.
Until its maturity is complete, as we have said. If you lance it before that, the pus will drain excessively, causing much harm and filth, and perhaps its edges and cavity will harden. Some tumors may need to be lanced across the body, when necessary, or according to what the limb requires.
Small tumors should be lanced once, while large tumors should be lanced widely or with many incisions, according to the tumor's size.  Some tumors may require skin excision or cutting, if they have become like a rag.
Some have, to a degree, died, like what happens frequently with boils and abscesses.  Some are incised in a three-angled manner, and some are cut away, like a rue leaf, similar to a groin tumor.
Some use a round incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar cuts.  For tumors that lack a head, such as flat, smooth tumors, a simple incision should suffice. If the tumor is large, has accumulated for a long time, and has been lanced, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, then apply pressure to the tumor for another day, then drain some more pus.  Repeat this gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, pregnant, a young child, or elderly, the animal spirit frequently weakens with the sudden discharge of pus.  The patient may die without your awareness, so be very cautious.
After lancing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and examine it. If the tumor wound is small or the incision simple, use a bandage of linen or worn cotton. If the tumor is large and the incisions numerous,
You should insert a bandage into each incision until some of them touch. If the tumor has penetrated or broken through the skin, you should fill it with worn cotton or linen strips, without moisture, and bandage it for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until healed. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the treatment and remedies described in many places in this book and in the sections. If it is winter and the tumor area is richly supplied with nerves, you should wet the bandages with warm drink and oil.
And place them on the area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply the bandages soaked in water and oil, or in cool drink and oil, until, as we said, the third day, then you should dissolve the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar treatments until healed. This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  As for detailed methods, each tumor type's treatment is described in a summarized and categorized manner.
Chapter Forty-one.
On the treatment of tumors appearing on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, which are a type of pustule, containing pockets like a chicken's egg sac.  There are many varieties, some fatty, some containing moisture like mud.
Some contain moisture like porridge and stew, and similar substances.  Some are hardened and solid.  All are safe to incise and remove, unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
And the procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument, the image of which will come after this, called the *midas*, so as to know what they contain.  If it contains moisture, incise it lengthwise with a simple incision in this fashion:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~.  If the moisture has drained, dissect the sac that contained the moisture, and cut it all away, leaving nothing at all; for often it returns if any remains.
Then dip a piece of cotton in the astringent ointment, if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day, as it will consume any remaining sac.  Then, repeat the application of cotton with astringent, two or three times, if needed.
Until you are certain that nothing remains of the sac. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals.  If the swelling contains fatty matter, incise it in this fashion:
Make a cross-shaped incision, and place the forceps on the edges of the wound.  Dissecting from every side, exert all your effort in removing the peritoneum that contains it.  If an artery obstructs you, do what I have described to you before.  And do the same for the swelling, if it is hardened, from the incision.
The treatment itself is as I have described.  The incision on a hardened swelling is easier, because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people have absolutely no moisture.  This is because I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the swelling as hard as solid stone, rough, white, and no one could break it, even if one threw it, it would only chip.  And the other swellings that occur in the head, unlike this one, such as the swellings that appear on the heads of children...
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision in each, and position the flaps from below to facilitate the flow of matter downward. Then treat it with appropriate remedies.
Chapter Forty-two.
Concerning the incision on swine that often appear in the neck:  These swellings often appear in the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins.  They may appear on the rest of the body.  Sometimes a single one appears in the neck, sometimes multiple ones, and some develop from others.
Each such swelling has its own enclosed membrane, as with swellings and tumors of the head, as we described. There are many types of these swellings, some stony, some containing moisture, and some malignant, unresponsive to treatment.
Of those I have seen, some were favorably presented to the touch. Their appearance was near the color of skin, movable in all directions, not attached to the neck's nerve, veins, or arteries, and not deeply embedded. Therefore, a simple incision should be made from top to bottom of the body, in this manner, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
Peel it from all sides and stretch the skin flaps with a hook or two, or many hooks if necessary, as we said for tumors of the head. Remove it little by little, taking care not to sever a vein or nerve. The scalpel should not be overly sharp.
So that your hand does not increase the cutting or cause the patient distress by cutting what is not necessary. If you sever a vein or artery and this hinders your work, place powdered astringent or some of the substances which stop bleeding into the wound.
Tighten the wound and leave it until the swelling subsides, the wound relaxes, and it begins to fester. The bleeding will then stop. Return to your work until finished. Then examine with your index finger if any smaller splinters remain, cutting and removing them.
If a large vein is present at the base of the splinter, it should not be cut from its root. Instead, it should be tied with a strong doubled thread, left to fall out of itself without harm, and then the wound should be filled with used cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then treated.
If the entire splinter is removed, the edges of the wound should be brought together and stitched immediately, after making sure there is no remaining fragment. If you see that a fragment of the splinter's base remains, however.
