You should aim to cut at the root and base of the splinters and use the sutures and methods we previously described. For splinters containing moisture, apply a simple poultice where you see signs of their ripening, placing the poultice below as we have mentioned.
Then, after the poultice, apply Egyptian ointment or similar to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with flesh-growing ointments until healed.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On the incision of the throat for a tumor that arises inside the throat.
The ancients have described this throat incision, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it.  This agrees with their words.  However, those with a sore throat should avoid throat incision, as it will not benefit them.
In order that all tumors and the lungs may be diseased, and those who have a burning tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no problem with the windpipe, then it is necessary to use a throat incision to escape the damage that occurs from suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we should make an incision in the throat under three rings of the windpipe or four small horizontal incisions between two rings, as much as an incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the physician is timid, then he should stretch the throat skin with a hook, and then cut the skin until when he reaches the windpipe, he should avoid blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the peritoneum that we have described, and he should ascertain the incision of the windpipe from the phlegm that comes out of it along with what is being punctured and from the cessation of the voice.  Leave the wound open for a time, and when the time during which suffocation was feared has passed, bring the edges of the wound together from the skin.
And he should suture it alone, without the cartilage.  Then the medicines that promote the growth of flesh should be used until it heals.  The author of this book said, the explanation of this statement that we have related is that, if they see the patient's throat blocked by one of these tumors that have been mentioned.
When the patient is close to death, and he is distressed at the prospect of death, they went to cut the throat so that the patient could breathe through the wound and be saved from death.  But they were ordered to leave the wound open until the course of the illness is completed, and its duration is three days or similar.
At that point, they ordered the wound to be sewn up and treated until it healed.  What I saw with my own eyes was that a servant took a knife and inflicted a cut on her throat, cutting through some of the windpipe.  I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening, as a slaughtered animal weakens.  I uncovered the wound.
I found the blood that came from the wound to be slight, so I was certain that she had not cut a vein or an artery.  Air was escaping from the wound.  I quickly sutured and treated the wound until it healed.  The servant experienced nothing except a slight hoarseness in her voice, nothing more.
And after a few days, she returned to her best condition.  Therefore, we say that cutting the throat does not involve any danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision of the swelling that appears on the larynx from the outside, and is called the swelling of the larynx.
This swelling, called the swelling of the larynx, is a large swelling the color of the body.  It is common in women and comes in two types.
Either it is natural or incidental. The natural one has no remedy. The incidental one is of two types: one resembling fatty tumors, and the other resembling a swelling arising from arterial entanglement.  Incising it is dangerous. Therefore, one should not use iron at all, unless it is small. If, upon probing and examining it with a probe, it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any vessels, then incise it as one would incise a tumor. And remove it with the sac it is in if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it, then treat the area with appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-five.
On incising tumors of various types.
Tumors are numerous; all their types have been described in the classification.  Here, we must explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it is problematic.  I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and terrible pain until the boiling of the pus subsides. And when the pus matures, the fever and intensity subside.  A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain; it is contained within a serous sac, a specific casing, and is the same color as the body.  It begins like a boil and becomes like a swelling, larger or smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture; and the colors of moisture are many, as stated in the classification.  Therefore, when you are about to treat a tumor, you must first probe and examine it with the instrument called a 'probe', as its image will be shown in the following chapter.
Assess the size of the tumor, then withdraw the probe and observe what follows in its wake. If a flowing moisture of any color emerges, make a simple incision, as mentioned in other tumors. If no moisture follows the probe, know that it is fatty.
Incise it with a firm incision in this manner, as I have taught you, and suspend it by tongs. Gently separate the skin from all sides, and if possible, carefully remove the sac containing the tumor intact.
If the sac breaks during the procedure, and you are unable to remove it intact, which often happens, remove it piece by piece until nothing remains, for if any part of it remains, small or large, the tumor will recur in most cases.
If you are unsuccessful and a small portion remains, cauterize the wound with sharp cauterizing substances after completion of the procedure, and tighten the wound. Apply something to soothe the inflamed tumor and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the tumor is large, suture its edges and treat it with a healing agent.
If a bleeding vein, whether pulsating or not, appears, immediately apply powdered alum to the affected area and keep it firmly pressed for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the blood stops flowing.  Then return to removing any remaining part of the tumor.

The forty-seventh chapter.
On the treatment of the male breast.
The breast of some men, resembling that of women, may swell at puberty until it resembles a woman's breast, remaining swollen and unsightly.  One who dislikes this should make an incision on the breast in the shape of a crescent moon, like this.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~. Then, remove all fat.  Then, fill the wound with the medicated ointment.  Then, stitch together the edges of the wound and treat it until healed.  If the breast sags and relaxes because of its bone structure, as happens with women,
Then, you should make incisions on its upper sides in the shape of two crescent moons, each touching the other at its ends so that the larger line surrounds the smaller one, in this fashion. From line ~B~ to line ~C~, then the skin between the incisions is peeled away, the fat is removed, and the mentioned sutures, cauterization, and any necessary treatment are used until healed.
If the incision does not encompass the necessary amount of tissue, either because of the patient's distress or bleeding, then the wound should be filled with cotton dipped in caustic ointment and left until it consumes any remaining fat, then treated until healed.
Chapter Forty-eight.

Chapter Forty-nine.
Incising the tumor which appears near an artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often results.  Likewise, a vein can also cause swelling and a tumor.
The signs by which one knows if the swelling and puffiness are from an artery or a vein: if from an artery, the tumor is oblong, confined deep within the body, and when you press the tumor with your finger, you feel a crackling sensation.
And that from a vein, the tumor is round, on the surface of the body.  Incisions on these tumors are dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other parts of the body, and if they are quite large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those also in the extremities or head should be avoided.  For those that are swellings of the arterial mouth, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle beneath it and pierce it to the other side and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I explained to you in the ligation of the arteries in the neck, then pierce with a lancet the area between the ligaments until all the blood is expelled.
The swelling will subside, and then use the treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off, then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is from a vein rupture, you should hold as much of the swelling with the skin as possible with your hand, then insert a needle below the area you held with your hand and pierce it, with a doubled thread in it, until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the swelling tightly with it, according to what I told you about tying the bandage on both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire swelling at the intersection of the first needle.
Tie your threads in four places, then cut the swelling in the middle until what is inside comes out, then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied, then apply a compress soaked in a potion and oil to it. Then use the treatment that involves twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
Concerning the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
Just as swelling occurs in the artery and vein, so too does swelling occur in the nerve if a blow occurs or from excessive fatigue, or the like. This is more often the case in the wrists and ankles.
And in every place where joints move, it is a hard swelling, similar in color to the rest of the body.  It is often painless. If pressed firmly, the patient feels a sensation like numbness.  The swelling is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, not forward or backward.  Any swelling in the joints should not be treated with iron, as it might cause a wound.  What is in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, hold it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook, scrape it, then remove it and stitch the wound. Treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-one.
Concerning the removal of warts that appear on the abdomen.
Warts, called fungous because of their resemblance to fungi, often appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people.  Their base is thin, their head thick, and sometimes their edges become inflamed.  They can be small or very large.
I once observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling fungi in appearance.  Their thin, whitish bases were inflamed and cracked, with moisture constantly dripping from them. I removed them; the weight of one was approximately eighteen ounces and the other approximately six ounces.
When removing them, if the lesion is moist and the wart is whitish, moist, and thin-based, cut it with a broad scalpel. Have cautery readily available, as considerable bleeding often occurs during excision.
If bleeding becomes excessive, cauterize it. If the patient is fearful of cutting with metal, take a strong lead thread and tightly bind the wart (with these characteristics). Leave it for two days, then increase the tension of the lead thread; continue tightening the lead.
As the lead tightens, the wart will eventually break off and fall away on its own, without further intervention.  If the wart has a thick base, there is some risk and uncertainty in removing it completely, particularly if it is on the abdomen.  However, it may be possible to remove half or part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals. Beware of removing a wart that is dark-colored, has little sensitivity, and a dull appearance; it could be a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-two.
In the treatment of umbilical tumors.
Umbilical tumors may arise from various causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen, allowing bowel or other viscera to protrude, as in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from accumulated gas.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the protrusion of viscera, the tumor will be the same color as the body, soft and painless, and of a varying shape. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestines, its position will be even more varied than described.  If you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then reappear, and there might be rumbling. It often enlarges greatly when entering a bath or experiencing strenuous exertion.  If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and does not increase or decrease in size, then if it is due to blood, the tumor will show a bluish/dark color in addition to these symptoms.  If it is due to a growth of flesh, the tumor will be hard and immobile, and consistent in size.
If it is due to gas, the touch will be soft.  In this case, one should refrain from treatment, for there is danger and deception, as I have explained in the chapter where I discussed tumors arising from arteries and veins.
If the protrusion of the navel is due to intestines or viscera, you should instruct the patient to hold still and stand upright, then carefully trace the outline around the entire navel with a stylus. Then, you should instruct him to lie on his back before you.  Then, use a wide scalpel to make a circular incision around the navel at the location marked with the stylus. Then, lift the center of the tumor upward with a large hook, and tightly tie the incision site with strong thread or fine silk.
The knot of the ligature should be a figure-of-eight.  Then, open the center of the drawn-up tumor above the ligature, insert your index finger, and probe for the intestines. If you find that the intestines are caught in the ligature, loosen the figure-of-eight knot and push the intestines back into the abdomen. If you find the viscera, lift them with the hook.
And cut off the excess. If an artery or vein intervenes, tie it off.  Return to your work.  Take two needles and thread strong thread through them. Insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, anchoring them firmly. Then, tie the tumor in four places to the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the drawn-up flesh becomes gangrenous and falls off on its own, or cut it away once it thins and becomes gangrenous.  Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the protrusion of the navel is from overgrown flesh.
Or if it is from moisture, you should cauterize the tumor, as we have said, and remove the blood or moisture you find there. Then, treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have previously mentioned the types of cancer and how to treat them with medicines, warning against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate.  We have also described cancer originating in the uterus and warned against its treatment.
The ancients stated that when cancer is in a location where it can be completely excised, such as cancer in the breast or the thigh and similar easily removable organs, especially if it is small and in its early stages, it should be removed completely. However, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me who succeeded in doing so.  If it is located in a site that is accessible, as we have said, proceed to draw out the black fluid repeatedly, then dissect it if it is full of blood vessels.
Then apply a compress that enables the work to proceed, and insert the appropriate hooks into the cancer, then cut around it from all sides including the skin thoroughly, until nothing of its roots remains.  Allow the blood to flow, do not cut it quickly.
Instead, squeeze the affected areas and draw out all the thick blood with your hand or any suitable instruments. If, during the procedure, excessive bleeding occurs due to an artery or vein being cut, constrict the affected area until the bleeding stops, and then continue treatment until healing occurs.

Chapter fifty-seven.
On the purification of boys and treatment of their errors.
Circumcision is nothing more than the separation of connection, like other surgical procedures. However, since it is an action of our own volition, and specifically used on boys, the best possible procedure must be implemented.

Chapter Fifty-eight.

Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.
We have previously described the types of stones, their treatment with medications, and the differences between kidney stones and bladder stones, along with the signs of each.  We have also discussed stones requiring manual removal or incision.

Sand is also visible, and they scratch and play with their penis.  It often hangs down, and the anus often protrudes in many cases.  Children often recover from this up to the age of fourteen; it is more difficult in the elderly, and in young adults it is intermediate.
The larger the stones, the easier the treatment; the smaller ones, the opposite.  When we come to treatment, first the patient should be given an enema to remove all the impurities in his intestines, as this may obstruct the examination of the stones.
Then the patient is taken by the legs and shaken downward to bring the stone down to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps repeatedly from a high place.  Then he is seated upright before you, hands under his thighs, so the bladder is tilted downwards, and then it is examined.

If it is a fully grown boy, you introduce it into his anus and search for the stone until, when it is under your finger, you move it a little at a time to the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you want to incise.

Incise between the anus and the perineum, not in the middle, but to the side of the left groin. The incision should be over the stone itself. Your finger should be in the anus, pressing it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide from the outside and narrow from the inside, to the extent possible for the stone to exit.
Do not enlarge the incision too much; perhaps the finger in the anus will press the stone during the incision, causing it to exit without difficulty.  Be aware that some stones have angles and edges, making their removal difficult.
Some are smooth, like a nut, and round, making removal easy; but those with angles and edges require a slightly larger incision.  If it still does not come out, you should devise a means to remove it, perhaps by grasping it with a firm forceps, the tip of which is like a rasp.
To grip the stone and not lose it; or to insert a delicate, curved instrument under it.  If you cannot do this, widen the incision slightly.  If much blood is present, cauterize it with a cautery.
If there is more than one stone, first push the largest one toward the bladder opening, then make an incision on it.  Then push the smaller ones out afterward.  Do the same if there are more than two.  If the stone is very large, it would be foolish to make a very large incision, as this exposes the patient to one of two outcomes.
Either he will die or he will have constant dribbling of urine, because the wound will never heal properly. But try to push it out; or devise a means to break it with forceps to remove it in pieces.
When you are finished, treat the wound with kender, sabr, shiyan, and stitches. Place a dressing soaked in oil and a drink, or rose ointment and cool water over it to calm the hot swelling. Then the patient should lie on his back, and the bandages should not be removed for three days.

Insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will lead to the corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of issue with a corresponding treatment until it heals.

The chisel's tip is sharp and pointed, fixed to a stick. Then, take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone, to prevent it from returning to the bladder.  Then, gently insert the chisel's iron into the urethra until the chisel reaches the stone itself.

Chapter Sixty-one.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely form in women. If one of them develops a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for several reasons. One, the woman may be a virgin; the second, it is difficult to find a woman who would allow herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or related, and third, you cannot find a woman skilled in this practice, especially in manual procedures. Fourth, the incision site for a stone in women is far from the stone itself, requiring a deep incision.
In this case, there is danger. If necessity demands it, one should seek a skilled female physician, though such are rare. If one is not found, seek a virtuous and compassionate male physician or bring in a skilled female midwife or someone with some knowledge in this field.
You should bring her in and instruct her to perform all necessary examinations to locate the stone. First, if the woman is a virgin, a finger should be inserted into her vagina to search for the stone. If found, it should be pressed against her finger.
Then instruct her to make an incision. If the woman is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina to search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and applying firm pressure.
If found, she should gently guide it away from the mouth of the bladder, down as far as possible, towards the upper thigh.  Then make an incision at the point where the vagina meets the upper thigh, whichever side feels the stone is located.
Her finger should remain on the stone, pressing it down.  The incision should be small at first. Then, using a probe, if she feels the stone, she should increase the incision size as much as necessary for the stone to exit.  Know that there are many types of stones.
There are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and branched stones.  Know their types so you can identify what you are dealing with. If bleeding occurs, sprinkle crushed alum over the wound and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone is removed. Prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men, to aid your work. If severe bleeding occurs, and you know the bleeding is from a severed artery, apply styptics to the area.
Press firmly with the forceps and leave it. Do not reapply. Leave the stone and do not remove it.  The patient may perish.  Then treat the wound. If the bleeding subsides after several days and the area becomes infected, return to your work, until the stone is removed.
Chapter Sixty-two.
In the incision on the watery dropsy.
Watery dropsy is the accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum beneath the scrotum, which surrounds the testicle, and is called the scrotum. It may also be in a special membrane which nature has prepared in one area of the testicle.
So that one might think it another testicle, and it is between the scrotum and the white peritoneum we mentioned.  This occurs only rarely.  This dropsy arises from weakness that affects females, in which this substance accumulates.  It may also arise from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture comes in various colors: sometimes yellow, sometimes bloody red, sometimes dark blackish-purple, sometimes watery white, which is the most common. The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where there is an accumulation of water, if it is in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the swelling is somewhat oval, like an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a separate membrane, the swelling is rounded on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, one might imagine it another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable.  If you wish to determine the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe we previously described.  Judge by what is inside the probe's impression.
If we turn to treatment with iron, you should order the patient to be bled, if possible, and if you see their body is full.  Then, they should lie on their back on a slightly raised surface, placing many cloths beneath.  You then sit to their left.
And instruct a servant to sit on his right, extending the patient's penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the lower abdominal region. Then, take a broad lancet and make a longitudinal incision in the scrotum's skin, from the middle towards the pubic area.
The incision should be made in a straight line parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in half, until it reaches the white peritoneum.  Carefully separate it, avoiding further incision.  Your separation should be more pronounced on the side that adheres to the testicle.  Continue the separation as much as possible.
Then, widely dissect the water-filled peritoneum, removing all the water.  Separate the edges of the incision with forceps, and pull the peritoneum upwards, avoiding contact with the scrotum's skin.  Cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either entirely or in pieces.
Especially the thin side, for if you do not fully sever it, you will not be sure the water will not return. If the testicle protrudes from its skin during the procedure, then return it to its place once you have finished cutting the peritoneum, and stitch the edges of the scrotum skin together.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until it heals.  If the testicle has already been damaged by another illness, you should tie off the vessels in the sac to prevent bleeding, then cut the testicle from the sac, remove the testicle, and treat it as previously described.
If the collected water is present in both sides, then know that it is a hydrocele in both. Incise the other side as you did the first, or if it seems easier to perform the procedure on only one side, then do so.  Then, wipe away the blood.
Place wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  On the outside, place another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of syrup and oil, spread it over the testicles and abdominal cavity.  Place a folded cloth, the 'rafad', on top. Bind it from above with the six-sided bandage, in this manner:
Then take two cloths, stitch them together, and fill them with fluffy wool, like this example.  Make the straps for binding from damp cotton or wool and fasten them with a belt around the waist.  One strap should be placed on the buttocks towards the back.
The second strap should go towards the pubic area, with the other two straps on the lower thighs and the remaining two straps on the groins.  All the straps are tied to the waist belt.  This hydrocele can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with a knife.
This involves taking a thin, heated cautery iron, to cut the scrotum skin, until the white sac containing the water is exposed.  Take another cautery iron like this:
It resembles a Greek eye. Then, using it, cauterize the sac while it is hot, until all the moisture is expelled. Then, stretch the sac with forceps and cauterize the containing layer and cut it as much as possible, until all are removed. Be careful not to burn the testicle.
Know that this method is safer and less prone to bleeding than the incision method. If during the incision or cauterization a hot swelling or bleeding or something else occurs, it is necessary to treat each issue with the appropriate treatment as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, let him use the cauterization method as I describe, if it is correct according to the previous indications.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should sit on a high chair. Then cauterize the swelling beneath it with a wide spatula. Make the cauterization along the body, with a wide opening so all the water drains. Then, place cotton on the area and bind it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during the cauterization, it is likely because the white membrane is obstructing the wound opening, preventing the water from draining.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the instrument used for extracting the water from the afflicted, which has been previously illustrated, or enlarge the wound opening slightly.
Know that the water may return and collect after six months or so. If it collects, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient must endure the days.
Chapter Sixty-three.
On the fissure of fleshy ulcers and their treatment.
Know that a fissure on these ulcers is a perilous matter, leading to death in most cases.  Therefore, we recommend leaving them alone and avoiding them. I will describe the treatment and the different types of ulcers.
I say that many tumors can occur in bodies whose structure is like that of the vagina. This can arise from various causes, either from a sharp excess flowing towards the vagina, or from a blow. The color of the tumor is the same as the color of the body, and there is no fluid in it.
The tumor is hard, and sometimes it may be stony, the color like mud, and has no sensation. It may also arise from a constriction and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from swelling of the veins. If it is from an arterial swelling, this is recognized by the scattering of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it will not yield; one should not attempt this at all.  As for what arises from the swelling of the veins, nothing will dissipate from it when pressed with fingers. This type may cause fissures in itself and in other flesh.
One should make a cut in the testicle's skin, then pull the testicle upward, bringing it out from the white peritoneum.  Remove the ligament from its vessels, tie the vessels, and cut the ligament after having freed it from every side of the testicle.  If the testicle has become attached to those growing flesh.
Then the testicle should be removed and cut. If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between vessels, then all such adhesions must be separated and cut in a circular fashion.
If the flesh has grown in the place of adhesion, which is behind, then all of it must be cut and the testicle removed, as we have said. When your work is complete, treat the wound with moist wool in rose-scented oil and drink, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-four.
On treating the dropsy accompanying dropsy.
Dropsy is a swelling twisted in some way, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the limbs.  It hinders the afflicted person from movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment for this ailment is similar to what was previously mentioned.
However, it is necessary to mention the treatment as the ancients did.  I say that the afflicted person should sit on a raised chair, then pull the limbs downwards, then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the vessels that are near the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it and stretch it vigorously.  Then, with a broad, sharp scalpel, make an oblique cut alongside the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.  Then, dissect from every side, as I described to you in dissecting the arteries of the temples.  Then, insert a needle with a double thread into it.
Tie it at the initial points where the dropsy appeared and also at its end. Then, cut it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body, and remove the collected impure and corrupt humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that require time to heal.  Removing the vessels nourishing one of the limbs is acceptable, for a time. If the dropsy affects all the vessels, then one limb, with the vessels, must be removed, so that the testicle does not lack nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wilt and be of no use.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal dropsy.
This dropsy occurs when a fissure appears in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen, near the groin, and the intestines protrude into one of the limbs through that hernia.
This hernia is either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types arise from many causes, such as a blow, a jump, a cry, or lifting a heavy object, and so on.  The sign, if it is from the extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long period.
It does not occur suddenly.  The swelling is flat to the depth because the peritoneum compresses the intestines.  If it is from a fissure in the peritoneum, the sign is that it is accompanied by severe pain immediately at the start, and the swelling is noticeably different beneath the skin nearby.
This is due to the intestines emerging and passing outside the peritoneum.  Pus may also emerge with the intestines, in which case this dropsy is called intestinal and purulent.  It may also be accompanied by wind, and the bowels may be obstructed with faeces, which can lead to the patient's demise.
Because it causes intense pain and rumbling, especially if compressed.  The treatment for these types of ailments with iron is risky; therefore, one must be cautious about resorting to it.  The procedure is to instruct the patient to push the intestines back into their cavity with their hand if they are able to return.
Then he lies on his back before you, and his legs are raised. Then the skin next to the groin is pulled upward, and the entire scrotal skin is cut lengthwise.  Then hooks are inserted into the edges of the incision, as much as needed to widen the slit.
The incision should be as large as possible to allow the testicle to be extracted. Then, the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin is peeled back until the white, firm peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  At that point, insert your index finger beneath the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, the adhesions behind the testicle are released.  Then, the right hand pushes inward against the scrotal skin, while the left hand pulls the white peritoneum upward and lifts the testicle with the peritoneum toward the incision.
You instruct the servant to pull the testicle upward, and you completely release the adhesions behind it.  Examine with your finger to ensure there is no twisted intestine within the firm white peritoneum. If you find any, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten strands, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin near the incision.  Then cut the ends of the thread, so that four strands remain. Then, tie the strands together in a firm knot, and bind the peritoneum, which we said was under the scrotal skin, firmly from both sides.  Then wrap the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the nourishing vessels can reach it. To prevent a hot swelling from developing.  Also make a second knot outside the first knot, at least two fingerbreadths away.  After these two knots, leave a space of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin the size of a finger.
To prevent a hot swelling from developing.  Also make a second knot outside the first knot, at least two fingerbreadths away.  After these two knots, leave a space of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin the size of a finger. Cut the rest completely in a circle and remove the testicle.  Then cut a slit below the scrotal skin, from which blood and pus will flow as described previously. Then apply wool soaked in oil to the wound, and use the ligature as described.
The white peritoneum, which we said to cut carefully to prevent blood flow, often presents this problem.  Leave the ligature until it falls off naturally.  If it takes too long to fall off, apply warm water and something that will cause perspiration to hasten its removal.
Then treat the wound with all other surgical treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
In this type of flatulence, I have seen no one dare treat it with iron.  The ancients mentioned creating [the treatment] as described for the flatulence with the pouch, namely by tying the vessels gently beneath the incision.
Then it is incised in the middle and the swelling is treated to open it up so that the vessels fall out, and the wound is treated as we described until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
In the hernia of the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, and nothing of the intestines descends into the groin.  If it does descend, this is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the rupture in the peritoneum increases until the intestines and the spermatic cord descend into the scrotum, and that appears from the extension of the peritoneum that is in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and prolapses the area, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I previously described.
It may also be treated with an iron, in this manner: the patient lies on their back before you, then you make a transverse incision at the location of the swollen protruding hernia, about the width of three fingers, and then you dissect the peritoneum under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath is exposed, then you take a reed, place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum, pressing it into the abdomen's depth. Then you stitch the two protruding parts of the peritoneum at the reed's end, and attach one to the other with stitches.
Then you withdraw the reed, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle, nor anything else, as I instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernias. Then treat it with the treatment of surgical procedures.
When the threads are severed, clean and close the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum will contract, and the hernia will not increase.
And this method is superior in this location, as it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
Relaxation of the scrotum skin.
The scrotum skin often relaxes in some people without the internal flesh relaxing, making the appearance unattractive.  Therefore, whoever wishes to treat it should lay the patient on their back.
Cut all the relaxed skin away from the thick skin, then bring the edges together.  If you wish, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure the stitches are secure, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the usual treatment for other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, it was necessary for me not to mention it in this book of mine.  However, I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be part of the physician's knowledge when questioned about it, and so that he may know the treatment for those who have been afflicted; and the other, that we often need to castrate animals for our benefit.
Like sheep, oxen, and cats, and other animals like that, I say that castration can be done in two ways: either by crushing or by cutting.  The method involving crushing is that we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you crush it with your hand until it breaks apart and is no longer discernible to the touch.  As for castration by cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal and grasp the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the appendages and make a single cut on each testicle.
Until the testicles protrude, then cut them off after removing their coverings, leaving no peritoneum on them except the thin membrane that covers the vessels. This type of castration is better than that done by crushing.
Because crushing might leave some part of the testicles, and the animal might desire mating, and then the wound must be treated until it heals.
Seventy-first chapter.
On the treatment of hermaphrodites.
The hermaphrodite in males is of two types. One is that which appears below the scrotum or in the scrotal skin between the testicles, resembling a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the scrotal skin.
As for females, there is one type, which is located above the vulva on the pubis, like small, protruding male testicles, one of which resembles a male penis, and the two are like the female ones.  The treatment for all three types.
The two types of males and the single type of females, the excess flesh must be cut off until its trace is gone, then treated with the treatment of other surgical wounds until healed. As for the second type of males from whom urine flows from within the scrotal skin, there is no treatment and it does not improve at all.
Chapter Seventy-one.
On cutting the clitoris and excess flesh from the vulva of women.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural state, becoming inflamed and unattractive, and in some women, may even enlarge, spreading out like that of a man, and interfering with intercourse.
Therefore, you should grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or forceps and cut it off.  Do not cut too deeply, especially not into the root, to avoid excessive bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And perhaps it will protrude outwards, resembling a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients called it the "tail-like disease."  You should cut it off, like the clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of hernia.
Hernia is when a woman's vagina is not pierced or the piercing is small, and it may be either congenital or acquired.  An acquired hernia results from a previous ailment and may be due to excess, growing flesh.
or thin or thick peritoneum, and it may be located deep within the uterus, along its sides, at the top or bottom, preventing intercourse, procreation, and childbirth, and possibly menstruation.  A midwife can determine this by observation.
If the obstruction is visible and close by, and if not visible, she should investigate with her fingers or a probe.  If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, then promptly pierce it, that is, place a sort of wedge on the labia, and then place your thumbs from both hands.
And the woman should lie on her back with her legs spread apart. Then, forcefully spread the labia until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction is opened. Then, take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. The woman should be copulated daily so that the area does not close again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a wide scalpel resembling a leaf of asparagus. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, grasp it with forceps and cut it. Have with you remedies to stop bleeding without cauterization, such as acacia, cynamomum, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube to prevent the wound from closing quickly. Hold it for days, then use a dry linen wick.  Treat it with the usual treatment for inflammation until it heals.  Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus, which should also be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, a cancerous tumor in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are swellings of the veins' mouths, causing copious and constant bleeding.  If hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  Their types and signs were previously mentioned in the classification, and we will mention them here.
Of those that are treatable and those that are not, I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if located deep within the uterus and not perceptible to touch, are not treatable with iron. Those that are in the mouth of the uterus and can be felt are those that are treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then the warts should be scraped with a lancet or a rough cloth and cut from their roots.  Then, after bleeding from one of the blood-stopping incisions, powder should be applied, without pricking.
Such as qaqia, shiyan, and frankincense, and similar substances.  Then, raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and astringents, and similar substances. Then, take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back. If the bleeding stops, then stop. Otherwise, apply leeches to the groin and breasts, without restrictions. Then, take wool, dip it in the juice of celandine or the juice of comfrey or the juice of elder twigs with astringent drink, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the affected area with ointment until healed.  As for a red sore, resembling the heads of boils, rough in appearance, it should be cut away as described for warts, and treated until healed.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the treatment of a tumor that appears in the uterus.
Many types of tumors can appear in the uterus, such as cancer, stony tumor, ulcers, wasting disease, devouring disease, warts, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors.  All these diseases and their types and signs have been mentioned in the classification.
Therefore, in this article, we should discuss the hot tumor appearing in the uterus, if it is among the tumors that accumulate over time, how it should be treated with iron.  It is necessary to observe whether the pain of the tumor at its onset is severe, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
If you see that it is red, and if it is sensitive to touch, do not rush to treat it with cauterization. Treat it with something that aids maturation, until these symptoms subside. Then, the woman should sit on a stool with two seats, leaning back.
And her legs should be brought together towards the lower abdomen, and her thighs spread apart. Her arms should be placed under her knees, and tied together with an appropriate bandage.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument for opening the cervix, the image of which will be shown later.  When using this instrument, one should estimate the depth of the woman's uterus to ensure the instrument does not penetrate beyond its depth.
If the instrument is too deep, it will harm the patient.  If the instrument extends beyond the depth, then pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterine cavity.  The spiral that runs through the instrument should be positioned from the top.
While you hold the instrument, the servant should rotate the spiral until the cervix opens. If a boil is visible and feels soft and thin, it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel.  If all the pus has been discharged.
A soft wick dipped in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound. The wick should extend outward from the incision in the cervix and, externally, on the uterus and pubis, clean wool should be soaked in boiled baker's water.
Then, treat it after two days with the appropriate ointment until it heals.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with the root of the wormwood or long zeraond.  Then return to the ointment. If the boil is hidden deep within the uterus.
It is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, but to treat it with what we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter Seventy-five.
On teaching midwives how to treat live fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife must first know the natural form of childbirth.  Signs include the woman's pushing downwards, her longing to smell the air, and the easing of her postpartum discomfort.
And if the fetus emerges quickly, know that this birth is in the natural form, and the fetus emerges headfirst, with the placenta attached or hanging from its navel.  If you see these signs, you should massage her abdomen to facilitate the fetus's quick expulsion.
If it descends headfirst, the placenta descends with it, and is cleaned thoroughly of impurities.  Any birth other than this is unnatural and objectionable, for the fetus may emerge on its feet, with its hands and feet before its head.
One hand or one foot, or the head and a hand, or it emerges curled, or perhaps on its back, and other such objectionable forms. The midwife must be skillful and gentle with all these things, and avoid errors and mistakes.
And I am explaining each form and how to handle it so that you will be able to recognize it and understand it.  If the fetus emerges headfirst, the natural way, and if the woman is greatly burdened and finds the delivery difficult, and you see her strength weakening, then have her sit on a chair and instruct women to assist her.
Bind her uterus with cooked herbs and moist ointments.  Then, the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers and cut the placenta, or cut it with her nail, until the moisture within it flows out.  Massage the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with the juice of fenugreek and a sheirj unguent.  Then, after the injection, tell her to cough and to sneeze her with a kunds.  Hold her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge soon.  If the fetus' hands emerge first, then gently push them back, little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a stool, and raise her legs above.  Then shake the stool on the ground, and the woman holds on to prevent falling during the shaking.  If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them out gently; it will come out.  If the fetus emerges on its feet, then you should push its feet upwards, then gradually turn the fetus to straighten it out, then take one of its feet and pull it out gradually.
If they emerge to the thighs, tell her to cough and sneeze her with a kunds, then it will come out. If it does not come out as described, then gently push the fetus back bit by bit until it is in its natural position, then it will come out smoothly. If it resists all these methods.
Take the juice of  *khatmi*, the juice of fenugreek, sheirj unguent, and dissolved gum. Grind them well in a mortar. Then apply it to the vulva and lower abdomen of the woman. Then seat her in lukewarm water until the fetlock joints are reached.
If you see that it has softened beneath, make her a poultice of myrrh and let her bear it. If she holds the poultice for an hour, then seat her on a chair, then sneeze her, block her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen, then the fetus will come out immediately. The fetus will emerge on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands bit by bit. Then have the woman lie on her back, her legs dangling downward, lying on a bed, and her hands clasped together. Then gently squeeze the upper abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her feet are raised.  If the fetus does not come out, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then squeeze above the fetlocks bit by bit until the fetus rises. Then the midwife should insert her hand and gently reshape the fetus.
Tell the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The fetus will emerge sideways, one arm dangling.  Gently try to return the arm.  If you cannot, then make the woman stand, and have her walk. If she cannot walk, then have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice described from the saliva. If the delivery begins, seat her on a chair, then work to return the arm and straighten the fetus to its normal shape. Make the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it comes out.
If the fetus emerges on its back, arms outstretched, and face towards the mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's arm and gently turn it and straighten it.  Once straightened, apply the grease and saliva as previously described.
Then tell her to move slightly, and when she does, seat her on a chair.  Tell her to lean to the right side, then instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with the stick.  The fetus will come out, upright on its side.
If the fetus and placenta descend in this manner, slit the placenta with your fingernail to remove moisture. If the placenta adheres to the fetus's face or neck, cut it from the navel to prevent suffocation and death. Then push the fetus inside to correct its position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly, as this will facilitate delivery.  Twins, or multiple fetuses, are often born.  Three or four may be born and survive, but this is rare. Five fetuses are unnatural and will not survive.
They are surrounded by a single placenta, even if numerous, and are separated by a membranous partition attached to the navel of each.  They may emerge as a single, naturally, easily delivered fetus.  They may also emerge in a difficult way, as described before.
If some of them are difficult to deliver and present unnatural forms, treat them as described previously.  Let the midwife be gentle and act with care, and ingenuity for each case, employing any methods that lead to safety.
Fetuses that can be conceived in the womb include one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten.  I have observed a woman who miscarried seven and another who miscarried fifteen, all conceived.
By the estimation of the all-knowing and all-powerful, all this should be part of a midwife's knowledge, as such occurrences may happen to her one day, and she should be prepared to handle them appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-seven.
On the removal of a stillborn fetus.
If, after treating the stillborn fetus with the methods of treatment described in the section on treatments, it is not expelled and you are forced to use your hands, then you must observe. If the woman is strong and healthy, and shows no symptoms that suggest potential harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downwards, and her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides or tie her to the bed so that her body does not shift when the fetus is pulled out.
Then, the mouth of her uterus should be moistened with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the *khatmi* plant, fenugreek, and flaxseed.  Then the midwife should anoint her hands with these ointments and saliva.  Then, she should gently insert her hands into the uterus, and then seek a location in which to insert the hooks from the fetus, and the appropriate places for this.
If the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, insert the hooks into its eyes, or the back of its neck, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or collarbone, or in the areas near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
And if the descent is on its feet, then in the pubic area and the middle ribs.  One should hold the hook with the right hand, and curl it between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and the hook into one of the positions mentioned.
Until it reaches an empty space, then insert another or a third hook, if needed, so that the pull is equal and does not lean to one side, then pull in a level manner.  The pull should not be straight, but should be moved in all directions.
To make its expulsion easier, just as one does with a tooth when extracting it.  In between, the pull should be relaxed.  If it is stuck in a particular area, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment, and insert them to rotate whatever is stuck. If some part of the fetus emerges, move the hooks to other positions.
She should do this gradually, until the whole fetus is expelled. If a hand comes out before the rest and cannot be pushed back due to compression, it should be wrapped in a cloth and pulled out until it is completely expelled, then cut it off.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Likewise, this should be done for the other hand and legs. If the fetal head is large and faces pressure during expulsion, or if there is accumulated water in its head, then a pointed/sharp scalpel should be inserted between the fingers.
With it, one should split the head, so the water comes out, or crush it with the instrument called the *mashdakh*, which appears in the image of other tools in the following chapter.
Likewise, do this if the fetus has a naturally large head.  The skull should be split or crushed with the *mashdakh*, as we said, and then the bones removed with forceps. If the head comes out and is compressed at the collarbone, it should be split to drain the moisture in the chest.
Then the chest will adhere.  If the chest does not adhere and cannot be expelled, cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or contains a tumor, it should be incised so that moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and positioning it toward the cervix is easy.  If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be cut to drain the contents.
If the rest of the organs are removed and the head is retracted and retained, let the midwife insert her left hand.  If the cervix is open, insert the hand into the neck of the uterus, feel for the head, and pull it with the fingers toward the cervix. Then insert a hook or two and pull with them.
If the cervix has adhered to a hot, inflamed tumor, it should not be forced.  Instead, moist, oily substances should be applied, compresses used, and she should sit in relaxing and moistening waters.
If the fetus is on its side, if possible, the procedures described for a living fetus should be used.  If not possible, it should be cut and removed.  Absolutely nothing of the placenta should be left inside.  Once you have finished the treatment, use the treatments described for hot tumors that occur in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should descend into constricting waters and use all other treatments.  I have witnessed a woman who conceived, then the fetus died within her, and then she conceived again.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared in her abdomen, swelling until it opened and began to discharge pus.  I was called to treat her and treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal.  I then applied some strong, drawing ointments.
Then a bone came out of the area.  After several days, another bone came out.  I was amazed by this, as the abdomen is a location without bone.  I surmised that it was from the bones of the dead fetus.  I probed the wound and removed numerous bones from the head of the fetus.
I continued this process until I removed many bones.  The woman was in the best of health.  She lived for some time, with only a slight discharge of pus from the area.  I have related this unusual case.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to achieve by his own hand in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This is the binder with which books are bound. It has two spirals at the ends of the wooden planks.  However, this spiral should be gentler than the binder, and should be made either of ebony or of lignum vitae wood. The width of each plank should be the thickness of two fingers.
Their edges should be about a finger's width, and their length should be a foot and a half.  In the middle of the planks, two dowels of the same wood are secured within them, with a length of half a foot or slightly more, and a width of about two fingers or slightly more. These two dowels...
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals.  An image of another similar instrument, gentler and lighter.
Made from ebony or lignum vitae wood, shaped like tongs, but with two dowels at the end, as you see.  The length of each dowel is about a foot, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the cervix with it...
Then, place the woman on a bed with her legs spread apart, and insert these two dowels, tightly, into the cervix.  While holding the end of the instrument below her between her thighs, open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with tongs, to the degree of opening the cervix you desire.
