Chapter seventy-eight.
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, the woman should be instructed to hold herself, then to be made to sneeze with a cundurango, and hold your hand tightly over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, then do so; otherwise, take a measure.
Pierce a hole in the cover, place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as fodenj, sithab, dill, chamomile, shih, sliakha, and qanthorion, all or some of these medicines.  Soak them in water and heat them over a fire, then place a silver tube on the hole in the cover, and the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then make her sneeze, as we said.  If the placenta comes out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to immerse her left hand in the grease of a she-goat or the saliva of the Egyptian viper.
Then she should insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta. If she finds it, grasp it and pull it out slowly, little by little. If it is stuck deep in the uterus, insert the hand as we described, until the placenta is found, then pull it out slowly, gently.
So that the uterus does not fall when pulled too hard, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left.  Then increase the amount of pulling, as it will respond and become free from the adhesion. If the cervix is closed, the treatment with sneezing, the pot, and the herbs, has been described.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, avoid force when pulling it back.  Instead, tie what has been removed to the woman's thigh, then inject her with the four-fold ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, but if it rots, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, it is necessary to use appropriate incense.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried the incense of myrrh and dried figs, a description of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated when menses and placenta are retained, and the like.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper.  The thin end is placed at the vulva, and the wide end on the fire.  Incense is placed on the embers, held until it is consumed, and then replaced with more.
The seventy-ninth chapter.
In the treatment of the unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with unperforated anuses, sometimes blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger; otherwise, she should pierce it with a sharp lancet, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and treat with ointments until healed.  If you fear that it will close again, place a lead tube in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
A blockage of the anus may also result from a wound abscess or a tumor.  It is necessary to incise this abscess, and then treat with the remedies mentioned, and cauterize.
Eighty-eighth chapter.
Treatment of warts that occur on the lower parts.
Warts that occur on the lower parts are a thickening and hardening that occurs near the anus, externally or in the space on one side, and may be one or more in number.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge a watery, whitish fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate the rectum and intestines.  Those that do not penetrate can be identified by what is discharged in the stool and wind when using laxatives for bowel movements.
And worms may come from them.  Some warts, if in the space, may penetrate the bladder and urethra.  Some may penetrate the thigh joint and coccyx.  What is known about the wart penetrating the anus also applies to those that do not penetrate.
Insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe of copper or iron into the wart, if there is no winding in the wart. If there is a winding, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.  If you feel nothing at all and no stool, wind, or worms emerge from the opening, as we have said, know that it is not penetrated; then proceed to treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it and the inability of the area to heal with medication.  If it penetrates the thigh joint or coccyx, the sign is the probe reaching that place.
If there is no winding to the bone, and there is pain in the vicinity, and pus constantly discharges from it, and treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment, these penetrating warts are all incurable.
Treating them is a fruitless effort for those unskilled physicians who attempt it.  As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, recovery is hoped for using iron, as I describe and have experienced.  The patient should lie on their back before you.
Raise the patient's legs upward, and incline the thighs towards the abdomen.  Then introduce a brass or lead probe into the wart, if there is a winding, so that you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has penetrated itself, exposed without feeling any tissue or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrating. Do not expend effort on it, as there is no cure for it, as we have said.
Some say it heals in rare cases in some people.  A treatment that may be helpful is to heat a thin cautery according to the size of the wart, as mentioned before, and insert it into the wart until it reaches the vicinity of the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
So that you know that all of the excess, solidified flesh, resembling the tube of a bird's feather, has been burned. Then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter until that burned flesh is removed. Then treat it with healing ointments, and if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment.  If you insert the probe, but it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between the probe and your finger, and you see the wart beneath the skin surface, then in that case, cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. As you make the incision with the probe inside the wart, continue the incision until you reach where the tip of the probe ends.  Remove the probe and discard it. Then, clean away all the solidified flesh resembling a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing behind.
Then, treat it with healing ointments until it heals.  If bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from completing the excision of that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine.  Cauterization with fire combines two beneficial effects: burning the excess flesh.
Stop the bleeding and dry the moisture. Then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the area becomes purulent and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then, at that point, treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of firm flesh.
These are the remedies that have a binding and drying effect.  If it is consolidated, then it has healed, and further attempts are not necessary. If the wart extends deep into the anus and away from the body surface, insert your finger into the anus and search with it.
If you feel the probe and there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh between you and it, and it is close to penetration, then the only approach is through hope and anticipation.  That is, you should use one of three approaches: either cauterize it, as we have stated.
Or to dissect it until you reach its bottom to gain access to insert the thread and treatment from close proximity. Do not exaggerate the dissection, lest you sever the muscle surrounding the anus, causing involuntary bowel movements in the patient. Then, treat it with the methods mentioned, and perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or, after dissecting it to the vicinity of the anus as we mentioned, either with a probe or another sharp-pointed instrument, penetrate that barrier and pierce it.  Then, thoroughly remove all the congested flesh that is in the incision to the extent possible.
Then, suture the entire wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed, leaving the wart open within the anus, which will be less troublesome for the patient.  The wart may be cured in this manner.
If, when inserting the probe into the wart, it is located on one side of the anus, near the skin surface and the ring of the anus, then take a probe with a pierced tip, like a shoemaker's awl, in this manner:
Then, insert a twisted thread of five strands or so into it.  Then, insert the probe with the thread into the wart until it reaches the bottom; if it is punctured in the edge of the anus from the inside, near the edge.
Then, take the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and pulling the thread out.  Gather both ends and tie them tightly. Leave it for a day or two.  Every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it firmly until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls off.  Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the wart is not punctured, puncture it as best you can, but if it is deep, you must not do so, lest you cut the muscle.  Then do with it as we have described until it is healed.
The shape of the pointed lancet used to cut warts should have a very sharp curve on one side and a blunt side on the other, so as not to cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids come in two types: either they are inside the anus, resembling red swellings or bunches of grapes, small or large, and blood constantly drips from them.
They can be single or multiple, and some are located outside the anus, along its edges.  However, those located outside the anus are usually less moist, dripping yellowish water or a little blood constantly.  They are the same color as the skin.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to have the patient defecate and strain until the anus protrudes, and the growths are visible.  Then quickly grasp them with forceps or your fingernail and cut them at their roots. If forceps cannot grasp them due to their moisture and looseness,
Then grab them with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened linen, or pull them out with your fingers. Cut them and then, after cutting, sprinkle sharp powders to act as cauterization, or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  If the anus does not come out, inject the patient with an enema containing a small amount of irritant to wash out what's inside the anus and encourage quick expulsion when the patient strains.  As for the growths outside the anus, their treatment is easy and simple.
To treat them, grasp them with your fingernail or forceps and cut them, then treat as previously described until healed.  For those who dislike cutting with iron, it is advisable to use a different method.  Take a thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upward, pierce it with the needle at its root from the other side, wrap the ends of the thread under the needle which is positioned across it, pull the wart firmly, tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the warts, leaving one unpierced.
To allow blood to drain, place a cloth dipped in rose oil or qirūṭī oil on the anus, instruct the patient to rest, and leave it until the warts fall off. Once they fall off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until healed.
As for treating fissures, they often arise from dryness, accumulation of waste material, and sluggishness. If they persist and remedies fail, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or fingernail until they become moist and the top layer of skin preventing healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, scrape it more forcefully than before until it bleeds and peels. Then treat it; it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On treating inverted and non-inverted nails, dry warts, and the nits.
A nail is a round, skin-colored swelling resembling the head of a nail that occurs throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes, and causes pain when walking.
One should cut around the nail, grasp it with a pick or a hook, and pull it out from its root, then treat it.  If desired, cauterize it as described in the relevant chapter.  As for dry warts, each is a small, rough, hard, round protuberance on the surface of the body.
They often appear on children's hands.  Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting it, or piercing it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off.  If desired, cauterize it as previously described with fire or a caustic agent.
Inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument I have mentioned in the treatment of nits.  Nits are also a small, hard, thickened protuberance on the skin's surface, penetrating deeply.  If exposed to cold, they produce a sensation similar to the sting of ants.
They occur throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a tube made of strong goose or eagle feathers, placing it around the nit, ensuring the tube encircles it completely, then turning the tube with your hand until it cracks the surrounding tissue.
Push it into the flesh and the nit will be pulled out from its root.  Alternatively, you can use a brass or iron tube in the same manner.
The top of the tube should be smooth and pliable, making it easy for the fingers to control and twist it.  Alternatively, cauterize the nit as previously described for cauterizing warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Illustrations of instruments used for injections in ailments of the rectum, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, porcelain, or hollowed or hammered brass.  Small and large versions of this instrument can be made, depending on the intended users. The ones used to treat small children, or those with narrow or painful rectums, should be very delicate.  Image of a large syringe:.
The upper funnel, where the bag is attached, should be wide, with a partition where the bag is attached, as you see.  The lower end, which enters the rectum, should be very smooth and solid.  On one side, two small holes; on the other side, a single hole.
The size of the holes should be the thickness of a reed or slightly thicker.  The bag for holding the medicine, made of animal bladder or sheepskin, should be shaped like a small tray, one span in diameter.
It is pierced with many holes, spiralling around, with a space of approximately finger's width between each hole. Then a strong thread, made of doubled ten threads or so, is inserted into these holes to gather the bag like a tray. When the medicine is placed inside, tighten the head of the syringe firmly between the stones. Then inject.  This is the image of the bag and its holes.
How to inject: fill the bladder or skin with medicine, warmed to the patient's tolerance. Be careful not to make it too hot or too cold. Then, the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed beneath. Then the tip of the syringe is smeared with ointment, egg white, or licorice saliva, or similar.  It is gently inserted into the rectum. The attendant of the bag holds it with both hands and squeezes forcefully until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowels.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient lowers their legs. If the medicine is pushing to come out, the patient should hold back as much as possible.  If the patient sleeps with it all night, it will be more effective for relieving. If injected for diarrhea and quick expulsion is desired, repeat the injection until cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe must be very gentle, smoother than the syringes used for children. This is an image of a gentle syringe, with its skin tightened.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have previously mentioned a classification of treatments similar to cauterization, and now I am describing here the treatment of cauterizations by hand and medicine thoroughly.  I say that wounds differ according to the object that caused the wound and according to the location of the wound.
The objects causing wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a cut from a sword or knife, a stab from a spear or a stick or arrow, and many other things.
As for wounds according to locations on the body, like wounds on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
I will describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a standard and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple wounds of the head, particularly because the compound wounds will be mentioned in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a bone fracture, and if it is from a blow of a stone or similar, and the skin alone was bruised, and the wound was large, and I feared for the patient the onset of a hot swelling, then immediately lance it with the lancet on the spot. Do not delay this, and draw blood from him to the extent of his strength, unless something prevents venesection, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is slight, and let the venesection be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and thick foods. If anything prevents venesection, then let him lessen his food intake, avoid meat, and abstain from drinks. Apply a compress soaked in rose oil, alone or mixed with a drink with astringent properties, to the wound if a hot swelling occurs.
If you are not concerned about a hot swelling, apply a compress to the wound if it is fresh and its blood has not been altered by the pernicious air that I will describe to you shortly, but if the air has altered it, apply some ointments.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and from a sword cut or similar, and its edges have not come together by themselves, bring them together by stitching, as described in my account of abdominal wound suturing.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its only hold is in a slight attachment, cut it off, then treat the wound with remedies to form firm flesh in place of the skin.  Tell the patient to eat foods that provide strength, such as heads and marrow, and similar items.
If a foulness occurs in the skin but it is not attached to the bone, cut away all the rotten parts and apply a poultice.  Then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding, and the bleeding does not stop with remedies, then search for the artery.
If you strike it and it does not stop bleeding, cut it off with a scalpel and tie it off.  If the situation requires it, and what we have mentioned is not helpful, cauterize it until the bleeding stops.  If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy and simple.  Just apply this powder to the wound while it is still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is to take one part frankincense, two parts myrrh, and three parts quicklime or un-quicklime.  Grind all of them, sift it, and pack the wound with it, and tie it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will clot with the blood, then it is tied from above with bandages.  Leave it without undoing it as long as it does not swell.  The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  Quicklime itself can do this if frankincense and myrrh are not available.
This action can also be done with a poultice in many large wounds if its application and binding are well done, and the wounds are fresh and bleeding, as long as the air has not changed them.  However, if the air has altered them somewhat, especially if it is during the summer.
Applying this powder is not the only way to treat it.  Use some ripening ointments or a barley flour poultice made with water and honey until pus forms, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
If the wound involves a simple fracture of the bone, pull it out with a dressing.  The treatment of skull fractures is mentioned elsewhere, in the section on neck injuries. If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treatments that constrict strongly, such as al-zunjar and al-zaj and similar substances, because they injure and spasm the nerve.
Absolutely nothing cold should be placed on the wound, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use mild remedies like moistened rosewater and zinc oxide, or oil-soaked or rose and mulberry-oil based ointments.
If also washed with fresh water and other mineral substances in this manner, and moist ointments.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with strips, then treat until healed.
If the wound has a cavity and pus has accumulated at the bottom, drain it at the lowest point. If an artery is severed and blood is flowing, tie it off, cauterize it, or cut it if necessary.
If part or all of the cartilage of the larynx has been severed but the veins are intact, bring the edges of the skin together with sutures along the larynx, do not touch the larynx itself, but smooth it out and restore it to its natural shape, then tie it tightly and leave it for a period of time.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until healed. If the wound has affected the bone, probe it. If there are bone fragments, remove all you can, or as many as possible. If not all can be removed, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powders we have described. If the bone in the wound has become necrotic and suppurated, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you see a cavity, observe if air escapes when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a deadly wound. If there is no cavity and it is fresh with blood, do not immediately apply powders or tighten it, lest the blood be trapped in the cavity.
It will recede to the heart and kill the patient. Instead, apply a drawing ointment twice daily. If ointment is unavailable, place a moistened compress in the wound to absorb the moisture.  Have the patient sleep with the wound uppermost.
To drain any accumulated matter. If three days or more have passed since the wound was inflicted, and the patient shows no spasms, abnormal palpitations, or shortness of breath, and their general condition is otherwise good, know that the wound is sound. Treat it with bandages and other remedies until healed.
If viewing the wound and applying gentle pressure does not reveal the cause of the persistent discharge, know that suppuration has occurred. Treat it according to the appropriate section.  The patient should lie on the wound to drain any accumulated matter. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it as described previously by suturing.
If large, apply powders; if small, apply powders.  If the wound has affected the bone, fracturing it, probe the wound and remove any loose fragments if they are readily available. If not, leave them until...
This will cause the wound to fester, making removal easier. As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment follows the principles we have outlined.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On wounds of the abdomen, intestinal protrusion, and their suturing.
A wound exposing the abdomen may be large, small, or medium.  A large wound may expose intestines, or several intestines.  Then, returning them to the abdomen and suturing them will be more difficult and harder.
A small wound too, may present difficulty in another way, requiring more challenging return of the intestines.  If the intestines are not returned to their proper place immediately, they swell and thicken, making their return more difficult.  Therefore, the most favorable wound is the medium one, because it does not cause the same difficulty in returning the intestines as the other two types.
Know that abdominal suturing has four types. The two general types are suitable for abdominal suturing and for suturing other bodily wounds; the other two types are specific; they apply to all wounds, but are especially suited to abdominal suturing.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification.  I say that if the wound is small and something of the intestines has protruded and its return is difficult, it is due to one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestines were previously inflated by the cold air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, alone or in which has been cooked barley, saffron and hyssop, and apply it to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with astringent properties may also achieve this, and is superior to plain water in resolving the swelling.  If you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which you have cooked  fennel or anise, as this facilitates their return with ease.
If its return remains impossible after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used for piercing the nostrils. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision, and incision is only resorted to as a last resort when all other methods fail.  This is a depiction of the instrument.
Its one curved side is limited, and the other side is unlimited.  The thin end is not as thin as a scalpel blade, but is slightly blunt.  It is a tool similar to a small pickaxe, as you see.  If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, it is necessary that their return be to their natural form.
Its specific location, if achievable, is best.  However, if the wound is extensive and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated above their head.  If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be raised higher than the lower body.
Likewise, if the wound is in one side of the abdomen, always keep the side with the incision higher than the other.  This should be used in major and medium surgical procedures.
For minor surgeries, position it as best you can. Then have a helpful assistant hold the entire wound in their hands and bring the edges together.  Then, reveal the wound section by section to the person performing the stitching.
This is the common method of single-stitching: take a needle or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Leave a space the thickness of a little finger on the wound's edges.  Insert a single needle, without thread, into the edges of the skin. Using the skin's edges and the peritoneum's edges (beneath the skin, from within), until you penetrate it on that side.  The edges of the skin and peritoneum have come together, making four layers. Then, you tie a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides, bringing the wound edges together tightly.
Then leave a space the thickness of a finger and insert another needle.  Tie it with the thread, as you did with the first needle.  Continue doing this with as many needles as needed to completely close the wound.  Use needles of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut through the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore the needles should be of medium thickness.  Do not insert the needle into the skin's edge too deeply.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not open before it heals, and also do not pull too tightly with the sutures so that the wound cannot heal.  Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient while they sleep.  Provide him with linen strips to hold the needle ends on each side.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed.  This method of stitching with needles is best suited for minor surgeries, as it may suffice to use one or two needles, or so.
As for the method of suturing a common wound, gather the four edges, meaning the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, in one step using a needle with a thread of moderate thickness and thinness.  Then, after the needle has passed through these four edges, return the needle from the same side from which you began.
So that the thread interlocks from above the wound, thus making the suture similar to the method of tying bags to secure goods.  Leave a space of the width of a little finger between each stitch.  This method of stitching is known to everyone.
With this method of suturing, a surgeon treated a man who had a wound in his abdomen caused by a knife.  The surgical opening was more than a span in size, and about two spans of the mid-intestine had protruded from the wound.  The opening was located in the center of the abdomen.
After the intestine had been protruding from the wound for twenty-four hours, he returned it.  The wound healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered.  He lived many years after that, functioning normally in all his activities.  The physicians had previously judged that he would not recover.
Surprisingly, I did not treat it with any ointment, because I was in a place with no medications available.  I used old cotton on the wound twice a day and treated it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specific types of stitching, I mention it based on Galen's text:  Begin the stitching at the skin, inserting the needle from the outside to the inside.  Once the needle has penetrated the skin and the muscle, continue in a straight line along the entire length of the abdomen.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle.  Then, insert the needle into its other edge, from inside to outside, at the edge of the diaphragm.  If you have inserted it, insert it again into this same edge of the diaphragm, from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle into its other edge, from inside to outside.  While inserting it into the peritoneum, insert it also into the edge of the diaphragm on its side, until it penetrates completely.
Then, beginning again from this same side, follow the edge adjacent to the peritoneum on the other side.  Bring the needle out through the skin near it, then return it to that same skin and follow the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side, tracing the edge of the diaphragm alongside it.
Bring it out through the skin on its side.  Do this again and again, until you have stitched the entire wound in this manner.  The general description of this stitching is like the stitching of furriers for furs.
By stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leaving it once from the other side, until it is empty.  As for the second type of specialized stitching mentioned by Galen, this is his statement in its entirety.
He means to stitch it in the manner some physicians stitch, by bringing together each part to its naturally corresponding counterpart, thus bringing the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the diaphragm to its other edge. This is how I describe it to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the diaphragm closest to you, from the outside, and penetrate it from the outside to the inside; leaving the edges of the peritoneum. Then the needle should be returned and penetrated from the outside to the inside of both edges of the peritoneum, and then returned.
And penetrate it from the inside to the outside in the edge of the diaphragm on the opposite side.  This method of stitching is better than the simple, common stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's statement, verbatim. Some say that stitching the abdomen can be accomplished with two needles. This means to insert a single thread into both, starting by inserting the needle from your side and penetrating it to the other side; then insert the other needle from the opposite side and penetrate it to your side, according to the stitching of the leather workers, as is customary.
Know that if the rupture is in the center of the abdomen, stitching it is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its treatment is like other wounds.  If you grasp the wound fresh with its blood before the air has altered it, and you have returned and stitched the intestines securely, then apply a styptic dressing.
If the wound has been affected by the air, apply some ointments that you have available until it suppurates and the stitches fall out, and the peritoneum and diaphragm adhere; then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines available, apply a poultice of old cotton as soon as it begins to suppurate, and change it twice a day as I have taught you, until it heals. You will not require any further treatment in most cases, if the wounds are simple, unless you fear that the wound will cause major organs to be involved in the pain.
Therefore, it is necessary to dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit.  If he feels pain or putridity in his intestines, as often occurs, then inject him with a lukewarm, dark, astringent drink.
Especially if the putridity has reached the intestines and has become a penetrating wound into its cavity.  Know that the thicker portions of the intestines are easier to heal than the thinner ones.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it will not heal from a wound that occurs within it at all, due to the abundance and size of its blood vessels, the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nerves.  However, if what protrudes from the wound is the jejunum and you catch it quickly, return it in the same manner as you would return a severed portion of intestine.
If it has been a while and the jejunum has turned green or black, then it should be tied with thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, because the jejunum contains veins and arteries.  Then cut what is below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so it is easier for you to lift and remove it when the jejunum falls out and the wound becomes suppurative.  This describes a wound that occurs in the intestines.  Now, if a perforation in the intestines occurs, and it is small...
In some cases, it may be possible to repair it, for I have seen a person who had been wounded in the abdomen by a spear thrust.  The wound was to the right of the stomach, and it festered, and feces and air were discharged from it.  I treated it, though I did not hope for a cure. I continued to treat him until he healed, and the area closed.  When I saw the area had healed, I feared that the patient might have some unfortunate occurrence within his body.  But he had no such misfortune, and remained in the best possible condition. Indeed, he ate, drank, engaged in sexual intercourse, bathed, and performed exercises as part of his care.  Some experienced individuals have stated that if a small wound occurs in the intestines, it should be sutured in this manner.
Large ants, with large heads, are taken.  Then the edges of the wound are brought together.  An ant, with its mouth open, is placed over the edges of the wound. If it bites down and closes its mouth, its head is cut off; it then adheres and does not come loose. Then another ant is placed near the first one.
This is repeated with several ants according to the size of the wound, and then the wound is closed and sutured.  Those heads remain attached to the intestines until the intestines heal and recover, and no harm occurs to the patient.  The intestines can also be sutured with thin thread.
The method for removing the animal glue from the needle is to take the end of this thread from the glue and pull it free.  Then a fine, spun linen thread is tied to that end, and this linen thread is then placed in the needle. The glue thread is used to suture the intestines, and then the intestines are returned to the abdomen.  This method of suturing with ants and glue is only by way of hope and expectation.
However, if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, there is no remedy and no cure.
Chapter Eighty-six.
In the treatment of colds and nasal obstructions.
Know that any wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic, protracted, and ulcerated without healing, and constantly discharges pus that does not cease, is generally called a fistula, in whatever organ it may be, and we call it a cold or fistula.
It is, in fact, a hardened, dense, white mass without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some called it a feather.  Sometimes it is moist, constantly discharging pus; sometimes the moisture may cease.  This moisture may be profuse or scant, thick or thin.
I have previously stated in the classification of surgical injuries that any ulcer that does not heal or form flesh does so for one of nine reasons; one of which is either a deficiency of blood in the body, or poor quality of the blood, or a hard mass of flesh within or on the edges that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or, it is due to its being excessively harmful and dirty, or that the ulcer itself is foul, and the matter it discharges is of poor quality, or that the treatment is inappropriate, or to a widespread disease in the region, or to a specific condition of the region.
Or, it is due to its being excessively harmful and dirty, or that the ulcer itself is foul, and the matter it discharges is of poor quality, or that the treatment is inappropriate, or to a widespread disease in the region, or to a specific condition of the region. As is seen in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors slow by nature, or because of a single or multiple bones present.  I have explained all these causes, their signs, and treatments with medicines in the classification.
Here I must inform you about the treatment of boils and colds that occur through manual work.  Know that boils can occur in all parts of the body, some of which terminate in large veins, arteries, or nerves.
or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the multiple-part joints like the hand and foot, and some lead near to a major organ, and the like.
Those boils that are of this nature are among the difficult-to-cure diseases that do not accept treatment, and working on them is troublesome and ignorant.  If there are boils that do not reach the mentioned locations, and I hope and expect to cure them...
If you wish to understand this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the boil runs straight, probe it with that. If the boil has a bend, probe it with a thin lead probe, because lead softens the body.
It will slide when entering and bend towards the curve.  If the boil has many openings that you cannot identify with a probe, puncture one opening. Then the moisture you inject will follow to the other openings and drain out.
Then, diligently investigate in any way possible to ascertain if there is bone or nerve, if the bottom of the boil is distant or near, or if it is a single boil with multiple openings.  Determine all of this to the best of your ability by inquiring and noting the pain when your hand touches the area.
And similar to that are the signs and causes of the swelling.  If you have ascertained all of this truthfully, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  If...
If the boil is outwardly close or in a healthy area far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the locations I mentioned, then incise the boil as previously described and remove the hardened matter, corrupted flesh, and excess flesh from within.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its opening, until it heals. If the boil is deep and in a straight line, it should be incised as deeply as possible, then cleansed of all its corrupted flesh.
Then use a finely-spun thread, saturated with strong medicines, and insert it into the bottom of the boil, which you locate with the iron probe. Do this repeatedly until the strong medicine consumes any remaining corruption at the bottom of the boil.
Then dress it with ointments that promote healthy flesh growth until it heals.  If it does not heal with this, cauterize it according to the method previously described. If the cause of the boil is bone, and you are certain of this, probe and incise it as I instructed you, unless something prevents you.
If it is a vein, nerve, or major organ, as we said, and if the bone is exposed to you and has some corruption and darkness, then remove the corruption until it is gone, and then treat it with what will knit it together until it heals.  If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of the corruption.  Therefore, expose everything on it again, and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your effort, then dress it. If it heals, then well and good.  If not, know that the corruption is in a very deep cavity.  Expose everything on it again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, then pull it out with the finest forceps suitable for this.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove all of them, leaving nothing behind.  If any ligaments obstruct them, and cutting them poses no danger, cut those ligaments and any surrounding flesh, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones at that time, apply something to the wound that will putrefy it, and leave it for some days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones are putrid.  Keep the wound open; do not allow the opening you created during your work to heal.
If you fear that, place on it a cotton swab dipped in pulverized sulfur mixed with oil, or a cotton swab dipped in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments.  If those ligaments putrefy and the bones become loose, removing and pulling them out will be easy.
If it is a single large bone, such as the tibia or femur, and only its surface is corrupted, thoroughly scrape it until the blackness and corruption disappear, then close the wound. If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it is necessary to spread the wound.
Cut it all the way to where the corruption ends.  Then treat it until it heals.  I will tell you about a case of a man's leg that has a cold, to use as an example and help you in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years ago, a man experienced pain in his leg, caused by a movement within his body, until the materials reached the leg and formed a large swelling. This was not due to an external cause.  Then, time passed, along with the errors of the physicians, until the swelling opened.
A great deal of material flowed from it, and he was poorly treated until his leg became affected by a cold, and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and bodily humors. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled surgeons.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, with materials flowing from those openings in a great flood. The man's body had wasted away, and his complexion had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings, which led to the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them connected to each other from all sides of the leg.  I then cut open one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone, which I found corrupted, eaten away, blackened, and rotten, with holes that reached the marrow. I described what I discovered.
I removed the corrupted bone, which I believe was the only corruption in the bone that I had cut and described, and I believe that I had removed it completely. Then I used bonding medicines on the wound for a long period, but it did not heal.  Then I uncovered the bone again, further than the first time.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, and I described what I saw of that corruption as well.  Then I tried to close it, but it did not heal or join.  Then I opened it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempted to close it, but it would not heal, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
I removed it along with its marrow, and then closed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of work and opening was necessary because of the patient's weakness, their limited tolerance, and my fear for their life, since they were experiencing frequent fainting spells due to excessive vomiting.  He recovered completely.
Solid flesh grew in the place of the bone, and his condition in his body improved. His strength returned, and he acted normally.  No ailment affecting his walking ever troubled him.  If a bone in any part of the body had broken.
One should open it in this manner: Take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone.  Have someone pull it upward, and make another bandage of thicker wool than the first.
Then, tie it to the flesh beneath the bone, and pull its end to draw the flesh downward.  While exposing the flesh at the area to be opened, so the saw does not injure the flesh, place a board or plank under the bone from below, firmly.
For if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone.  The incision should be slightly above the corrupted area, so that there is no corruption within the bone's cavity, which would not be apparent to the senses, forcing you to reopen it again.
If the bone is corrupted but not protruding, and its parts are joined together, with the corruption within its center or a part of it, expose the flesh on all sides. Then place the board underneath, and open it from the side where the corruption is.
Once you've completed the incision, open it from the other side, and let the incision be a little distance from the corruption, as we have said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and open the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the corruption is in the hand or foot, the treatment is very difficult. However, you should remove the corruption in whatever way it appears and excise and clean it in any way you can.
By whatever method is possible for you, as long as you do not encounter a vein or nerve.  Know that the cuts and saws for cutting these bones are numerous, according to the position, setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and fragility of the bones. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a tool suited to each type of work.
Know that the work itself will indicate the type of tool you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for whoever is skilled in the craft and has seen many diseases. He may devise tools for each disease, and I am depicting several tools at the end of this chapter for you.
Make them examples to follow and measurements by which to judge others, if God wills.  Image of a saw:.

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the spreading of bones.
The limbs may decay either from an external cause or an internal one.  If you treat this decay with medicine and the treatment is ineffective, and you see the decay progressing in the limb, unchecked by anything.
Then you should amputate that limb where the decay has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or from a great calamity that is greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign of this affliction is that the limb turns black, so that you think fire has burned it.
Or it may decay after turning black, until the decay spreads to the part of the body next to that limb, and affects the whole body.  Therefore, act quickly to amputate it.  Also, if the cause of decay is a bite from some venomous creature, such as a sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, or similar things.
If the decay or bite is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger, and do not delay until the decay spreads to the hand.  Similarly, if the decay has reached the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not delay until the decay spreads to the elbow joint of the arm.
Also, if it has reached the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow joint, at the joint itself.  If the decay progresses and you see it approaching the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder, as that will bring the patient's death.  Use other treatments to the extent possible.
Likewise, do this with the leg. If decay has reached the toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints. If decay has reached the instep, amputate the entire leg.  If it has ascended to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and submit the patient to death.  The method for amputating or spreading the limb is to tie a bandage at the bottom of the place you wish to cut, and tie another bandage above the place.
One servant stretches the lower bandage downward, and another servant stretches the upper bandage upward.  You yourself scrape the flesh between the bandages with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed.  Then it is cut or spread.  You should place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place, causing the patient excess pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the spot quickly, or apply some hemostatic styptics, then return to your treatment until finished.
Then bandage the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals.  I will tell you about a similar case, affecting a man's leg:  the same affliction I describe to you, which occurred on his leg with blackening and a burning sensation like fire.
This decay first appeared on his finger, eventually affecting his entire foot.  When the man saw the decay spreading through the limb, with the intense pain and burning he experienced, he had it amputated at the joint, and he recovered.
After a long time, the same decay appeared on his index finger. He came to me, and I applied the remedies I had prepared for the hand after cleaning his body.  But the decay did not stop, and continued to spread to the other finger.
Until the decay spread to his entire hand. He asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to restrain the decay, and fearing for his life from the amputation, because his strength was failing.  When he lost hope in me, he returned to his home.
I was later told that he eventually amputated his entire hand and recovered. I have related this story to assist in treating similar illnesses and as a guide for treatment.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject remedies into them.
If a fleshy tumor occurs in an organ and persists for a while, eventually bursting or rupturing, releasing all the matter within it, and leaving the area empty like a vessel.
The skin on it, like a tattered cloth, becomes thin, and the damage has not progressed to the extent that it has affected bone, nerve, or ligament.  For this reason, it is called an abscess, and not a fistula, unless the duration is prolonged, so that the damage affects one of these structures.
In that case, it is called a fistula or a sinus.  Its treatment is to cut away all of that skin, as it goes around, especially if it is thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it is not adhering to the area because of the damage.
If you expect the skin to adhere, and the damage has not reached that extent, and there is thick flesh in the skin, treat it with injections.  Observe the abscess. If it is large and the pus it produces has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and alum, equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey, then take what you need and dilute it with water and honey, inject it into the abscess, constrict its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract it by pressing.  Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor disappears.  If this medicine is unavailable, you may inject with ash water, which is prepared by taking the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed clean. If the patient is not responsive to the Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject him with water and honey in which some powdered alum has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed, because honey is apt to cleanse and wash, and syrup adheres to the abscess, especially if the syrup is somewhat astringent and dry.  If you do this several times and are certain that the abscess's corruption is gone, inject it with something that promotes flesh growth there.
Such as taking some of the palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and a binding syrup, or inject it with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have demonstrated in the treatise on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and does not accommodate the injecting tube.
Then widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or Syrian ointment inside it until it widens.  Likewise, if the opening is wide, sew its edges together, leaving only enough space for the injector to enter without excess.
Also, if the opening from which pus is flowing is elevated, incise it at the lowest point so that the pus drains downwards, because if pus is retained in the depths of the abscess, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot incise the abscess downwards as desired, then attempt to position the limb so that the pus flows out easily, as best you can, and ensure that no pus remains trapped within it.
As for the adhesive medicines that are applied to the abscess from the outside and how to apply them, take one of the adhesive ointments suitable to your needs, spread it on a linen cloth, ensuring the cloth covers the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut an opening slightly larger than the abscess's opening, spread some soft ointment onto another piece of cloth, sized to fit the opening, and place it in such a way that it is spaced away from the opening of the abscess so pus can drain easily. Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, the one on the abscess opening is removed periodically to allow the collected pus to drain, so you may observe whether the discharged pus is copious or scant, mature or immature.
Furthermore, examine the abscess site itself; does the patient feel pain, or not; is there swelling, or not?  If you continue your treatment in this manner and observe that the discharge from the abscess opening is minimal,
Know then that the abscess has adhered or is close to adhering.  In that case, treat it again tomorrow or on the third day.  Once you have treated the wound, replace the cloths with new ones and renew the ointment.  If, after a long period, thin pus is discharged from the abscess, do not despair of its adherence.
For its healing is near, because some people have slow wound healing, while others have rapid healing.  If, after many days, unripe pus drains from the abscess, know that it has not adhered.  If the abscess's adhesion is delayed,
Then increase the drying medicines, and let the medicines be moist in consistency but dry in strength, like the palm ointment, if more opobalsam has been added.  The most effective treatment is to use palm ointment.
It can also be treated by applying rose oil and sprinkling moderate, viscous, old wine over it, then mixing it with it and applying it.  A similar procedure can be used if palm ointment is unavailable:  use honey boiled until thickened, or take myrrh, licorice, and kender, powder them, and sprinkle them over the honey while it is on the fire. Then, apply it to a cloth and bind it to the abscess, or apply the thickened boiled honey, sprinkle the medicines on it, place the cloth over it, and bind it.
Long-leaved wormwood, iris root, kresne flour, and dittany, either individually or mixed, can also be used.  Grind and sift them, sprinkle them on the honey I described, and apply them.  If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its core.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it as a nasal tumor as previously described.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
In treating the bruised fingernail and the severed or superfluous finger, and the cleft joining of fingers.
The daḥs is excessive flesh that grows beneath the fingernail of the hand or foot, and possibly other fingers. If it persists and treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it rots and decays, consuming the base of the nail, possibly destroying it entirely, and possibly the decay reaching the bone, resulting in a foul odor, and the fingertip becoming broad and discolored, dark-grey.  If you treat it with the methods described in the previous sections, and your treatment is unsuccessful...
Then you must cut away all the superfluous growth from the nail with an instrument, and then cauterize the wound.  Cauterization is very effective in this case.  And if the bone is healthy and the nail also healthy...
If the corner of the nail had pressed the flesh inward, causing it to be pricked and irritated, then a thin strip of material should be placed under the corner of the nail that is pricking the flesh, and raise it upwards, and gently cut away that flesh.
Apply to the remaining flesh the burning, corrosive medicines until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals.  However, if the decay has affected the bone, you must cut away and remove the bone, for it will never heal as long as there is rotten bone within it.
If you see decay and erosion spreading through the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as previously described, and then treat it until it heals.  If the nail is struck or wounded, causing severe pain, you must first bleed the affected area.
Then, split the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique cut from top to bottom.  Be careful not to extend the cut to the flesh beneath the nail, as this will cause severe pain to the affected area and will cause an overgrowth of flesh in that place.  Then treat the affected area with something to relieve the pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bone in parts of it, or perhaps a nail.  Some parts may grow at the base of the joints of certain fingers.
Or it may grow on some of the finger phalanges.  Those that grow on the phalanges of the finger do not move, and those that grow at the finger joint may move.  Any purely fleshy part should be easily severed; you should cut it off at its base with a wide scalpel.
However, if it grows at the base of the joint, its removal is difficult. Avoid cutting it.  If it grows on the finger at one of the phalanges, you should first cut off the flesh in a circular fashion around the bone, then scrape the bone using one of the appropriate rasps for this purpose.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this often happens at birth, or from the healing of a wound, burn, or similar injury.  You should split that adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural state. Then place a piece of gauze or a perforated piece of cloth soaked in rose oil between them so that they do not quickly reunite, and separate them or place a thin lead plate between them until the area heals properly.
Likewise, if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the palm, split the adhesion as the shape of the limb allows.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting veins.
Veins are tortuous, thick vessels filled with dark, black blood, occurring in most parts of the body, but most frequently in the legs, particularly in the legs of travellers, laborers, and carriers.  Therefore, one should first use vigorous rubbing of the body to remove the black fluid.
Then, bleed the affected person with a lancet.  Treatment with an iron instrument has two types: one is to cut and remove the black blood; the other is to cut the vein and remove the entire vein.  As for cutting, the leg should first be soaked in warm water, indeed.
Until the thick, cloudy blood dissolves.  Then, the leg of the patient is tied with a bandage from above the thigh to below the knee.  Then, the vein is cut in one, two, or three wide incisions.
Then, the black blood is extracted by hand from the bottom of the leg upward and from the top downward, until a sufficient quantity of blood, or as much as the patient's strength can tolerate, is removed.  Then, the patient is bandaged and instructed to avoid foods that generate black blood, and emesis and bleeding are repeated.
When the veins become full and this harms the patient, as for vein cutting, it is done in this manner: the patient's leg is shaved if it has much hair, then the patient enters a bath and the leg is soaked in hot water until it turns red and sweats, or the patient engages in strenuous exercise.
If a bath is unavailable, to heat the limb, the skin is then cut alongside the vein lengthwise, either near the knee or lower down at the heel.  Then, the skin is opened with hooks and the vein is dissected from all sides until it is visible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it is red and glistening.  Once freed from the skin, it also resembles a tendon.  Then, a blunt probe is inserted beneath it until it rises and emerges from the skin, at which point it is secured with a smooth, blunt hook.  Then, another incision is made near the first, roughly the width of three fingers.
Then, dissect the skin away from the vein until it is exposed. Then, lift it with the probe, as was done before, and secure it with another hook, as was done initially.  Then, make another incision, or several incisions, if needed.
Then, cut and sever it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull and dissect it until it emerges from the second incision. Then pull it towards the incision above, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, above all the incisions.  Once it is all out, cut it.
If pulling and dissecting do not work, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread, tie it and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist your hand in all directions until it comes out.  Be careful not to break it, as if it breaks, it will be very difficult to dissect it further.
This can cause harm to the patient.  Once you have dissected it entirely, apply wool soaked in a potion and rose ointment or oil to the wound and treat it until healed.  If the varicose vein is tense and has twists and turns in different directions.
If it is not straight, as we said, then you should make an incision on each twist and bend, then secure it with hooks until you have dissected it entirely.  Be careful when making incisions to avoid cutting or injuring the vein, as it will make it difficult to dissect and preserve your efforts.
Image of the incision used for dissecting the varicose vein.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a bend like other hooks, nor should its tip be sharp, so as not to injure the vein.  Its bend should be thick and smooth, for if it is thin, it will cut the vein with its thinness.  Instead, it should have a degree of thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-one.
In the treatment of the civilian vein.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely fertile countries.  It may also arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, resulting from a suppuration that occurs beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and the abscesses of gourd and the worms that develop between the skin and flesh.  A sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is that in the leg there will be intense inflammation, then the area will swell, and then the vein will begin to emerge from the location of that swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal.  If its tip appears, then a small piece of lead weighing one to two dirhams should be wrapped around it, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it outwards, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows large, cut off a portion and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it from its origin before it is all expelled, because if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a swelling, suppuration, and a foul ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it is necessary to gently draw it out gradually until it is completely expelled, leaving no part of it in the body.  In some people, this vein can grow to a length of five or ten cubits.
I have heard that it has reached twenty cubits in length in one man. If it breaks off during treatment, insert a reed into the hole and push it deeply into the body to empty it of its material, and try to heal the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint or the leg itself, forming many openings, and a branch emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the classification and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On the abscess of the worm generated under the hoof, known as the affliction of the cattle.
This disease is called in some of our countries the affliction of cattle, because it often affects cattle. It is a single small worm that develops between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, moving up and down.
It is felt as it crawls from one part of the body to another, until it penetrates the skin at a point, coming out and forming an abscess from the putrefaction of some fluids, as occurs with worms, snakes, and the gourd-like tumor in the abdomen.
Its danger lies in the possibility that, if it crawls through the body and reaches the head and the eye, it may burst through it and emerge, thereby destroying the eye. This often occurs.  Therefore, if one wishes to treat it and remove it, this should be done when it is crawling and visible to touch.
One should tie firmly above and below it with a strong bandage, then cut open and remove it.  If it has burrowed into the flesh and cannot be found, apply a cauterization with fire to the spot until it is burned out.  The greatest danger is its potential to damage the eye, as we mentioned.
If you see it near the eye in the head, tie firmly below it on the forehead, then cut open and remove it.  The patient should maintain the purification of his body with medicines that loosen putrid and bad fluids, and be cautious of foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-three.
Concerning the incision on the disease known as the "naفر".
This disease, called the nafr in our country, is a pain that appears in some organs, then moves from one organ to another.  I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a woman in distress in some remote area. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in the vein of the arm. After an hour, I saw that swelling creeping up the forearm, like a worm ascending towards her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain disappears from that spot and settles in the shoulder. Then, after an hour, it ran throughout the rest of the body until it was in the other arm, and she recounted to me that it was circling her entire body as I observed.
I was amazed by the speed of its transfer from one organ to another.  Before, I had only heard of this disease affecting organs in this way, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I had seen it in this woman.
I attributed this only to the fact that the woman was from the desert, lean of body, with exposed veins.  Therefore, this moving sensation was apparent to the touch.  It would not appear in a similar way in those of affluence, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels the pain, if it appears to you visibly as we have described, then firmly press above and below it with urgency, and cut open the area until that congested sensation comes out.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and dispersing the air, such as with rotten bean or skabing bean and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the locations in the body where they are lodged.  As for their variation by type, there are large and small arrows, hollow and solid glass arrows, and those with three or four corners.
Some have tongues, others have splinters.  As for those located according to the organs they strike, they are of two kinds: either the organs are major, hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow strikes one of these organs, and signs of death that I will describe to you later appear, you should avoid extracting the arrow, for death will most often befall the person. If these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not deeply embedded in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Signs of an arrow striking the brain, piercing the bone, and wounding the membrane covering the brain include severe headache, swooning, and dizziness.
If these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not deeply embedded in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Signs of an arrow striking the brain, piercing the bone, and wounding the membrane covering the brain include severe headache, swooning, and dizziness. Redness in the eyes and tongue, convulsion, confusion of mind, vomiting, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears; perhaps speech and voice fail, and a white, porridge-like moisture, resembling the watery part of meat, may flow from the wound.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow, if it has not already been extracted.  As for the signs of an arrow striking the heart, and if it is near the left breast, and felt as if it had lodged in something solid, not hollow.
Perhaps the arrow's movement resembles the pulse, and dark blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting; know then that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels around the neck swell, the patient's complexion changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking to inhale cold air.  If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be close to the small ribs, and the breathing will be heavy, accompanied by severe pain.
He sighs and all the shoulder muscles move.  If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness flows from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may flow from the wound.
His condition is evident. If the arrow strikes the abdomen, pierces it, and something from the bowels, or the intestines, or the intestines themselves, have been punctured flow from the wound, then there is no hope for treatment or for removing the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine flows from the wound.
If something protrudes from the wound and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed. As for the other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are usually unharmed.
Unless the arrow hits an artery or nerve, and unless the arrow is poisoned, I am informing you of some things I have observed concerning these arrows so you may use them as a guide in your treatment. An arrow struck a man's eye socket at the base of his nose.
I removed it from the other side, beneath the ear cartilage, and he recovered without any harm to his eye.  I also removed another arrow for a Jew, which had lodged in the cartilage of his eye beneath the lower eyelid. The arrow had largely disappeared, with only its small tip, which adheres to the shaft, remaining.
It was a large arrow, from a composite bow, with a square, smooth iron head, lacking ears. The Jew recovered without any further eye problems. I also removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and it was an Arab arrow.
It had two ears, and I split it open between the windpipes.  It had penetrated his throat, and I carefully worked on it until I extracted it. The Christian recovered.  I also removed another arrow from a man's abdomen, and we believed he would die from it.
After about thirty days, or thereabouts, and nothing had changed in his condition, I split open the arrow and worked on it, extracting it. He recovered and no further problems arose. I saw a man with an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
However, after seven years, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh.  I saw a woman who had an arrow lodged in her abdomen. The wound had healed over it, and the arrow remained. No change in her condition occurred, and she experienced no hindrance in her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow lodged in his face. The wound had closed over it, and he experienced little pain.  Many such cases exist.  I removed an arrow from a Sultan's commander who had an arrow lodged in the middle of his nose, slightly tilted to the right.
The arrow had completely disappeared. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow entered.  I found the arrow wound very narrow. I probed it with a fine probe but felt nothing. He was experiencing a prickling and pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped this prickling was from a part of the arrow.  I dressed the area with a dressing to draw out and ripen the spot, hoping the area would swell and the arrow's location become apparent, so I could then cut into it.  However, nothing happened to indicate the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued to dress the wound for many days, but nothing happened.  Eventually, the wound healed completely.  The patient remained hopeless about removing the arrow for a while.  Until one day, he felt the arrow inside his nose.  He told me of this. I then applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it.  I felt a thin arrowhead stuck to the wood. I continued to enlarge the wound with that strong medicine until the arrowhead became visible.  This took approximately four months.
Then, when the wound had widened and I could insert forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it wouldn't come out.  I continued to manipulate it with various instruments until I grasped it with firm forceps, as will be illustrated later in the chapter.
The physicians had judged that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it.  The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely, suffering no further harm. I am telling you how to remove certain arrows so that this will serve as an example and guide for what I have not yet described.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped through description nor encompassed by a book.  Instead, the skillful craftsman draws parallels from the few to the many, from what is present to what is absent, and invents new methods and instruments to deal with unusual cases.
Concerning this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the members where they lodged in two ways: either by pulling them from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, emerging from where they entered.
If the arrow is protruding into fleshy tissue, it should be pulled out.  If it cannot be removed immediately, you should leave it for several days until the surrounding flesh has become inflamed, making its removal easier.  The same applies if it is lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for several days and try pulling and moving it daily.  If it still does not come out after a few days, you should carefully pierce around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine drill to widen the space for the arrow, then pull it out.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull, penetrating one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms I previously mentioned appear, do not pull the arrow out. Leave it for several days until the situation improves.
Proceed with pulling out the arrow. If it is deeply lodged and cannot be removed, use drills around the arrow as described earlier. Then treat the wound until it heals.  If the arrow is concealed in some part of the body... If it is hidden from the sense, search for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with appropriate instruments. If you cannot reach it due to the narrow wound and the arrow's deep penetration, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, ...
Cut it open to widen the wound, and then remove the arrow. If the arrow has two barbs, grab them. Remove the flesh caught on them from every side by any means you can.  If you cannot remove the flesh by breaking the barbs or twisting them, ...
When attempting to remove the arrow from any location, use your hand and forceps in all directions to extract it.  Be extremely gentle so that the arrow does not break, making it harder to remove. If you cannot remove it within a reasonable time...
Leave it for several days until the surrounding flesh around it decays. Then try again; it will be easier by then.  If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned in the relevant section, and be cautious to avoid cutting any vein, nerve, or tendon. Use ingenuity.
By any means possible, remove the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly, as described.  When pulling the arrow, the patient should be positioned as they were when the arrow struck, which is the optimal position.
If this is not possible, use whatever means are possible.  As for an arrow exiting from the opposite side, either something will be protruding outwards, or you will find the tip of the arrow near the surface of the skin and see it protruding.
Cut the skin over it, keeping the incision to the size of the forceps. Then pull it out.  It will be easier to remove. If it is lodged in bone, rotate your hand, so that it breaks the bone and creates space for the arrow.  Then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for several days and try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still in place, push it out with it.  If the shaft has fallen out, and you want to push it out, insert a hollow instrument into the arrow's barb, and then push it out with that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits inside the hollow.  The arrow will be easier to remove that way. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the affected flesh, if possible, and then treat it appropriately.
If the arrow is in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, cut and be careful not to sever a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps used to remove arrows:.
