Its tips are like a bird's beak, crafted like a rasp so that when it grasps an arrow or anything, it does not release it.  Large, small, and medium sizes are made, depending on the size of the arrow, the size of the wound, and its width.
Image of the hollowed-out cannon:.
Image of the solid cannon:.
This is solid at the end, like a roller, to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-five.
On bloodletting.
The veins customarily bled in the body are thirty.  Of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *hasissin*; the two arteries in the temples, visible; and the two veins in the eye sockets, known as the *naazirin*.
The vein standing in the middle of the forehead; the vein at the tip of the nose; the two veins in the neck; the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth; the two veins in the lower lip; these veins, known as the *jayhark*; and the two veins beneath the tongue.
And as for the veins that are bled in the arm and hand, they are five. One of them is the *qayfal*, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head.  The *akhl*, which is the middle vein, is a composite of a branch of the *basliq* and a branch of the *qayfal*; the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the *basliq*, which is located on the inner side, is also called the axillary vein.  The common people call it the vein of the abdomen and the cord of the arm. It is situated on the forearm, and where it attaches it is clearly visible above the thumb.  And the *asīlim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, with two branches.  In the leg and foot there are three veins: one beneath the knee on the outer side; the second, the saphenous vein, located at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the *nisa*, located at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their incision is beneficial for chronic colds, migraine, scabies, and chronic, troublesome head sores; and I will describe how to make their incisions.
To do this, shave the patient's head and rub its back in the area of the veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well. Then the patient's neck is bound with a turban until the veins appear behind the ears, in the low parts of the head. Probe them with your finger, and where you feel a pulse under your finger, mark the spot with ink.
Then take a scalpel, the kind known as a lancet, and insert it under the vein into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin, making a complete severance.  The length of the cut should be the width of two clasped fingers.
Or thereabouts, and release the amount of blood you desire.  Then tie them with ligatures and leave them until healed.  I have described their cutting and cauterization in the beginning of the book. As for the arteries in the temples, their incision is beneficial for chronic migraine, severe headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and intense, persistent eye discharge.
Their incision, as I describe to you, involves binding the patient's neck with a turban until the veins clearly appear and their pulse is evident under your finger. Then mark the spot with ink.  Thereafter, raise the skin above the vein with your index finger.
Insert the lancet from below, lift the vein upwards, and sever it as you did with the other veins. Release the blood as needed. Then loosen the patient's neck restraint and place your finger on the vein for a moment.
Then place a compress and ligature on it, tying tightly from above. Leave it until it heals.  Their cutting and application have been previously mentioned in the beginning of the book.  As for the frontal vein, after incision of the cephalic vein, it is beneficial for chronic facial conditions such as sores, ulcers, and persistent redness.
Incision of this vein, as I describe to you, involves binding the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears. Then you take the instrument called the "axe" -- and this is its image:
Place the pointed tip of the axe on the vein, and strike from above with a chisel or similar object.  Allow the blood to flow as desired. Then release the patient's neck restraint, and secure a bandage to ensure healing.
It may be opened with a broad lancet, but it should not have a sharp tip like other lancets, rather it should be slightly broad-tipped.  Incision should be made in a slight oblique manner because the bone is close by; if the lancet is thin, it might break.
As for the veins located at the eye sockets, they are beneficial for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, and other ailments, as well as facial maladies.  Their incision involves the patient binding their neck with a turban, while you stand over their head.  Make an incision, slightly oblique in a lengthwise direction, with a small, somewhat broad lancet.
If the area has little flesh, a thin-tipped lancet might break.  Then, draw off the desired amount of blood, place a bandage over the veins, and secure them for one night.  Then, remove the bandage.  As for incision of the nasal vein, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
Severe headaches and facial ailments, such as the red swelling that appears on the nose, especially if chronic, may be treated by binding the patient's neck.  Then, hold the nose with your left hand and take a long, thin lancet.
Insert it into the middle of the nostril itself, between the nasal septum, in a straight line.  Since the vein is not perceptible there, blood will emerge immediately.  You should push the lancet in slightly and draw off the desired amount of blood.  Then, bind it for a night, as it will clot quickly.
As for the veins on the sides of the neck, their incision is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic skin conditions such as dark vitiligo, scabies, and foul ulcers, and sores.  Their incision involves the patient tying a bandage around their neck beneath them, while the surgeon stands over the patient's head.
The patient sits in a chair, then the vein is incised lengthwise, with a somewhat wide incision.  Then, drain the moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed.  Then, repeat the process with the other vein.  Then, loosen the bandage.
Apply a moderate degree of pressure to the veins to prevent suffocation, and leave them until the following day; the wound will heal.  As for the veins on the temples, their incision, after incision of the temporal vein, is beneficial for mouth ulcers, gum disease, foul sores, chapped lips, and foul sores.
Those located in and around the nose.  The method of incision is to have the patient sit facing you, and tie their neck with a turban.  Then, turn their lips and look for the veins; one will be on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and recognize them by their blackness.
Around them are also thin black veins, and cut them off completely. If this is unclear, and you do not know which veins are which, focus on cutting the largest and clearest ones.  Do the same with the veins on the lower lip.
As for the two veins beneath the tongue, incision after incision of the temporal vein is beneficial for obstructions in the throat caused by diseases of the tongue and mouth diseases.  The method of incision is to have the patient sit before you, facing the sun.  Lift the tongue and look under the tongue on one side, and on the other side, for a vein.  Their colour is towards black.
Incise them and be careful, do not cut them deeply.  Below them are arteries, and there is a risk of bleeding from these arteries.  The three veins that are incised at the elbow are those commonly used for bloodletting among people. Incision of them has two approaches.
Either by a wide, aromatic lancet, or an olive-shaped one, to the depth; or by a surgical knife, with a sharp edge—that is, lancing—[and this is its illustration].  Illustration of the wide, aromatic lancet:
It should be wide, as you see, in order to be suitable for opening the hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins, which contain thick, dark blood. This is the illustration of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening the thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood.  This is the illustration of the lancing lancet.
This lancing lancet, which is suitable for incision, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width or narrowness of the veins.  It can be used as a model for other similar tools, and is well-known among practitioners.
And as for the jugular vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the ailments that are under the throat and neck, towards the chest and abdomen. The phlebotomist, when opening it, must be cautious and have a sense of its location.  If there is an artery beneath it, an error and further probing of the lancet could cut the artery.
This results in blood leakage, so it should not be opened with a lancet used for probing, but rather incised with a lancing lancet. If the jugular vein is not clearly visible, one should avoid it and turn to another vein or look for a branch of it.
Or open the brachial vein, which is clear, and incise it with the lancing lancet, as we said.  If you want to open it directly, you should, before tightening the arm, palpate the area to identify the pulse location, then mark it with ink.
Then, the arm is tied, and the vein is incised with a deviating cut using the lancing lancet, as we said.  Be sure that the incision is made away from the artery.  If, when tightening the bandage, you see a bulge in the area you marked with ink, this bulge is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood during the venesection spurting like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood.  At that point, quickly place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, often it will stop, then tighten the arm.
Leave it alone, and warn the patient not to neglect it. It should be placed on a pillow and not moved for several days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and you are unable to obtain a remedy quickly, cut off the artery if it is exposed.  Its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, and use one half and firmly bind it to the vein location with bandages and splints until the next day.  If the bleeding stops, good. If not, treat it with the previously mentioned methods of applying cautery to stop the bleeding.  Stopping the bleeding is not difficult in most cases.
Because the wound is small and the bandage is secure on the arm, be aware of this.  As for the black vein, the benefit of its venesection is to draw blood from the top of the head and the lower part of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we said.
The surgeon who performs the venesection should be careful to place the venesection on a pillow, for beneath it are nerves.  If the lancet penetrates too deeply and hits a nerve, numbness will result that is difficult to treat, and possibly will not heal at all. This nerve is often perceptible by touch.
If in some individuals it is hidden and delicate, not clearly visible, one should make a shallow incision with a lancet and avoid the nerve as much as possible.  If the vein is between two nerves, incise the vein lengthwise.  As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its venesection is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein especially, if you wish to perform venesection, use the olive-shaped lancet or the broad, broad-leaved lancet, for it is the safest of all veins because there is neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when performing venesection, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft area.  If the desired site is not struck on the first attempt, it will not be harmed to repeat the venesection several times, although it may swell in some individuals if not performed on the first attempt.
But that swelling does no harm.  Concerning the method of venesection, its effects, and what should be done to remedy them, first it must be understood that venesection is used either to preserve health alone.
And it is used in illnesses.  The venesection for preserving health and maintaining it, and preventing illnesses, should be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely, the cephalic, the basilic, and the median cubital vein.  It should be performed at the beginning of spring, if signs of fullness appear.
Venesection should be performed on a Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours of daylight have passed.  Venesection used for illnesses, however, has no specific time; it is performed when needed, regardless of whether it is day or night.
And at any hour and at any time.  Children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years of age. If someone intends to perform venesection for any reason...
He should cleanse his bowels before venesection with a mild enema if there is much accumulated phlegm, so that foul matter from the intestines will not be drawn into the veins during venesection, harming the principal organs.  The hypochondriac, the drunkard, and the intoxicated should not be bled until these conditions have subsided.
One should also avoid venesection following dysentery, vomiting, diarrhoea, excessive sexual activity, exertion, exercise, late nights, fasting, and anything that weakens the body or mind.  Then, one should consider thinning the humours beforehand if the blood is thick.
Through foods, drinks, and medications, if possible.  Then, he should take a bath, if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise, so that the blood may become thin.  He should perform the venesection in the middle of the day, as we have said, and he should try to clear his mind of all bad feelings that day.
He should avoid worries, anger, and fear, and all bodily ailments such as excessive fatigue and exhaustion, sexual activity, and the like.  He should prepare his environment with items commonly used by people—perfumes, flowers, and amusements—to the extent possible for each individual.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the one the patient sits on.  The phlebotomist then strokes the arm twice or thrice.  Then he tightens the tourniquet with the ligature and twists it twice or thrice, and the tightening should be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightening prevents the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then, after tightening, the patient should rub his hands together.
Until the veins swell and are visible to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially.  Then he places his left index finger on the same vein slightly below the point where he intends to draw blood, so that the vein does not move and avoid the blow. For some veins are like tendons, moving when lanced, and some are filled with air; when the lancet is placed on them, they sink below the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein. If it does open it, it is a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be cautious and meticulous in all of these matters.  Then he lowers the lancet. If the vein opens at that time, then [he should continue]. Otherwise, he returns to the same spot a little lower or higher, quickly, if the spot has not become swollen.
If the patient swells or feels pain, leave it for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, as it might cause a hot swelling.  He should not enter a bath. Then he can repeat the procedure if he wishes. If the lancet pierces and the opening is small, and the blood flow is thin.
And if you fear that the amount of blood will not be sufficient, return to the same hole with the lancet gently and in a straight line, increasing the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the area becomes swollen. For many people, a small opening can cause swelling.
If you see that it has swollen, do not return to it at all.  It will not help you. Apply some clarified oil to it.  It facilitates blood flow and is better in this situation than plain oil or other ointments.
Use clarified oil in all your phlebotomies when blood flow is difficult.  This remedy, and the *shakhznaya*, can also do this, if one of them is applied to the spot.  The blood will thin and liquefy if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the phlebotomy, which often happens, especially for someone who has not been bled before or if the vein opening was small, quickly apply a sponge soaked in warm salt water to the swelling and bind it for an hour.  It will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood has completely exited the vein itself or another vein.  If, after a few days, there remains some discoloration (blackness or greenness) at the site, it will not harm. If desired, apply some dissolved myrrh and aloes.
Or some of the juice of the *fūdanj* and the like.  A swelling and protuberance often occurs when bleeding the basil. If you place your hand on it, and find that it trembles when you touch it, that is a bad protuberance; beware of applying any of the things we have mentioned.
For it may bleed arterial blood, but bandage it with something that will constrict to firm the area, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  It is necessary to remove blood from each person in proportion to their strength and the predominant color of their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red.  Likewise, if you see it thick, allow it to flow until it becomes thinner, and if it is sharp, until its sharpness diminishes.  For those who are strong and full and need to remove the blood in one go...
To widen the vein incision, and have the lancet be broad; for the weak, the opposite.  It is necessary to remove it in stages and have the puncture narrow.  The best tool for vein incision is one that is slanted and oblique, a cut rather than a puncture.
This type of bleeding is free from hemorrhage and nerve damage.  It is better and safer than bleeding across or lengthwise.  If someone is prone to fainting during bleeding, it is necessary to give them something to eat beforehand, like bread soaked in pomegranate juice or sherbet.
If he is free from illness and the blood is removed in three or four stages, if his temperament is cool, let him eat bread soaked in myrrh drink or honey drink flavored with herbs or sweet, fragrant drink.
If fainting occurs during bleeding, and the cause is excessive blood loss, it is necessary to give him meat broth and a thin, fragrant drink.  Use fragrance with galbanum, and gently wash his chest with it.  Use all the methods mentioned in the section on fainting.
For those who are bleeding due to excessive vomiting, and whose strength is weak, if they wish to release their arm's blood again, it is necessary to gradually release the blood, little by little, according to their strength, over consecutive days.
If someone wishes to release their arm's blood a second time and their body is strong, they should do so after seven or nine hours from the first bleeding.  If someone wishes to draw blood from their body to the opposite side from which it initially flowed, this should be done on the second or third day.
If a person has a large amount of hot, inflamed blood in their body, causing fever, it is necessary to remove a large quantity of blood in one single session, widening the opening until fainting occurs, after ensuring all the conditions for bloodletting are met. Place your hand on their pulse while the blood flows to prevent death instead of fainting.  This often occurs if the leeches are not careful and inattentive. This should not be done when intending to release the arm's blood a second time.
If the vein opening closes and blood flow is difficult, do not forcefully prod or twist it; this is very harmful.  Instead, either leave it until it can be opened again, or use a lancet blade to remove the clotted blood from the vein opening.
Or apply something dissolved in water, like salt, or a potent antidote (like theriac or shekhsnaiya), and gently prod the vein until the blood flows. If the vein has swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If further bloodletting is absolutely necessary, either open a new vein above the affected area, or bleed from the other arm or a different vein.
For bleeding the arm's vein, use the arm's vein instead of the vessels in the eye or temples, if those are not available or difficult to locate, since it is a combined vein from those. To perform this procedure, the patient should immerse their hand in warm water until the forearm turns red and the vein becomes clearly visible; then the vein is gently constricted with a bandage.
Then, make a small incision across the vein, not lengthwise or outward, and make a wide opening.  Incise just above the wrist joint.  If blood flow is difficult, return the arm to the warm water basin and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you've achieved your desired amount.  If it's summertime, you may be able to avoid returning the arm to warm water.  Most often, placing the arm back in warm water is used in winter.  Bleeding from this vein is the safest of all veins. Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it.  Bleeding from the vein in the right forearm is helpful for liver ailments.  To perform this procedure, the wrist is bound with a bandage or your hand after the arm is immersed in warm water until the vein swells.
Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it.  Bleeding from the vein in the right forearm is helpful for liver ailments.  To perform this procedure, the wrist is bound with a bandage or your hand after the arm is immersed in warm water until the vein swells. The vein is very clearly visible. Then, make a slight incision across the vein.  Even if you cut it completely, it will not harm him.  Be careful; do not press too hard with the lancet, as there is a nerve to the fingers beneath it and the area is devoid of muscle. Then return the arm to warm water.
Leave it [the arm] to continue bleeding.  If you do not return it to warm water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and stop flowing.  Once you have extracted the desired amount of blood, apply a mixture of grease and salt to the vein to prevent it from closing quickly.
Likewise, this procedure should be followed for all constricted veins.  Bleeding from the left arm is beneficial for spleen ailments.  Do the same procedure as you did with the other vein.  As for bleeding from the saphenous vein, it is beneficial for illnesses in the lower body, such as uterine ailments.
And for menstrual irregularities, kidney ailments, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.  The procedure for bleeding from the saphenous vein is to immerse the leg in warm water, and apply massage until the veins become prominent.  Then, bind above the joint of the leg with a bandage.  The vein is located near the heel, roughly at the position of the thumb.
From it [the saphenous vein] many branches spread throughout the leg.  Make the incision on the widest branch, or near the heel where it joins, as this is the best and safest approach.  If you incise on the front of the leg, be cautious of the nerves below the surface on the front of the leg.
Make your incision by deflecting the vein, as if you are trying to sever it.  The lancet should be sharp.  If bleeding is difficult, return the leg to warm water and allow the blood to flow until it is emptied.  If the surgeon misses the vein on the first attempt.
If the surgeon misses the vein on the first attempt, then return to a point slightly higher on the vein.  The area is safe, and there is no danger, as long as the nerve is avoided, as we have mentioned.  The same procedure applies to the saphenous vein of the other leg.  As for the vein of Venus, its location, as we stated, is at the heel, on the outer side.
Its [the saphenous vein's] benefit is for hip pain if it is due to hot blood.  The procedure for bleeding is to have the patient enter a hot bath, then rapidly tighten the leg from the hip to above the heel with a thin, long bandage.  It will only appear then.
If it appears, then open it in any manner; either by deflecting it (which is best), or by cutting it off completely or by cutting it in half.  The location is safe.  In most people, it is extremely hidden.  If you do not find it.
If it does not appear at all, then open some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot near the little and ring fingers.  Be careful of the nerves and release the amount of blood you desire.
Then release the bandage, place a compress on the incision site, and bandage the area.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-six.
On Cupping and its Use.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass. Cupping can be done in two ways: one by incision and bloodletting, and the other without incision.
This cupping without incision is of two types.
And the cupping vessels of the nape of the neck, the two sides of the neck, the chin under the lower jaw, the shoulders, the coccyx at the base of the spine, the elbows in the middle of the arms, the legs, and the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the fine veins spread throughout the flesh.  Therefore, it does not weaken the body as much as venesection, and cupping, whether by fire or otherwise, should not be used in any disease arising from fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If cupping is needed due to illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning, end, or middle of the month.
For some people, an excess of blood requiring cupping causes heaviness and headaches.  Others experience fullness, redness in the face, head, and neck. Some experience itching on the face, temples, and darkening, and heaviness in the eyes, and some experience itching at the cupping site.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouths, and have gums bleeding and spitting of blood. Others sleep excessively and see blood, redness, slain people, and wounds, and similar things in their dreams.  When we see any of these things...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed of the day.  The benefit of cupping the temple is in relieving heaviness in the head and water that flows to the eyes.
However, this should be done after the body has been emptied. This cupping can be a substitute for lancing the veins.  It should be avoided by those with a cold head or a cold, as it will do them great harm.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly, or those with cold diseases in their heads, and those who habitually use it may develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cup slightly to avoid the development of forgetfulness.  As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for lancing the temples and lancing the neck.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, splitting of the breathing apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The shoulder cup should be raised slightly, because if lowered, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for pains in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, constriction, and toothache. It is a substitute for lancing the neck.  The cupper should be instructed not to insert the cup too deeply, lest they cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments. It may take the place of lancing the veins on the lips.  As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the forearm, it is beneficial for what is beneficial for lancing the three veins: the neck, temples, and the crook of the arm, because it draws blood from all the delicate veins in the flesh and draws these delicate veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press too hard, because the area is lacking in flesh and has nerves and arteries beneath.  As for a single cupping on the coccyx, it is beneficial for piles and sores of the lower parts.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, copper cup, as the area requires strong suction, and glass cups may break.  The cupping should be done vigorously.  As for cupping the legs, it diminishes fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, inducing menstruation, and beneficial for boils and abscesses.  It acts as lancing the safenous and saphenous veins, but it greatly exhausts the body and causes fainting in most people.  Cupping the saphenous veins is similar in benefit to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place the empty cup and apply moderate suction.  Do not prolong the application of the cups, but quickly place and quickly remove them to allow the humors to flow evenly to the area.  Repeat this process until the area reddens and opens.
When redness from the blood appears, then apply gentle pressure and resume suction gradually.  Then observe the condition of the body. For those with thin, porous flesh, a single pressure is sufficient, so that the area is not ulcerated.
The cupper should widen and slightly deepen the pressure, and adjust the suction gently and with slight movement. If the blood is thick, then it should be pressed twice. The first time to open a path for the thin, watery blood. And the second time to completely remove the thick blood.
If the blood is extremely thick, then the pressure should be repeated a third time to achieve the goal.  In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one pressure is sufficient.  If we want to remove a large amount of blood, we should apply more pressure.  If we judge the blood to be thick.
Therefore, a deep pressure should be applied. The moderate limit for pressure is just the depth of the skin. What ointments should be used when applying cupping, and what waters, and what the cupper should avoid?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin and tight pores...
The areas for cupping should be anointed with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments.  In summer, for example, the ointment of the Khari, or violet ointment, or sweet almond ointment, or pumpkin seed ointment.
And in winter, for example, narcissus ointment, or lily ointment, or chamomile ointment, or hyacinth ointment, and the like. If the phlegm is thick and cold, the ointment should be of hellebore, or bindweed, or frankincense, or rue, and the like.
If the cupper has large pores and soft flesh, then the use of ointment should be avoided.  These individuals should wash their cupping vessels after cupping with rose water, or cold water, or elderberry water, or pumpkin water, or rue water, and the like.  And for those whose blood is excessively moist...
They should wash their vessels with vinegar, or with rue and sumac water, and the like.  And those whose phlegm is thick should wash their vessels with aged wine, or hellebore water, or rue or chamomile decoction, and the like.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it should be performed one or two hours after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the leeches should take care of before and after the procedure.
First, one must observe if the cupper or leech is bilious, and if their blood is predominantly hot and inflamed.  Then they should consume coolings such as pomegranate, dandelion (with vinegar), endive, mulled wine, gelab, and the like.  Their food should include chickens, lamb (including the stews and stewed meats), and the like.
And if their temperament is cold, they should be given honeyed drinks and myrrh drinks or buzury mulled wine.  A moderate, fragrant wine, between old and new, should be consumed.  A reduction in food should be ordered, and their diet should consist of chickens, quails, sparrows, and pigeons (with a sauce).  On the day of cupping and bloodletting, drinks should be consumed more than food.
Sometimes, certain individuals should be given, before cupping or bloodletting, or afterwards,  a therapeutic antidote or musk medicine or sheeltha to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood.  This should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cupping without stipulations, these are cupping that are placed upon the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidneys, and the groin area.  Because these organs cannot tolerate stipulations, the intent is to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or we use it to dispel a cold wind that has gathered in an organ, such as placing a cupping on the abdomen and navel.  It loosens the organ, warms it, and relieves pain by dissolving that wind.
It can be placed on the kidneys if there is blockage or stones, because its strong suction may open the blockage or remove the stones from their position.  Similarly, it is done when placed on the liver and spleen, when wind affects them.  These cuppings may also be used empty, by suction alone.
It can be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water in cases of swelling.  This involves filling a large cupping with hot water alone, or water in which certain herbs suitable for this purpose have been cooked.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed.  This is the method of cupping using fire.
Its opening should be two fingers wide, as illustrated.  Its depth should be half a span.  On one side, near the middle, there should be a small hole, the size of a needle's insertion, made of thick-rimmed Chinese or yellow brass.
Smooth, flat, and polished so as not to injure the affected area when placed on it.  There is a transverse, central depression of brass or iron in the middle for placing the candle in the fire.  Such cupping vessels can be made larger or smaller than described, according to the illness.
The practitioner must understand the proper size.  Cupping for children and thin individuals is different from that for men and those with larger bodies.  The method of applying this cupping vessel with fire to the area is to light a wick made of tightly wound linen or a small candle of pitch and place it in the center of the hardened rod (in the center of the cupping vessel).
To allow the heat to rise from the bottom of the cupping vessel to prevent burning the patient.  Then place the cupping vessel on the affected area and hold the finger over the hole we previously mentioned, so that when we hold the cupping vessel, the finger does not need to be removed.
Steam will emerge through that hole, and the cupping vessel will be detached from the area.  Then light the wick again as needed.  However, the cupping vessel used for dropsy is without a hardened rod or hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the area only.  This is its form.
The larger the cupping vessel, the better it is, as it can hold more water.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On applying leeches.
Leeches are used primarily on areas where cupping cannot be applied, either because of their small size, like the lip, gums, and similar parts, or because the area is lacking flesh, like the finger and nose, and similar parts.
To use them, select leeches from clean, uncontaminated freshwater, then leave them in fresh water for a day and night to become hungry and empty their stomachs. Then the body is first bled by venesection or cupping, then the affected area is cleansed.
Until it turns red, then a leech is placed on it. When it is full, it will fall off.  If it is possible to draw the affected area with a cupping device, that is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze. If bleeding continues after the leeches fall off and it is a trickle, then soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Apply it over the area until the bleeding stops. If there is a lot of blood, sprinkle crushed alum, or cinquefoil, or similar astringents, over it to stop the bleeding, or place peeled halves of lentils on the area.
Leave it until the lentils adhere to the area, and the bleeding will stop. If reapplication of leeches is needed, and other leeches are available, do not use the same leeches again. If the leeches refuse to attach, cleanse the area with fresh blood.
Or, insert a needle into the area until some blood comes out, then place a leech.  If it senses blood, it will attach. When you want it to detach, sprinkle some powdered sorrel, salt, or ash on it, and it will fall off.
The Third Chapter.
On Fractures.
This chapter, too, is necessary for the practice of medicine, dealing with the setting of fractures and dislocations of bones.
Know, O my children, that many ignorant physicians and laypeople claim expertise in this chapter, those who have never studied the writings of the ancients on it or read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge is lost in our land, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner of it. However, I have gained what I have gained through extensive reading of the writings of the ancients and diligently understanding them until I extracted this knowledge. Then, I dedicated myself to practice and apprenticeship throughout my life.
I have presented to you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. And after I have brought it close to you and relieved it of prolixity, my experience has been applied to it, condensing it to the utmost brevity, clarifying it with utmost clarity, and illustrating for you many images of the instruments used in it.
This is an addition to the explanation, as I did in the previous two chapters. There is no power except with God, the Exalted, the Great.
The first chapter.
In it are summaries and collections of the matter of fractured bones, which should be presented before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one.  Before we begin to discuss the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary to present in the beginning of this chapter a collection of statements and sections. This will first compel you, and anyone else eager to learn this noble craft, to understand them and grasp their truth.
So I say, whenever a fracture, dislocation, injury, or fall occurs, one must first hasten to splint or reduce it, or both. Unless prevented by something like weakness, or the person affected was a child or an old man, or the time was extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, their diet should be restricted to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, fatty meats, and overeating are forbidden, along with any food that excessively fills the veins with blood, until the swelling subsides and there is no longer a concern of further fluid build-up at the site.
Then, the patient should return to their original diet, as was their custom. If the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should eat foods that are rich, thick, strong, and viscous, such as rice, barley, wheat, and beef stomachs. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and similar foods, will result in faster and better healing of the fracture. Know that fractured bones in strong men and the elderly cannot fully connect and heal to their original state.
Because of the dryness and hardness of their bones, some fractured bones, even those as pliable as the bones of young children, might still connect and heal.  However, nature forms a substance around the fractured bone, similar to glue, which is thick and causes the bone fragments to adhere and hold together.
This holds the pieces together until it reaches the original strength and solidity, so that nothing hinders its functions.
Therefore, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, viscous, and thick, as we have mentioned.  Know that the types of fractures vary according to the different body parts, for example, a fracture of the leg bone differs from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone differs from a fracture of the spine.
Likewise, all other parts of the body differ. We will describe each type of fracture in detail in its own section, and the type of bone fracture itself can also vary.
Because a fracture may be a simple break without fragments, or it may be a lengthwise break with fragments, detached and non-detached splinters.  The fracture may also occur with a wound and tear in the skin, or it may be a slight crack.
Each type requires a specific method of reduction, which will be described in detail in its proper place.  Signs of a bone fracture include its deformity, unevenness, visibility to the touch, and creaking sound when you gently tap it with your hand.
If there is no visible deformity, creaking, or discomfort when palpating the bone, and the patient does not experience significant pain, then there is no fracture, but possibly a strain, a minor fracture, or a slight crack.  Therefore, it is absolutely not advisable to move it by pulling or tapping it.
Instead, apply the appropriate medications, which will be mentioned later, to the area, and then gently bind it.  Know that if a bone breaks into two without fragments, each piece is separated from its counterpart.
You should immediately realign and straighten it before a hot swelling develops.  If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for several days until the swelling subsides, then gently realign it by any suitable means.
Know that reduction and realignment are easier when there are no fragments in the bone.  Then, it will be bound as described later. If there are fragments in the bone, the fractured limb (arm or leg) must be stretched from both sides.
Either by your hands, if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both ropes and your hands.  Position the limb on a level surface in its natural shape.  Once the fractured bone fragments have been stretched, carefully return the fragments to their proper positions by any method you can devise with gentleness.
Take great care to avoid causing the patient pain or discomfort in your actions.  Your effort should be to bring the two bone pieces together in the best possible alignment.  At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hands. If you find anything out of place, correct and adjust it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and forceful prodding, as many unskilled practitioners do.  Often, they cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb with their actions, as I have repeatedly observed.
After the realignment, careful treatment, and stretching, keep the limb still and quiet. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness or sleep, when turning over, or experiencing restlessness, and during bowel movements, and with all his movements to the greatest extent possible.  Ensure that the limb is held in a position that minimizes pain.
If, while the limb is held in that position, the patient feels pain or discomfort, he should move it to another position in which he does not feel pain.  He should ensure that this new position is straight and aligned.
So that no crookedness occurs in the limb if it is forced.  As for the method of stretching a fractured limb, it is as I describe to you.  Know that fractured limbs vary in size, condition, and configuration.  Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and similar parts...
The bandages should be soft and gentle for small parts like the arm and fingers.  For larger parts like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be firm and strong.  A wide bandage is needed for a large limb, to hold it firmly from all sides, without any gaps.
Then, after completing the alignment, apply the appropriate plaster. Begin by applying a soft splint to the fracture site.  Then, wrap the bandage around the fracture three or four times, depending on the limb's requirements.  Slightly tighten the bandage, and then move it up towards the upper part of the fracture.
Tighten the bandage less than you did for the fractured area, then move the wrapping away from the fracture site a little and gradually loosen the tension until a proper alignment is achieved.  Then, take another bandage and wrap it similarly over the fractured area.
Wrap the bandage further, moving towards the lower part of the fracture. Maintain the tension and looseness of the wrap as described for the initial, upper wrap.  Place soft splints or cloths between the layers of bandage to correct any bends or misalignments in the fracture.
If there is any misalignment, do not add anything; otherwise, apply another bandage. Then, if there is no swelling or inflammation in the limb, apply firm bandages over these layers.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to reduce the swelling and remove the puffiness. Allow it time to settle, then apply firm bandages, which can be fashioned from appropriately shaped and cut reeds or from ebony wood.
Made of pine, palm fronds, papyrus, or similar readily available materials,  each splint should be of the same size and shape.
However, the splint placed directly over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others. The length of the splint should be appropriate to the size of the fractured limb.  Image of splint:.
Then, apply another bandage over the splints, tightening it to the same degree as the first bandage.  Secure them from above with strong threads, ensuring the tightening is particularly strong over the fractured area. The tighter the bandaging, the closer to the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and not too thin, preferably made of damp flax.  Threads made from tightly twisted flax strips, as some unlearned individuals do, are a serious mistake.
Because this results in excessive tightening.  Thin threads are also unsuitable, as you cannot achieve the desired tension.  There should be at least a finger's width of separation between the splints.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening them properly, place soft padding or fluffy wool beneath them so that nothing causes discomfort.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin is broken, we will discuss that separately.
Know that not every fractured limb should be bandaged from the first day.  For a large limb, splints should not be applied until five or seven days, or even longer, depending on whether you are confident that inflammation will not occur.
This is a description of the bandages used by the ancients for fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It is a general, concise bandage suitable for fractures and adaptable to most temperaments, especially children and women, as it is neither excessively hot nor cold.
Take the flour dust, the residue from the millstones, and knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white. Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then dry it.
Another bandage for fractures, dislocations, and sprains is made from: ten dirhams each of mastic, gum ammoniacum, acacia, frankincense, and myrrh; five dirhams each of gum arabic and licorice; twenty dirhams of Egyptian frankincense; and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix with Egyptian frankincense water, or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  Then apply this bandage; it is well-suited, quickly sets broken bones, and is appropriate for most people due to its balanced nature.
Another bandage useful for fractures and sprains is made from: ten dirhams each of myrrh, mastic, and white hellebore; five dirhams each of gum arabic and licorice; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all together finely, sift, and knead with water or egg white, then apply.
A bandage for dislocated joints and bones, relieving the pain of such dislocations, bone fractures, and skull fractures:  take prepared wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area.  This bandage does not have the strength to set a fracture.
However, it is excellent at relieving hot swelling and pain in particular. Another bandage for setting broken bones: take the leaves of the fig tree and the leaves of the wild poppy, grind them both together, and apply as a damp compress.  A further, brief bandage is used when a fracture is displaced.
If you want to treat the remaining swelling, take root of hellebore, chamomile, violet, and caraway seed, all in equal parts. Grind together and mix with paint if the limb is not inflamed; if inflamed, mix with coriander water or water, and apply.
Another stronger resolving bandage, used when a hard swelling occurs following a displaced fracture, is made by taking equal parts of hellebore root, flaxseed, fenugreek, basil,  violet, and chamomile.
Grind all together and mix with vinegar water, or plain water, or paint, depending on the heat of the limb and the relief of its heat.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that this should be applied to a fractured limb when setting it.
However, the plaster made of wax and oil, nothing more, should be of medium thickness.  Galen believed that when setting a fractured limb, one should apply things with dryness and some heat, such as myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, and similar things.
A remedy for weakness and pain: take mugwort, chickpeas, shaved human hair or bird feathers, hellebore, and salt, all in equal parts. Grind, sieve, mix, and apply as a bandage.  As for the proper amount of remaining ligament...
Then, it should be resolved.  Observe if the patient experiences no pain, no rubbing, and no movement of the fractured bone from its position.  Do not dissolve it for many days.  However, if there is severe rubbing, disturbing pain, or swelling at the location,
Immediately dissolve it at once and do not delay. Remove the dressing. Then take a soft cloth or a damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the itching subsides and the pain ceases.  Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bandage it overnight to relieve inflammation and swelling and pain.  Then return it to a gentle bandaging and a light dressing.  Do not apply the initial tight bandage.
And care for it until it heals.  If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and swelling have completely disappeared, and you need a bandage and tension, then repeat what you did initially. If nothing mentioned previously occurs in the limb,
then do not dissolve it until after three, four, five, or seven days. It may sometimes take twenty days, all according to what appears to you from the condition of the limb as we have said, until the fracture is stabilized and the flesh heals over it.
Then, increase the tension of the bandage further than your original dressing and also increase the patient's nutritional intake as previously mentioned. If you see the fracture site has dried out and wasted more than necessary, understand that nourishment cannot reach it.
Then loosen it with lukewarm water each time you unwrap it, and let that be every three days. Reduce the bandage tension slightly. By doing this, nourishment will reach the limb and it will heal quickly.  As for what the ignorant among the bone setters do, by breaking the limb again...
For if it is not properly set the first time, and it is set crookedly, that is an error on their part and a great deception.  Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it. I have never seen any effect of that from any of them. The correct approach is not to use it.
Chapter Two.
In fractures of the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous and varied in form, and their causes are diverse. Some fractures are caused by sword blows, and in these cases, the bone may be completely broken, extending to the peritoneum beneath the bone. As the carpenter does with wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "carpenter's fracture".  Alternatively, the sword might only cut a part of the bone, breaking only its surface, without penetrating to the end, and this is called a simple fracture.  The wound of either type of fracture may be large or small.
Some fractures are caused by a blow or impact with a stone, or a fall on a stone or similar object.  This fracture may be penetrating, approaching the membrane under the bone, or it may be superficial, affecting only the surface of the bone.
The wound of these fractures may also be large or small.  Some fractures are hidden within the bone, a slight fissure or crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  A fracture can result from a fall or impact with a stone or similar object, causing a portion of the bone to penetrate inward, creating a depression, similar to how a blow to a copper object can cause a portion of it to sink inward.
This is most often seen in the soft skull bones of children.  All these types of fractures may have detached or non-detached fragments, and we will discuss the treatment for all of these in their respective sections, and we will identify all these types of fractures through examination.
Inspecting them with probes and removing any putrid flesh.  The hairline fracture is recognized by examining the bone, rubbing and staining it with ink, for the fracture will appear dark.  As for the treatment of the fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you observe evident symptoms of danger, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bloodshot and red eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach or treat the patient, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for his recovery, then take action in his treatment. This is so that if the injured person comes to you at the very beginning of the injury, and that was during the winter months...
In any case, you should strive to remove the bone before the 14th day. If it is during the summer months, you should hasten to remove the bone before the 7th day, before the membrane beneath the bone deteriorates, causing the symptoms we have described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is a crushing and contusion, then you should cut away the crushed and contused portion, as I am describing to you—that is, shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the shape of the wound and the patient's tolerance. If, during the exposure, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, address it appropriately.
This is done by filling the area with cloths soaked in rose-water and ointment.  Clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding is controlled.  Then, begin to manipulate and extract the bone.  This can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a narrow, delicate incision.  This is how it is done:
Then, use another cut, slightly wider than the first.  This is how it is done:
Then use yet another cut, wider than the second.  You should have several differently-sized incisions, some wider and some narrower, with extremely sharp edges.  They should be made from Indian steel or good quality wrought iron.
Use gentleness when striking the incision to avoid disturbing the head and injuring it.  If the bone is strong and hard, it is advisable to pierce around it with a  "non-immersive" drill before using the incisions.
Because it does not pierce beyond the skull's bone, since the drill has a rounded edge below its sharp head, like a ring or small circle, which prevents it from sinking in and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that for every skull, you have a drill whose sharp tip is the correct length and width for the thickness of that skull.  Here is an image of three types. Of the drills, large, medium, and small.
As for how to pierce around the fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated.  Then move the drill to another spot, placing it at a distance equivalent to the thickness of a rod or similar.
Then, use the chisels to cut between each pair of holes.  Do this with as much gentleness as possible, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with another instrument you have prepared for that purpose.
Such as splints and delicate forceps.  You must be extremely careful that the drill or chisel does not touch the periosteum.  If you have smoothed the bone and separated it from the periosteum, if it was attached, and removed it, you should then strip it.
And smooth any roughness remaining on the bone with another instrument similar to a chisel, but it should be thinner and gentler than the other chisels. If any small fragments or splinters of bone remain, remove them gently with the instruments you have.
Then treat the wound afterward with the compresses and ointments that are mentioned.  As for the other aspect of the procedure, it is truly easy, free from danger; Galen mentioned it and praised it highly.  This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the section of bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that section, position the tip of this lens-shaped chisel beneath it. This is its image/form:.
The lens-shaped portion should be smooth, not cutting anything. The sharp portion of the chisel should be along its length, as you see, so that the lens-shaped portion rests against the periosteum and the sharp edge of the chisel faces the bone. Then strike the chisel from one side.
With a small hammer, so that all the bone is cut gently, as it turns, and you are safe from injuring the membrane, no dangerous accident will occur at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of men, yes, even if he were asleep.
If any membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lens-shaped chisel itself, and remove it gently; it will come away without harm or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not penetrate the membrane, and the broken surface of the bone remained rough and with slivers, it is necessary to remove that roughness and those slivers with delicate instruments, different sizes, so that you can use the most appropriate one for each place according to the requirements of the work.
The bone, fractured and shaped as it is, you should use the widest of these instruments first for scraping the bone, then use a finer one after that, and continue in this manner until you reach the use of the finest and most delicate of them all.
As for the other small, hairline fractures and subtle breaks, each should be treated according to what will restore it to soundness. This is something that is not hidden from anyone who has even a minimal skill in this craft and has paid attention to what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, dip it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening.  Then take another cloth, folded or triangular. Dip it [the second cloth] in rose water and oil, place it on the first cloth, and do this with the utmost gentleness to avoid weighing down the peritoneum.  Then, use a wide bandage over it, tightening only enough to hold the wound.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two, until the hot swelling subsides.  Then, remove it and use thereafter the drying remedies such as iris root, quince flour, pulverized kender, and zarand, and similar ones.  These remedies should be prepared as poultices.
And sprinkle them [the remedies] on the wound, dry as they are. In general, use a remedy that cleanses without stinging.  Be very diligent in treating the wound to ensure it is clean, free of grease, grime, or pus.  Do not allow pus to accumulate within it at all.
Because if pus accumulates on the brain membrane, it corrupts and rots it, causing great harm to the patient.  This may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and if the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is certainly doomed. If the discoloration was caused by a remedy applied to it, and that remedy had the power to produce such discoloration.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, thoroughly mix them, and apply them to a cloth.  Then place this cloth on the peritoneum.  Then treat it with the appropriate types of treatment until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a fractured nose.
Know that the only parts of the nose that can fracture are both or one of the upper cartilages, because they are bones.  The lower part is cartilage and cannot fracture, but it may be bruised, bent, or perforated.
If one of the cartilages is fractured, you should insert the little finger into the nostril.  Straighten the fracture from inside with your index finger and from outside with your thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape.  Be gentle in your actions, taking care not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be straightened with a rounded instrument of moderate thickness. If the fracture is in both sides, do likewise.  The fracture should be treated on the first day after it occurs.
If possible, or else after seven or ten days when the acute swelling has subsided, introduce a thread of linen into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the threads be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some earlier surgeons suggested soaking the threads in clarified butter and changing them daily.  I do not agree with this; rather, the threads should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then the threads are left in place until the bone is firmly set and the cartilage solidified.  After the thread is in place, goose feather tubes wrapped in soft linen can be inserted into the nostril.  This will hold the fracture more securely, and prevent the patient from having difficulty breathing.
This is not a necessary procedure.  You can either use the threads or do without them. If during the procedure a hot swelling appears on the nose, apply a compress of fine linen or cotton dipped in vinegar, rose oil, or some kind of diachylon ointment to the nose.
If no hot swelling occurs, it is appropriate to apply a compress externally made of fine semolina and crushed kandar mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it.  Do not bind the nose at all; if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it splintered, the fragments must be extracted using an appropriate instrument, then the gap sutured and treated with ointments that promote healing and suppuration.  If a wound develops inside the nose, it should be treated with threads and lead tubes. Until it heals.
Fourth chapter.
On the setting of the lower jaw if fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured, but not accompanied by an open wound, if the fracture is only external, and not fractured in two, and has not deviated inward, then its knowledge is easily obtained.
If the fracture is in the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is in the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted, and the bump of the fracture should be gently lifted from inside to outside.
With your other hand outside the bone, you should control its alignment. If the jaw fracture has splintered into two, then traction must be applied from both sides in a straight line until alignment is achieved. If it has affected the teeth, they will have grown.
Or if it has separated, tie what you hope will remain together with a gold, silver, or silk thread.  Then place a splint over the fractured jaw, then a folded cloth, and over that a large, tight bandage or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then tie it from above according to how you are able to tie it and align it so that it does not become dislocated. Tell the patient to rest and remain still, and provide him with soft food.  If you suspect a change in shape in any way, proceed to remove it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to how you are able to tie it and align it so that it does not become dislocated. Tell the patient to rest and remain still, and provide him with soft food.  If you suspect a change in shape in any way, proceed to remove it on the third day. Then, after removing the splint, correct the area with flour dust and egg white, or semolina flour, and place a soft bandage over the dressing.  As long as the bandage adheres and the bone's condition does not change, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  This fracture often strengthens within three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use the methods we have mentioned repeatedly to relieve it until it subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, if a piece or pieces of bone have become detached, carefully remove those pieces using a tool suitable for the task. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, if you have removed the pieces of bone and nothing remains, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for the wound. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of a fractured collarbone.
The collarbone is most often fractured from the front, at the acromial end.  Its fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it fractures and splinters with two fragments without splinters, which is easiest to treat;
Or the fracture may have splinters, which is more difficult to treat; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants.  One should hold the arm adjacent to the fractured collarbone, and the other should extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, and there are no protrusions or irregularities. If more traction is needed, place a ball of lint or wool under the patient's armpit.
Adjust the bone as needed, and pull and elevate the collarbone.  Press on the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot pull the collarbone outward because it has been driven inward, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a medium-sized cushion under the patient's shoulder.  The assistant should press down on the shoulder, so the deep-seated collarbone bone is elevated.  Then correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the collarbone has broken off and is moving, gently remove the splinter. If the splinter is lodged within the bone, use one of the instruments prepared for cutting it out. After placing the instrument that protects the diaphragm, which is made of wood or iron, under the collarbone, this is its image:.
It resembles a spoon, without any twists. Its width should be adjusted according to the size of the bone, and its length according to the necessary work. Let it have two ends, as you see, one wider and the other narrower.
If the wound you made when removing the bone splinter is wide and a hot swelling is present, stitch the edges of the wound together. If the opening is small or if you fear a hot swelling, cover the wound with strips and dressings according to the size of the opening.
If a hot swelling is present, soak the strips in rose-water and vinegar or a drink, and apply them.  As for the tightening of the bone, if there is no wound or opening, apply a dressing made from mill dust mixed with egg white to the bone. Place the soft splint on it. Then, if needed, place the ball under his armpit.  Then, take a very long turban, whose width is a span or thereabouts.  Then, apply a folded bandage over the splint and dressing.  Then, take a splint made of thin wood. Make its width three fingers' breadth and its length the same. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap that cloth and the splint in the part of the turban that covers the fracture site. Then, tighten the turban over the fracture, as you would wrap it. Wrap it around his neck and under his healthy and affected armpits, and return it repeatedly in each direction.  As you see, the tightness ensures a firm hold on the fracture, and this should be clear to you.
And the whole aim is that the splint not be removed from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligament loosens and you see that the splint has shifted, correct this and tighten the bandage.  Then, have the patient sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their armpit when sleeping at night.
To raise the arm from the side, so that the fractured collarbone is elevated to the level of the shoulder, or tie the arm to the neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs in the area within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason to do so. Maintain the tension and leave it until the fractured collarbone is healed and consolidated, and it strengthens and becomes more solid within twenty-eight days. It may be in some people in less time than that.
Chapter Six.
On the reduction of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its broad area; rather, its bony projections fracture.  Whenever a part of it is fractured, or it is broken in its middle, this is determined by touch.  Therefore, according to the appearance of the fracture, apply a splint.
Bring it back to its natural shape by every means possible, then apply powdered millstone, egg white, and softened clay to the area.  Place a folded cloth pad on top, then apply a wide splint made of wood.
Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder, or a little wider.  If under the splint, there is a depression in any part of the shoulder, smooth that depression with soft clay until the splint lies flat.  Then secure it from above with a long bandage.
Apply a firm bandage and secure the splint so it does not move from its place.  Examine the bandage daily; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has shifted.  The patient should lie on their healthy side.  The shoulder will be healed in twenty days.
For a day or twenty-five days.  If this period is completed, loosen the bandage and you are safe.  It is a bone that one does not fear shattering or dislocation.  If a piece of bone protrudes and is piercing the skin, cut around it and remove it. Do as described for the fractured collarbone in relieving any inflammation that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
For the setting of fractured ribs.
The chest can fracture in its middle, though this is infrequent.  Its extremities are more prone to breakage and fragmentation.  Symptoms of a fractured mid-chest include a downward slant, intense pain, and difficulty breathing and coughing.
There may be blood expulsion, and the depression of the fractured bone will be apparent, easily felt.  To set the fracture, the patient should lie on their back with a cushion between their shoulders.  Then, their sides should be pressed down, and the ribs should be brought together with the hands from both sides.  Efforts should be made to realign the fracture from all possible angles.
Maintain this until the bone returns to its proper shape. Then, apply a bandage and splint, placing a light splint of thin board, such as poplar or willow, or similar material, over the bandage. Then, carefully tie the splint to the fractured bone to prevent its shifting.  Wrap the bandage around the back in a circular fashion, repeatedly tightening it firmly.  The bandage should be checked frequently, and tightened if it loosens.
If the injury necessitates removal of the splint due to swelling, pain, or inflammation in the area, remove it immediately and address the affected area appropriately.  Then, reapply the bandage if deemed necessary. Apply a dressing and maintain pressure until healed.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures typically occur in the thick portions of the ribs adjacent to the spine and their anterior ends.  This is because they are cartilaginous, and pressure is often applied due to impact.  Palpation will reveal this, and its recognition is readily apparent.
To treat the fracture, align the fractured ends of the rib with the fingers to restore its proper shape.  Then, apply a dressing and support the fractured bone with a splint, if necessary. If the rib fracture is inwards, the patient will experience severe pain.
And the pain is like the pain of those with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the diaphragm, causing difficulty breathing, coughing, and significant blood spitting.  This is a difficult condition to treat, and many past practitioners tried various approaches for it.
Some have suggested that the patient should eat foods that produce gas and wind to inflate the abdomen and push the fractured rib outward.  We do not recommend this, lest it further inflame the condition if it has not already begun. If the inflammation has already occurred, this would only exacerbate and confirm it.
Some others suggested applying a cupping device to the area, then applying strong suction.  This is similar to a logical approach, but there is a concern that the cupping device might be pulled unintentionally to the area due to its weakness.  Others suggested covering the area with wool dipped in hot oil.
The ribs become filled in the space between them, so that the ligaments will be aligned when wrapped around the rib cage. Then the patient is treated with the treatment for pleurisy, both with diet and medicine.  If the patient's condition is severe and unbearable. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, we should cut open the area, expose the broken rib, and then place the device that protects the peritoneum (as previously described) beneath it.  Then gently cut the bone and remove it.
Then we stitch the edges of the wound, if it is large, and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If a hot swelling appears during this time, quickly apply rose-oil poultices to the area and treat the patient internally with something to relieve the swelling.
He should lie on the side where he sleeps more comfortably until he is healed.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, it rarely happens; most commonly they are injured by contusion.  Likewise, the vertebrae of the back. If this occurs to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not... Then observe. If you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he cannot move, extend, or grasp them; and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels nothing, and finds no pain in them, know that in most cases he will not recover; he is doomed.
And if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking, know that the spinal marrow has remained unharmed, and the patient will recover with treatment. If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected, and you wish to know if they will recover, too.
Then look at his legs. If you see that they are relaxed and the same has happened to them as to the hands, and then, when he lies on his back, wind and stool come out involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he cannot do so, know that he is doomed, so do not bother with treatment. If nothing like this occurs to him, the matter is less serious. Treatment for what has happened is to try to alleviate the inflamed swelling.
By placing rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, on the affected vertebrae three times a day, until the inflamed swelling subsides.  Then apply a strengthening, dry dressing to the area and bind it with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he is healed. If a splinter or something else has formed in the bone from the injury, you should cut through the skin and remove that bone fragment. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with stitches. Then treat it with healing ointments until healed. If the other coccyx bone (the tailbone) is broken, you should insert the little finger of your left hand into the rectum.
Then, using the other hand, adjust the broken bone as much as possible and achieve alignment.  Then apply a bandage and splint, if necessary, and bind it tightly.
If you feel a fractured splinter in it, cut open the area and remove it. Treat the wound with the previous methods until healed.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Fractures of the hip bones are rare. If a fracture does occur, it usually involves splintering at the ends, longitudinal splitting, and inward tilting.  The patient experiences pain, pricking, and numbness in the affected leg.
To set it, run your hand over it to determine the nature of the fracture.  If the fracture is only at the ends, adjust the ends until they resemble their normal shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach until you are able to set the fracture.  Once you have adjusted it, apply a bandage and then a splint of wood or leather, securing it firmly.
Do not fear the fracture shifting or the splint loosening.  Fill the hollows of the waist with something that will provide support for the splint until it is properly positioned.  Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side, if a hot swelling develops.
Refrain from manipulating or setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply something to soothe it, as previously described. Then, resume setting and securing it properly. If fragments or splinters of bone are visible at the ends, they should not be removed.
Do not touch it [the bone], but adjust it from the outside, as we said, and leave it secured until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it using one of two methods. One method is to take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness, like this:
And tie straps to its ends.  Then suspend it from a high point. The patient sits on a chair, and then places their fractured arm on the stick so that their armpit lies against the middle of the stick's curve.  Then hang a heavy object from above it.
Or, a servant pulls it downward. Then the physician straightens the fracture with his hands until the fracture is put back in place.  The other method is that the patient lies on their back, and their arm is suspended from their neck by a strap.
Then instruct two servants, one to hold the upper part of the fracture in their hands, and the other to hold the lower part, and each to pull in their direction. If you wish the pulling to be stronger, tie a strap under the fracture and another over it, and have each servant pull in their direction.
