If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the center of the strap should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the strap should be in that location and on the elbow itself.
Then gently straighten the fracture without force, until the fracture is correctly aligned and joined together well. Then tighten it, unless a hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling does appear, leave tightening until the seventh day. Apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil to it.
Until the swelling subsides.  Then tighten it.  The method for tightening is to place the bandage over the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth bandage over that, then bring the arm up to the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
Hold the cloth and the tightening on the upper arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and if nothing changes about the broken bone.  If you fear something may change about the broken bone, use splints.
That is, place a splint over the fracture itself, which is wider and stronger than other splints.  Let there be a space of one finger's width between each splint.  Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an extra three fingers' width on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening described in the first section.
The tightening should be stronger on the site of the fracture.
The further from the fracture, the less the tightening should be. If you see the placement of the splints and the tightening as we described when you set the limb, do so.
If you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the ligaments every three days, lest a rash or puffiness occur at the site, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening. All of this can be corrected with what we have described.
If you are confident that none of this will happen, do not loosen the bandage until many days later. The patient should lie on their back, with their hand on their stomach.  A padded armrest filled with wool, properly positioned, should be placed under the elbow.
Examine at all times, night or day, lest the fractured limb's shape change or the bandage loosen.  Correct everything with your effort.  Make the patient's diet as we previously described: First, let the food be gentle, until the bone is strengthened.
Then the diet should be thickened.  The arm and leg usually strengthen in forty days.  At that time, the bandage should be loosened, and the patient should use a bath and be treated with ointments appropriate for this.  If the fracture is severe and extensive.
Do not loosen the bandage until fifty days, or even two months.
Chapter Twelve.
In setting a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the radius. One is small, and is the one next to the thumb; the other is large, and is placed beneath the smaller one below.  A fracture may occur in the larger radius alone, or in the smaller radius alone, or in both together.
If the upper small radius is fractured, then setting it is easy, and healing is quicker. If the lower radius is fractured, the fracture is bad and healing is difficult.  Worst of all is when both bones are fractured.  If the fractured bone is the upper small radius.
Therefore, the physician, in setting it, should make the traction gentle, so as to align it. If the larger radius is fractured, the traction should be stronger. If both radii are fractured, the traction must be considerably stronger.
When setting and pulling, the hand should be positioned on a pillow, extended. The thumb of the hand should be positioned higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers. The patient should be seated upright.
Let the pillow be at his height so that the patient does not strain himself.
Then an attendant stretches the arm from below, either with their hand or a bandage, and another attendant stretches it from above.  Then the physician aligns the bone, returning it to the best possible position. If the fracture contains splinters, they are removed.
Return each splinter to its place to the best of your ability. If a splinter protrudes, piercing the skin, and you cannot hope to set it, then cut into it and remove it as described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate section is devoted to it.
Then take treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears during the initial setting, apply a cloth soaked in rose-oil and white wax ointment (qiyruṭī). Let it be of medium thickness, and apply the cloth gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the qiyrutī and apply a bandage prepared from mill dust mixed with egg white.  Then apply the splints.  The splint placed directly over the fracture itself should be somewhat wider and stronger. Know that an arm usually requires six splints.
The fracture may be in one radius or in both.  Then make your traction on the fracture site stronger and tighter.  As you move the traction upwards or downwards, gradually loosen the traction, as previously mentioned in the beginning of the chapter.
The cloths used to wrap the fracture should be soft, damp, and not overly stiff.  The thread used for tying should be fine linen, of medium thickness as described.  Inspect the limb and ligaments after several days.
If anything needs correcting, such as an irritation appearing on the limb, the limb should be bathed in warm water until the irritation subsides.  Leave the limb unbandaged overnight for rest, then resume traction.
If the traction has become too loose and the bone has shifted, or similar problems have arisen, correct it to the best of your ability.  Also, if the limb is deprived of nourishment due to excessive traction, loosen it slightly and leave it for a few days until nourishment reaches it, then tighten it again.
If the patient experiences none of the aforementioned problems, the bandage should not be removed until twenty days or thereabouts.  Then, hang the patient's arm from his neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient should avoid strenuous movements and sleep on his back.
Know that this arm fracture will be healed in thirty days, or thirty-two, or perhaps in twenty-eight days, all depending on the patient's temperament and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of fractured hand and fingers.
The metacarpals and phalanges of the fingers are rarely fractured, but often bruised.  When the hand is fractured or bruised.
The patient should sit on a stool, with a chair in front of him at the same level.
Then he places his hand on it, extended, and the attendant of the fractured bones extends it, and the physician straightens it until it is properly aligned.  Then, if there is no hot swelling, the bandage and splint should be applied, and then a brace should be placed over the area.
Wrap it in a soft cloth; if the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball-like shape from cloth, and instruct the patient to grasp it with his fractured hand, then tie it with a long cloth.
The brace should be made of soft leather, so that the skin can adjust with the bending of the entire hand, and it should be tied as needed. If the fracture is outward, then a brace should be placed above, and another below, in the hand, so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then the bandage is applied as the hand rotates, and tied between the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture is in one of the fingers, if it is the thumb, align it correctly and then tie it with the hand.  If you wish, a small, upright brace may be placed to support the fracture.
Do not move it. If the fracture is in other fingers like the middle, index, ring, or little finger, align them and bind them with the healthy adjacent finger or bind them together. That is better, or place a small upright splint on them.
As we said for the thumb, and examine after the fracture is set, and avoid the hot swelling.  Apply the appropriate remedy if anything of that sort occurs, as described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On setting a fractured thigh.
The thighbone frequently fractures and is evident to the touch because it shifts back and forth.  Setting it involves applying a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture, while the patient lies on their face.
Then each bandage's attendant stretches it toward its side, equally, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft bandage, either of wool or the like, should be applied.
At the origin of the thigh, towards the pubis, the stretch should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee so that the stretch is downwards.  Then, the physician should align the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural form.
The bone will heal well.  At that time, the dressing and bandage should be maintained, unless a hot swelling develops in the limb. If swelling occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot swelling subsides, and then return to treatment.
As for the bandage, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture twice or three times, leaving some excess. Then the leg should be wrapped until the heel is at the origin of the thigh, and a long thread should be inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread extending above on both sides.
Then, wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Next, place splints on the thigh, at the location of the fracture itself, and make one splint on the leg bone.  Then fill the space between the thigh and leg with soft cloth so that the bandage is even.
Then begin with a strong bandage, three or four turns, at the center of the fracture site.  As you move the bandage away from the fracture site, make the tension less, softer, and looser.
Then, take the ends of the thread you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie them to the overlying splints.  Then, run the ends of the thread downwards until they reach the heel of the foot, and with them tighten the other ends of the splints as well.
So that the bandage does not shift from its place, then leave the bandage on as long as there is no pain, swelling, or inflammation to the limb. If anything of this sort occurs, promptly remove it, and correct whatever has happened, as we have described repeatedly.
If there is a splintered piece of bone piercing the flesh, then it should be smoothed, if possible. Otherwise, cut open the flesh, remove the splinter, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals.  Sometimes this thigh fracture can be bandaged without including the leg.
As we mentioned regarding the upper arm and forearm, except that this kind of reduction does not cause lameness to the patient.  However, if the bone is reduced without the leg being included, the patient will always be lame.
Know that the thigh takes 50 days or a little more or a little less to heal, depending on the variation in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On fractures of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely fractures, but is often bruised.  If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a fragmentation of its parts, and this can occur with or without a wound.
Diagnosis is made through observation, and the fracture is treated by aligning the separated parts with the fingers until they come together and unite, as best as alignment, gentleness, and precision allow. Then a circular bandage or splint is applied, if needed.
And further binding is applied above that which corresponds to the injury, then all cases described for other fractures, such as inflammatory swelling, and the like, should be treated in the same manner, addressing each symptom appropriately until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On setting a fractured shin.
The shin has two bones; one is thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its setting is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg is twisted in all directions. If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg twists forward. If the thicker bone is fractured, and this occurs from the bottom, it is clear to you.
Therefore, use traction and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly, just as in other cases, but if the fracture of the shin is extensive and has many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and take care in setting it.
There is an additional procedure for the leg compared to the arm.  After aligning the splints and completing all your work, take two pieces of pinewood, similar to those used for smoothing the walls of rooms (or possibly strips of palm leaves, or something similar),
Select pieces of moderate thickness, not too thin, and of length equal to the leg from the knee downwards.  Then wrap each piece with two layers of cloth lengthwise. Place one along one side of the leg and the other along the opposite side.
Let them extend from the knee to below the foot. Then tie the two pieces in three places, at the ends and the middle.  With this restraint, the leg will not tilt right or left, and will be properly supported. A groove of wood may be used along the leg.
Place it [the groove/restraint] to prevent movement, especially in fractures accompanied by a wound.  Then examine the leg daily, carefully watching for swelling, puffiness, or any other abnormality. If any of these occur, treat them as necessary until recovery.  The leg bone will heal within thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the leg and fingers.
The heel bone is rarely fractured.  The leg bones may be fractured, and the fingers are rarely fractured, but are more commonly bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs and you see those bones overlapping, have the patient stand upright on the ground, then place your foot on the protruding bones and press them down and straighten them.
Until they return to their proper positions. Then, take the bandage and splint, placing a small, flat board with two ends under the sole of the foot.  Securely bind the foot with the cloth and other necessary materials.
After three or four days, release the bandage. You will find the bones aligned, whether fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as described.  If some of the fingers are fractured, manipulate and align them as described for the fingers of the hand.
Then, make a splint for the fractured finger, extending the length of the finger and slightly wider than it.  Place the board, as previously described, beneath the foot, and bind it securely. If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Make a splint for each finger, sized accordingly and wrapped in soft cloth. Secure the board beneath the foot, ensuring each end of the board extends beyond the sole of the foot for good support.
You should not forget to consider all the aforementioned symptoms in other fractures, and address each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the fracture of the female pubic bone and the male pubis.
When the female perineum is fractured, have her sit in a squat position, then slowly lean her back, holding her from behind.  Then, the midwife should fill the vagina with cotton until it is full and round like a ball.
Then, the woman should rock, raising her pelvis slightly, and the cotton will be expelled, so that a ball forms at the entrance of the vagina, and the bone fracture will be reduced.  Then, place a support under her back.  When she wishes to urinate, gently remove the cotton so that she can urinate.
And return it to the position it was initially placed, then return to her original support.  Do this for seven days or thereabouts and it will be healed. If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube of reed to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube forcefully to inflate the bladder within the vagina, and the fracture will be reduced. Then fill it with cotton and keep her as described until she is healed.  Likewise, if the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this method is used for its reduction.
And its application is the same as described for the hip bone.  The correct approach to these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, is apparent to you, especially if you have some skill and understand this book. Indeed, the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
Follow the correct method for reducing and fixing it.  If a man's penis is fractured, take a goose's windpipe, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage made of cloth over it and bind it. Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the reduction of bone fractures when accompanied by wounds.
If someone presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur, humerus, or similar, one should promptly perform bloodletting if the conditions for bloodletting are favorable, as we have previously described.
If the wound bleeds, you should immediately cauterize it with powdered alum, if nothing else is available.  Then proceed to set the fracture on that very day, and do not delay.
If no inflammatory swelling occurs, if inflammatory swelling does occur, postpone the reduction until the ninth day, so that the inflammatory swelling subsides.  Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for it will expose you to undesirable symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding and exposed, you should gently attempt to reduce and align it with your hands and a slight extension. If you are unable to reduce and align it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument.  This instrument is made of iron, approximately seven or eight fingers long. And its width should match the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take three or four of these, as needed for treatment of each type of fracture.  They should be round and slightly thick, so as not to bend when pressed during use.
It should have a sharp tip, a bend at its end, thicker at the top and progressively thinner towards the bottom.  This is its image:.
It is called, in Greek, *permos*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent tip should be placed on the protruding bone end and pressed once.  If the bone has returned and is somewhat aligned, then finish by aligning the fractured ends against each other.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument does not grip it properly, then the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can hold it. If the bone cannot be repositioned as described, then cut it with a similar type of saw as we have previously mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, as best you can.  Then, remove any remaining roughness or thin flakes from the bone. If, after repositioning the bone, the patient experiences severe, painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can reposition it to its natural position, do so, as you will greatly benefit the patient.  Once the bone is completely set, dip a cloth in a dark, astringent liquid, especially if it is summer, and do not place compresses or anything greasy on the wound.
So that putrefaction and decay will not occur.  Then, use splints once you have finished setting the bone.  Leave the wound uncovered, by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, sized to fit the wound.
Beware, most carefully, of tightening the wound with the fracture, as often the ignorant physicians do this, bringing their patients either death or sores or colds.  Let your tightening be gentle and relaxed, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear some bad symptoms, as we have described, and if the patient experiences troublesome pain at the affected area, it is not appropriate to apply splints. Make bandages from strong cloths in place of the splints and secure them.
If, after a day or two, you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the liquid, and then use the dressings and ointments that we are accustomed to using for wounds, such as the four-part ointment, and similar ones.  You should also loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that pus drains downwards easily.  If the wound takes many days to heal, and the pus does not stop, know that there are small fragments of bone.
Therefore, you should probe the wound with a probe.  Any fragments that have loosened, you should remove.  If any fragments are still stuck, and are irritating the limb and causing pain, make cuts to remove them in any way you can.
If the wound develops a cold, or decay, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, you should counteract each such symptom with a similar treatment as described in its section.
If a large, prominent bone like the femur or humerus, or similar large bone, is fractured and protruding from the limb, do not attempt to pull or remove it. Often death results from this.  Leave it until it decays; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place to treat it; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the suppuration that occurs after some fractures.
This suppuration often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially when it is near the joints.  It spoils the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from its normal function.
If the suppuration is fresh, use astringent medicines such as quince, frankincense, myrrh, gum arabic, acacia, and similar substances, taking some or all of them, and mixing them with an astringent liquid or egg white or vinegar.
Apply it to the suppuration with care and bind it tightly. Leave the binding on for many days, then remove it and repeat the application until the suppuration disappears, God willing, or apply a tight lead plate to it.
Lead has the property of removing all inflammation in the limbs. If the suppuration has hardened and become severe, and necessity requires its removal, cut through it from above, and cut or scrape away the excess until it is gone, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb, after being set, remains thin and not in its original condition.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either the excessive loosening and binding of the ligaments improperly, or the excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive and improper manipulation, or excessive movements at the wrong time, or insufficient and weak blood supply in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that the blood increases, using a bath, bringing joy and cheer, and similar measures.  Then apply asphalt to the limb to draw much nourishment to it, or continue to bathe it with lukewarm water. So that nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On the treatment of fractured bones that have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after the healing of a limb that has been set, the fractured bone shows crookedness or protrusion, or if it is misshapen, and the appearance of the limb is thus disfigured. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, one should not accept the claim of those who assert that the limb should be broken again from the head. Many ignorant physicians and setters have done this in our country.
This action is strongly censured, leading to great danger and, at its worst, crippling. However, if the crookedness and distortion are recent, it is necessary to apply water in which relaxing herbs such as the leaves, root, and crown of the flower, and the like, have been cooked.
And it is bandaged with relaxing dressings, such as carefully crafted diachylon, or the sap of the root of the flower is taken, mixed with chicken fat and sheikh fat, and bandaged with it, or plump figs are taken, pounded with pigeon dung, and similar remedies known as resolving inflammation.
And the distortion can be resolved through continuous gentle massage with the hands, and the limb should be moved in all directions at all times. If the crookedness is old, severe, and hardened, and the need arises to treat it with iron.
Then, it is necessary to cut it from above, release the bone connection, and cut off the excess of the distortion or bone with gentle cuts.  Care and effort should be applied in this, and then the wound should be treated with what has been previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocated joint, preventing movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing severe pain and suffering to the patient.  Therefore, if a jaw dislocates, one must promptly attempt to relocate it.
Delay this not at all, for if delayed, swelling of the area, a swelling, will develop, making relocation of the jaw difficult.  Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved, nor stretched while it is swollen, since painful spasms and suffering often occur in the patient.
However, if this occurs, the patient should be bled, then allowed to rest until the swelling subsides somewhat.  Then, the affected area should be bathed in warm water and oiled.  The jaw should then be gently relocated, and each affected area treated as described in its appropriate section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged, as was done with fractures, from the upper body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely dislocate; and their displacement occurs in one of two ways: either they slip slightly from their positions, relaxing a little; or they completely dislocate, relaxing entirely. To the chest, so that the patient's saliva drips and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stammers with speech.  But if the dislocation is slight, it will in most cases return to its place easily.
However, if the dislocation is complete, one must quickly reposition it and delay nothing.  The patient's attendant should hold the head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or he should insert both thumbs, if the jaw is dislocated on both sides.  And with the rest of his fingers outside, he should align it.  He should order the patient to relax his jaw and allow it to move in any direction, while the physician aligns the jaw and pushes it back to its place.
If repositioning is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, use warm water and ointment compresses until repositioning is easier.  Do not delay repositioning, as we said.  If they are returned to their place and the patient's mouth closes without relaxing, then place linen bandages with a resin and ointment mixture of rose-red wax on them.
Then tie gently with a loose bandage.  The patient should sleep on his back, with his head propped up between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left, nor try to chew anything.  Instead, his food should be soft and easily swallowed.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, let him eat whatever he desires.  He should use gentleness and not exert himself to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw is healed and firm.
If the jaws, when dislocated at any time, do not return to their proper position, there is often fever and constant headache, and possibly the patient's stomach may become upset, and he may vomit repeatedly. If you see this, know that he is seriously ill.
Often, one who has this condition dies within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On relocating the collarbone and shoulder joint.
The collarbone does not usually dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate outwards, which is discernible through palpation. To realign it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then, press firmly on the area with your hand; it will return to place.  After this, apply bandages and splints, securing them tightly.  The shoulder end, connected to the shoulder blade, rarely dislocates. If it does, it should be repositioned as described above, to the extent possible.
Then apply bandages, splints, and dressings, and instruct the patient to rest and remain still until healed.  Using the same treatment, the shoulder joint is repositioned if it, too, dislocates.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On repositioning the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is downward toward the armpit, the second toward the chest, and rarely upward toward the top of the shoulder.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade or in front of the nerve. It most often dislocates downward toward the armpit, especially in those with thin flesh, as it moves in and out quickly in them.
However, in those with thick flesh, it is the opposite; it moves out and in with difficulty. Sometimes, a person might experience a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and become hot.  One might suspect a dislocation, but it is necessary to examine this to ascertain the truth.
Then, in treatment, if the dislocation is downward towards the armpit, you will find an obvious difference between the dislocated shoulder and the healthy one.  You will find a protrusion at the head of the dislocated shoulder.  Under the armpit, upon palpation, the head of the dislocated shoulder will feel like an egg.
The injured person cannot raise their hand to their ear, nor move it in any way.  Similarly, if it dislocated toward the chest or upward, this will be evident upon palpation.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if the patient is young or the condition is recent.  To reduce it, have the attendant raise the patient's arm upward, then place your thumbs under their armpit and lift the joint firmly upward into place, while the attendant raises and extends the arm.
Then, gently lower it downward, and it will return quickly.  If it does not return with these methods, and the dislocation has been present for many days, the patient should bathe in warm water and use poultices that soften and relax tissues, such as cooked roots of the plant _line_, fenugreek, and King's crown in water.
Then, the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under their armpit.  The physician places their heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand, extending it downward.
The physician pushes the ball with their foot, and it will return to its proper place.  Alternatively, if you wish to reduce it in another way, have a person taller than the patient stand to the side, placing their shoulder under the patient's armpit and lifting it upward so that the patient hangs suspended in the air. And another attendant pulls the patient's hand down toward their stomach. If the patient is light, something else should be suspended from the hand to weigh it down, and the dislocation will return immediately.  There are also other methods of reduction.
Another method is to place a long, round wooden rod in the ground, similar to a mortar pestle but neither thick nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on the top of the rod, and the patient, standing along the rod, has the rod placed under their armpit.
Then, the patient extends their hand downward on the other side, and also extends their body forcefully on the other side. The joint will quickly return to its place. If reduction proves difficult with all the previously mentioned methods, then use this treatment: take a wooden rod about two cubits in length. Its width should be about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a rounded head, to easily enter the armpit in this manner.
Then a soft cloth is tied to the rounded head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.  The rod is placed under the armpit, and the entire hand and arm are extended downwards along the rod.  The rod is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a support beam.  The hand is extended downward, and the rest of the body is left hanging on the other side. The joint will immediately enter its place. If it enters in any way possible, a moderate-sized ball of wool should then be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared dressing, made of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white, is applied over the entire shoulder area, rotating upwards.  The ball is tightly tied under the armpit. The bandage is tied over the dressing from above, and the hand is suspended from the neck.
He must not move his hand for seven days. The patient's food should be kept light so that the limb strengthens more quickly, and heals faster. Then, after seven or five days, he is allowed to exercise, and if the limb remains firm and does not relax, then he is healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three cauterizing materials should be used, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done, and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint does not remain stable,
If the bandage and tightening are repeated many times, and the joint still does not stabilize and falls loose, and the patient cannot raise the arm, know then that the nerve in the top of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or relaxed.  Know then that the joint will never be stable in its position.
As for the dislocation of the jaw, which is near the chest and breast, and behind, it is to be repositioned by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  All other tightening and treatment should be used until it is healed. If, after healing, the limb shows sluggishness or delayed movement upon examination,
The patient should use hot baths frequently until that sluggishness loosens and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
In the treatment of the dislocated elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, particularly forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is visible and palpable, regardless of the specific direction of dislocation.
If you align the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, this will be clearly evident from the concave appearance of the joint. The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be brought into contact with it.  You should act promptly and relocate the dislocation at once, before a hot swelling develops.
If a hot swelling develops, relocation becomes difficult and possibly the injury will never heal.  Especially if the dislocation is backward, it is the worst and most painful of all dislocations, and often results in death.
If it is possible to reduce the dislocation, the attendant should extend his arm fully with both hands, while the physician's hands are placed above and below the elbow. The physician pushes the joint back into place with all his thumbs or the base of his hands.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once so that the base of the hand strikes the shoulder, which is adjacent. If the dislocation does not return, use very strong, forceful extension, wherein two attendants extend the arm.
And the patient is held by two attendants as well, so that it does not move during the extension. Then the arm is turned in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around the hand. If the physician works on the joint with his hands, he should apply ointment to the joint to facilitate easy dislocation.
Then the joint is pushed forcefully until it returns. After its return, a bandage with a compress and a drying agent, along with egg white, should be applied firmly.  The arm should be suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is loosened. If the joint is firmly fixed in its place, loosen the bandage and leave it. If you see that the joint is not sufficiently strengthened, reapply the bandage and bandage and leave it for several days until it strengthens, then loosen it. If any swelling occurs after the joint has returned and been loosened.
If movement is difficult, use moist heat in a bath, gentle massage, and kneading until it softens. Or, place a fatty ram's horn on the joint, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and a night. Then remove it and place him in a bath; when he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then apply the horn a second and a third time with baths until it softens.  If you prefer, apply damp, finely ground cow dung with ghee, and bind it tightly. Repeat this multiple times; it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, but it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, and have an assistant extend the arm. The physician should place his hand on the prominent part of the joint and push until it returns. However, one must look to see if the dislocation has moved to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the outside of their hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is prominent on the outside of the hand, then the inside of the hand should be placed on the board so that the physician's hand is positioned over the same bony prominence. If it returns at that time, fine. Otherwise, bind it with a dressing that relieves inflammation.
Leave it be and do not repeat attempts, as it cannot be borne, nor can it be reduced after days have passed, other than the joint remaining crooked and causing the patient no harm, unless the hand becomes slack and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve has been severed or crushed, and there is no remedy except to bind it with cautery.  Sometimes this helps, sometimes it does not. After the wrist is reduced, apply the dressing we have described, then bind tightly and leave for five days.
Then it is released, and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult and some hardness appears, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction. If a finger dislocates towards the palm's surface or the back of the hand, straighten the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to its place, then bandage the finger's head and suspend it toward the direction it dislocated to.
Leave it for two days, then release it and straighten it until it stands upright that day.  If it is night, bandage it in the same way.  Continue to disband it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bandage it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Likewise, if it dislocates toward the back of the hand, bandage it towards the same direction and treat it in the same manner as before until it heals.  Do the same if it dislocates in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating the dislocation of the spine.
When a vertebra of the spine or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, because death quickly comes to the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool is expelled involuntarily; he cannot hold it.  Often, some of his limbs—either his legs, or his arms, or one of them—become relaxed.  However, if only one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often dislocates further.
Its displacement can occur in four directions. That which dislocates to the rear is called a hump.  There is no treatment for a hump that has occurred due to childhood illnesses.  As for a hump that has developed from a fall, a blow, or similar events...
The ancients described various treatments with lengthy explanations, most of which are of little benefit.  I have summarized what is useful, shortening the extensive material they presented, clarifying the meaning, and providing a depiction of the instrument unlike what they described and explained.
I say that there is no remedy or cure for a hump that occurs in the front of the chest, nor for those that occur to the sides.  However, the one that occurs in the back, especially the kind I am describing, is treated by laying the patient on his face.
On a level platform near a wall, spread a moist mat beneath him to avoid injuring his chest. Then, place a vertical plank, planted in a hole in the ground, near his head at one end of the platform, and another plank near his feet at the other end of the platform.
A servant holds one plank, which is not secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other plank in the same manner.  Then, a soft, strong bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under his armpits. The end of the bandage is extended to the plank near his head and tied to it.
Then, another bandage is tightened above his hips, above his knees, and at his heels.  Then, all the bandages are gathered and tied to the other plank near his feet.  Then, each servant pulls the plank towards their side with the bandage, and the planks remain in their embedded positions.
However, they are not secured, as we said. The physician places his hands on the hump with force to cause it to straighten, or he places a board on it and leans on the board with his legs until it straightens. If it does not straighten with this treatment, take a board about three cubits long.
A space is dug in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, to accommodate the end of the board.  The middle of the board is placed on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls firmly to press on the hump.
It returns to its place. If you wish to do it with a hand-spun device, you should dig into the ground near the head of the patient, at the end of the room, two planks each a cubit long, and at his feet at the end of the room as well, two planks of the same length.
Let there be a span between each plank.  A hole is made in each plank through which the winding device runs.  All the planks are firmly secured to the ground so as not to move at all.  A twisted stick, which is the winding device, is inserted, using the ropes tied in the holes of the planks.
At its end is a hole secured with a stick a span long, to wind around.  The other two planks have similar attachments.  Then, the ropes tied around the patient's chest are pulled tight on the winding device near his head, and the ropes tied around his legs are pulled tight on the winding device near his feet.
Then, a servant stands by each winding device, twisting the winding device with their hand. The physician straightens the hump as we previously described. This is a depiction of the winding device, the room, and the patient.
Then, after the spine is returned and the area is aligned, the dried dressing should be applied, with the egg white, then the linen.  Then place a splint made from a board, whose width is about three fingers, or similar, and its length should cover the area of the hump.
And on top of some sound beads. Then tie with the necessary rope.  The patient should use mild food until healed. If some lump remains in the area after healing, then the softening and relaxing medicines should be used, along with the splint we described, for a long time.
A lead plate may also be used.  A lump may appear at the end of the spinal beads, leading to a mistaken diagnosis of dislocation.  This may be an extra bone protruding; do not treat it with this method, as death might result.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and subluxation that occurs in other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards.  It dislocates inwards most frequently, and rarely forwards or backwards.  A sign of dislocation inwards is that if you place the patient's healthy leg alongside the affected one, it will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will be unable to bend their leg at the groin.  The area adjacent to the groin will be swollen and noticeably prominent, indicating that the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outwards are the opposite of these signs.  The sign of dislocation forwards is that the patient can fully extend their leg, but cannot bend it without pain, located in the knee. If they attempt to walk, they will not be able to do so forwards.
The patient will retain urine and their groin will be painful. When walking, their gait will be on their heels.  The sign of dislocation backwards is that the patient cannot straighten their knee or bend it before bending the groin, and their leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly visible near the hip area.  As for the reduction of these dislocations, observe if the dislocation has been long-standing and the patient has not attempted reduction, remaining in its condition.  If so, there is absolutely no cure.
Therefore, it should not be exposed to this.  As for a recent dislocation, of any of the four types, immediately manipulate the joint by extending it inward and outward, and move it to the right and left.  Perhaps...
If it returns without further treatment, then if it does not return thus, a strong assistant should be positioned to pull the leg from below, either with their hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant pulls from above.
By inserting their hands under the armpits, then pulling with a soft bandage on the root of the thigh, another assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the pull is made either forward from the groin to the collarbone, or backward towards the back.
All of them should pull at once, so that the patient is lifted from the ground and suspended.  This type of pulling is common to all four types.  If the dislocation returns in this way, otherwise, each type requires a specific treatment as described.
As for its specific return if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their sound side, then a bandage is placed on the root of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then, the bandage is pulled upward from the groin towards the upper body, toward the collarbone.  A strong assistant then grasps the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and pulls outward with great force, and it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other methods of returning this limb. If you are unsuccessful and this type of treatment is not effective, the patient's legs should be firmly bandaged with a soft bandage around both ankles and knees.
Each bandage should be about four fingers' width apart.  The affected leg should be extended further than the other by about two fingers' width.  Then, the patient is suspended from the head by a wooden beam within the house, with the beam a distance of two arms' length from the ground.
Then, a strong boy is instructed to grasp the head of the thigh, and another boy holds the patient, while the boy holding the thigh pushes with force.  The joint will quickly return to its proper place.  As for its specific return if the dislocation is outward...
The patient should lie on a raised platform, as described for the humpback patient.  A bandage is tightened around the affected leg and chest.  Then, one plank is placed at the patient's feet, another at their head, and a further plank is placed firmly in the middle of the platform.
Damp cloths are wrapped around the planks to prevent the patient from being harmed. The plank should be placed between the patient's thighs to prevent downward displacement during the stretching. Then, each attendant pulls in their direction, and the physician aligns the hip with his hands.  If it returns to its place...
Otherwise, place the board on him, pressing it as described for the humpback, except that the patient must lie on his healthy side.  If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg must be stretched in this same manner on the platform.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand.  The pressure should extend downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be stretched downwards.
He should be raised off the ground, but placed on a firm surface, and the hip should be positioned outward, as described when lying on the platform facing down, with the bandages tightened.
As we previously stated, the board should also be used to press the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint has returned correctly, a sign that it has returned is evident, and you will know by stretching the patient's legs.
If you see that the legs are even, and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned properly.  Then, bring the thighs together, apply the dressing, and secure it with a bandage; ensure the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain at rest for three or four days.  Then, release the bandage and dressing, and compare the legs. If you find them equal in size, know that the joint has set. Allow the patient to walk.  If you notice any looseness,
Return and re-dress and secure it as you did originally. Leave it for three more days, and then release it.  Let the patient walk slowly over several days until the joint is strengthened, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of the knee joint.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It does not dislocate forward.  A sign of dislocation is to instruct the patient to pull the leg towards the thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated.  Treatment for all types of dislocation is for the patient to sit, extending their legs if able.  A servant sits behind him, holding his middle and gently bending it backward. Then, you sit on the patient's thighs.
Place your back against his face, put his leg between your legs, then grasp his knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers on his knee.  Firmly press both sides of his knee with your hands, while another servant extends the leg until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh, gently, not forcibly. Then bandage it, attaching the leg to the thigh, and bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them.  Only light walking should be allowed for a few days, until strength returns.
If you cannot return it by the method described, then use forceful extension with the bandages previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be slightly displaced or completely dislocated.  Dislocation may occur inward or outward. A sign of dislocation is that the heel will appear swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
As for treating a simple displacement, it is easy to return it by gently pulling and aligning it until it returns.  If it is completely dislocated, the patient should be seated, and a strong assistant should hold him firmly behind the middle of his back.
Then you, with your right hand, grasp the foot from above and with your left hand from below, at the ankle.  Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand, twice.  Then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, without force.
Repeat this procedure as described, twice.  Then, on the third attempt, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the heel. If it returns in one or two repetitions of this procedure, and you find the foot aligned, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the procedure, as it will return.
If it does not return with the methods described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground, and insert a firmly secured wooden stake into the ground, positioned between his thighs.  A cloth has been wrapped around it to prevent injury to the patient, and the assistant secures the patient's thigh.
Then another assistant extends the leg, either by hand or with a strap tied around the patient's neck.  Each assistant pulls in the opposite direction, with the stake held firmly between the patient's thighs to prevent the body from pulling downwards during the extension.  Then the physician aligns the joint with his hands.
Another assistant holds the healthy leg downwards.  If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splints, and secure them tightly with straps.  Tie the foot to the splints downwards.  Care must be taken to avoid the nerve located behind the heel.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes him pain.  Leave it on for two or three days.  If the bandage loosens, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day.  The patient should avoid walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and become permanently damaged, and treatment will not be effective thereafter.  If a hot swelling appears, then the treatments for its reduction previously described in other parts of the book of medicine and healing should be employed until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of the joint of the toes.
Any dislocated parts should be gently straightened with a slight pull, without force. This is not difficult but rather easy. If the dislocation is in one of the joints of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or on a board while standing, like when walking. Then, place your foot on the protruding joint, and press down firmly on it with your foot until it returns to its proper place and no protuberance is visible.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, covering the entire foot, with two ends.  Secure it tightly for three days.  Then loosen it and keep the patient from walking for several days until it strengthens, and a recurrence is avoided, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation that occur with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both.
When something like this happens and you attempt to treat and set it, death frequently follows.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless one is skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, and not rash or reckless.  Initially, one should use only medications that alleviate the acute inflammation, and leave the patient alone.
By God's will, except for what you hope will be safe from harm with the ease of the illness, and some hope appears to you in it, then return it to its original state immediately before the acute inflammation occurs.
If the limb returns to its desired state, use the treatment that alleviates inflammation and treat the wound with suitable desiccated ointments. If the dislocation is with a fracture and splinters have separated in the bone, then remove them. Follow in this what we have previously mentioned about simple illnesses in their respective places.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of risk, as previously instructed and advised. This will preserve your reputation and safeguard your standing, God willing.
